The Five Pillars and the First Principle
Ghazali cites the hadith of the five pillars and explains the first pillar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational hadith on the five pillars of Islam and the essential components of the first pillar, the shahada, as explained by Imam Ghazali.

Core Idea
Islam is built on five pillars, and the first requires both heart belief and tongue utterance.

In this lesson, we begin the core of Ghazali's treatise by examining the Prophetic hadith that establishes the five pillars of Islam. Ghazali emphasizes that this hadith is a landmark of truth, and he particularly focuses on the first pillar, the two testimonies, which must be realized in both the heart and the tongue. Understanding this principle is essential for everything that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the Hadith and Its Versions
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the two versions of the hadith from Bukhari and Muslim. Compare the slight differences in wording.
Both versions are authentic and reinforce the same pillars.
2
Identify the Five Pillars
From the hadith, list the five pillars mentioned: shahada, prayer, zakat, pilgrimage, and fasting. Recognize that the shahada is the first and foundational pillar.
3
Focus on Ghazali’s Explanation of the First Pillar
Notice that Ghazali singles out the shahada for special explanation. He states it requires both 'i'tiqad bil-qalb' (belief in the heart) and 'nutq bil-lisan' (utterance by the tongue). This is the key takeaway.
This corrects the misconception that only verbal declaration suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن من معالم الحق ، وصدق اليقين ، أن الإسلام بنى على خمس ، كما أخبرنا سيدنا رسول الله صلوات الله وسلامه عليه ، فيما أخرجه الإمام البخاري ، عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : بني الإسلام على خمس : شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله ، وأن محمدا رسول الله وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة ، والحج ، وصوم رمضان . وفيما أخرجه الامام مسلم في صحيحه عن عبد الله بن عمر رضي الله عنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : بني الإسلام على خمس : شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله ، وأن محمدا عبده ورسوله وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة ، وحج البيت ، وصوم رمضان . وعلى ضؤ هذا الهدى النبوي الكريم تتضح معالم الحقيقة ، في شرح هذه القاعدة الجليلة الأولى ، وهى الشهادتان التي لا بد فيها من اعتقاد بالقلب ، ونطق باللسان .
For among the landmarks of truth and the certainty of conviction is that Islam is built upon five, as our master the Messenger of Allah informed us, in what Imam al-Bukhari narrated from Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Islam is built upon five: the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, establishing prayer, giving zakat, pilgrimage, and fasting Ramadan.' And in what Imam Muslim narrated in his Sahih from Abdullah ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Islam is built upon five: the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is His servant and Messenger, establishing prayer, giving zakat, pilgrimage to the House, and fasting Ramadan.' And in the light of this noble prophetic guidance, the landmarks of truth become clear in the explanation of this first great principle, which is the two testimonies, which necessarily require belief in the heart and utterance by the tongue.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage begins the main content by establishing a foundational proof (dalil) from the Sunnah. Ghazali states that from the landmarks of truth and certainty is that Islam is built upon five pillars, as the Prophet informed. He then cites two versions of the hadith: one from Bukhari and one from Muslim. The slight differences (e.g., 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' vs. 'Muhammad is His servant and Messenger') are both preserved, showing the reliability of the transmission. The hadith lists the five pillars: shahada (testimony), prayer, zakat, hajj, and fasting Ramadan. Ghazali then draws the conclusion: in light of this prophetic guidance, the truth becomes clear in explaining the first principle, which is the two testimonies (ash-shahadatayn). He emphasizes that this principle necessarily requires two components: عقيدة بالقلب (belief in the heart) and نطق باللسان (utterance by the tongue). Thus, the first pillar is not merely a verbal statement but must be accompanied by inward conviction. This sets the stage for the detailed discussion of beliefs that follow.

Key Terms
بني الإسلام على خمس
الشهادتان
اعتقاد بالقلب
نطق باللسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the shahada is only verbal, without inner conviction.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that the shahada necessarily requires belief in the heart and utterance by the tongue, making inner faith essential.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does the first principle (the two testimonies) necessarily require?
A) Only verbal utterance
B) Belief in the heart and utterance by the tongue
C) Only belief in the heart without utterance
D) Actions along with the testimony

Key Takeaway
The first pillar of Islam is the shahada, which must be affirmed in the heart and spoken by the tongue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say the shahada in your daily prayers or in times of ease, do you truly feel in your heart that there is no deity worthy of worship except Allah and that Muhammad is His Messenger? Or has it become a mere habit on your tongue? What will you do today to renew the sincerity of your shahada?
?
If your heart were to be exposed and people saw the level of your conviction in the shahada, would they see a firm belief that transforms your life, or a weak faith that wavers with doubts? What step can you take this week to strengthen your inner i'tiqad?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said Islam is built on five pillars. Which pillar do you neglect the most in practice, and how does that reflect the gap between your tongue's testimony and your heart's commitment? What will you change starting tomorrow?

The Five Pillars and Four Creedal Categories
Introduces the hadith on five pillars and classifies creedal details into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational role of the five pillars of Islam and the four categories of creedal details as presented by Imam al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Islam is built on five pillars; creedal details are of four types.

This lesson introduces the first principle of Islamic creed: the two testimonies. It begins with the well-known hadith of the five pillars, establishing the practical foundation of Islam. Then it presents a classification of creedal details into four categories, which will guide the subsequent discussion of beliefs. Understanding this classification helps you see how different aspects of faith are rationally organized.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith of the five pillars
Read the Arabic text of the hadith as transmitted by Imam Muslim. Notice the two versions cited and the slight difference in wording. Reflect on why the author starts with this hadith.
The hadith establishes the practical framework of Islam.
2
Understand the first principle
Identify that the first principle is the two testimonies (al-shahadatan), which require both belief in the heart and utterance by the tongue. This is the foundation of all creedal details.
3
Learn the four categories of creedal details
The author lists four types: what is believed to be obligatory (wajib), what is believed to be impossible (mustahil), what is realized in existence (such as paradise and hell), and what is certainly transmitted (prophetic reports about the unseen). Study each category carefully.
These categories cover the main types of Islamic beliefs.
4
Reflect on the classification's purpose
Consider why the author provides this classification. It organizes the topics that will be discussed in the book and shows the rational structure of creed. The list is not exhaustive but exemplary.
The author says 'many types' — these four are key examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بني الإسلام على خمس : شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله ، وأن محمدا رسول الله
وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة ، والحج ، وصوم رمضان
(۱)
وفيما أخرجه الامام مسلم في صحيحه عن عبد الله بن عمر رضي الله عنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم :
بني الإسلام على خمس : شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله ، وأن محمدا عبده ورسوله وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة ، وحج البيت ، وصوم رمضان
( (۲)
وعلى ضؤ هذا الهدى النبوي الكريم تتضح معالم الحقيقة ، في شرح هذه القاعدة الجليلة الأولى ، وهى الشهادتان التي لا بد فيها من اعتقاد بالقلب ، ونطق
باللسان
وتفاصيل هذه القاعدة ، يأتي من جهة العقيدة على أنواع كثيرة :
منها : ما يعتقد وجوبه ،
ومنها : ما يعتقد استحالته
ومنها : ما يتحقق وجوده ،
ومنها : ما يتيقن وروده ،
Islam is built upon five: the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, establishing prayer, giving zakah, Hajj, and fasting Ramadan. (1) And in what Imam Muslim transmitted in his Sahih from Abdullah ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: Islam is built upon five: the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is His servant and Messenger, establishing prayer, giving zakah, pilgrimage to the House, and fasting Ramadan. (2) And in the light of this noble prophetic guidance the features of the truth become clear, in explaining this great first principle, which is the two testimonies, in which there must be belief in the heart and utterance by the tongue. And the details of this principle, from the perspective of creed, come in many types: among them: what is believed as obligatory, and among them: what is believed as impossible, and among them: what is realized in existence, and among them: what is certainly transmitted.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage introduces the first principle ('al-qa'idah al-ula') of the book, namely the two testimonies (al-shahadatan). It begins by citing the well-known hadith of the five pillars of Islam, quoting both the version from Bukhari (implied by footnote reference to Fath al-Bari) and explicitly from Muslim. The author then states that in light of this prophetic guidance, the truth becomes clear when explaining this principle, which requires both belief in the heart and utterance by the tongue. He then provides a classification of the details that will follow from the perspective of creed ('aqidah): (1) what must be believed as obligatory (wajib), (2) what must be believed as impossible (mustahil), (3) what is realized in existence (i.e., things that are known to exist, such as paradise and hell), and (4) what is certainly transmitted (i.e., prophetic reports about unseen matters). This classification sets the foundation for the subsequent elaboration of beliefs about God.

Key Terms
الشهادتان
وجوب
استحالة
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four categories are an exhaustive list of all creedal details.
Correction: The author explicitly says 'many types' (أنواع كثيرة) and lists four as examples, not an exhaustive enumeration.

Checkpoint
How many types of creedal details does the author list for the first principle?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Islam's creedal details are rationally organized into four categories: what is obligatory, impossible, existent, and transmitted — all stemming from the foundational testimony of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you truly feel it in your heart, or has it become a mere habit on your tongue? What one change can you make today to deepen your inner conviction?
?
Of the four categories (obligatory, impossible, existent, transmitted), which one do you most neglect in your personal reflection? How can you give it the attention it deserves starting now?
?
If the Prophet's ﷺ words about the five pillars and the creedal categories are the foundation of your faith, how much of your daily life is actually built on that foundation? What is one area where your actions contradict your testimony?

Pure Tawhid as the Foundation of Creed
Concluding paragraph that pure tawhid is the foundation of correct creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the correct Islamic creed is fundamentally built upon pure monotheism (tawhid) and rejects any form of mixture or deviation.

Core Idea
The foundation of true creed is tawhid alone, pure and unalloyed.

Imam al-Ghazali concludes his discussion on creed by stating clearly what the entire structure of Islamic belief rests upon. After presenting many detailed beliefs, he sums up: all of them are rooted in pure tawhid. This short but powerful passage reminds us that the core of our faith is uncompromising monotheism. Let's read his concluding words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms used: عقيدة, توحيد, أساس.
The passage is incomplete in the original due to page cutoff, but the main idea is clear.
2
Identify the main claim
What does the passage say the correct creed rests upon? It says it rests 'first of all' on pure tawhid. Why 'first of all'? This shows priority.
3
Reflect on the phrase 'pure tawhid'
The word 'pure' (خالص) indicates no mixture. The creed does not accept any 'kind of'... (the text is cut off, but likely 'any kind of polytheism or innovation'). Why is purity important in tawhid?
This connects to the broader theme of avoiding shirk.
4
Apply to your own understanding
Consider how this principle affects your daily faith. Does your belief in Allah come before all other beliefs? Is your tawhid free from any compromises?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهذه المعاني الجمة الصافية يتضح ، أن العقيدة الأساسية الصحيحة تقوم ـ أول ما تقوم - على التوحيد الخالص لله سبحانه وتعالى ، بحيث لا يقبل أي لون من ألوان
With these abundant and pure meanings, it becomes clear that the correct foundational creed rests—first of all—on pure tawhid (monotheism) of Allah, glorified and exalted, such that it does not accept any kind of...
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This brief concluding passage draws a summary from all the preceding beliefs. It states that from these abundant and pure meanings (i.e., the detailed beliefs just enumerated), it becomes clear that the correct and foundational creed (al-ʿaqīda al-asāsiyya al-ṣaḥīḥa) is built primarily upon pure monotheism (al-tawḥīd al-khāliṣ). The creed is so pure that it does not accept any mixture or alloy—the text is cut off, but it likely says that it does not accept any color of polytheism or deviation. The passage emphasizes that tawhid is the first and fundamental principle upon which all other beliefs rest.

Key Terms
عقيدة
توحيد
أساس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the concluding paragraph identify as the foundation of the correct creed?
A) Belief in angels.
B) Pure monotheism (tawhid).
C) Acceptance of intercession.
D) Detailed knowledge of the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
The correct Islamic creed is built solely and primarily on pure tawhid, rejecting any mixture or compromise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own beliefs, do you find that pure tawhid is truly the first and only foundation, or have you allowed other influences—such as cultural practices, personal desires, or intellectual trends—to mix into your creed? Identify one area where you may be compromising purity and commit to correcting it based on this lesson.
?
This passage says the correct creed does not accept any kind of mixture. What 'mixture' might you have unknowingly added to your understanding of Allah? Is there a belief you hold that, if measured against pure tawhid, would not stand? Take a moment to list one such belief and resolve to seek knowledge to purify it.

The First Obligation: Tawheed of Worship
Ghazali proves that tawheed of worship is the first obligation using scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first and foremost duty of every Muslim is to worship Allah alone, and that this is established by the Quran and Sunnah, not by rational speculation.

Core Idea
Tawheed of worship is the first and last obligation.

Al-Ghazali marshals overwhelming scriptural evidence to prove that tawheed—specifically the worship of Allah alone—is the primary obligation upon every accountable person. He then refutes the view that rational reflection is the first duty, clarifying that the prophets' consistent message was the call to exclusive worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the series of Quranic verses
Notice how each verse commands the same thing: worship Allah alone. This establishes the universal message of all prophets.
The repetition emphasizes the importance.
2
Examine the hadith of Mu'adh
The Prophet instructed Mu'adh to first call people to the testimony of tawheed when going to the People of the Book.
This shows priority in practice.
3
Understand Al-Ghazali's conclusion
He declares that this tawheed is the first obligation, not reflection or doubt, and it is both the entry to Islam and the exit from the world.
This directly opposes some theological views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا أول أمر في القرآن الكريم .
وقال سبحانه :
وَلَقَدْ أَرْسَلْنَا نُوحاً إِلَى قَوْمِهِ فَقَالَ يَا قَوْم اعْبُدُوا اللهَ مَا لَكُمْ مِنْ إله
فهذا دعوة أول رسول بعد حدوث الشرك
وقال هود لقومه : ( اعْبُدُوا الله ما لَكُمْ مِنْ إله غيره ) )
وقال صالح لقومه : ( اعْبُدُوا اللَّهَ مَا لَكُم مِّنْ إِلَهٍ غَيْرُهُ ) (۳) .
وقال شعيب لقومه : ( اعْبُدُوا اللهَ مَا لَكُمْ مِنْ إِلَه غَيْرُهُ ) ) . )
وقال ابراهيم عليه السلام لقومه :
اني وَجْهَتُ وَجْهِيَ لِلَّذِي فَطَرَ السَّمَوَات وَالْأَرْضَ حَنِيفاً وَمَا أَنَا مِنَ
الْمُشْرِكِينَ (٥)
وقال تعالى : ( وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا مَن قَبْلِكَ مَن رَّسُولٍ إِلا نُوحِي إِلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ لا إِلَهَ إِلَّا
أنا فَاعْبُدُون ) .
وقال تعالى : ( وَسْتَلْ مَنْ أَرْسَلْنَا مِن قَبْلِكَ مِن رُسُلِنَا أَجَعَلْنَا مِن دُونِ الرَّحْمَنِ عَالِهَةً يُعْبَدُونَ (۷)
وقال سبحانه : ( وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنْسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ (۸)
وقال هرقل لأبي سفيان لما سأله عن النبي ما يقول لكم ؟
قال يقول : اعبدوا الله ولا تشركوا به شيئا ، واتركوا ما يقول آباؤكم
وقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم المعاذ رضي الله عنه :
انك تأتى قوما أهل كتاب فليكن أول ما تدعوهم اليه : شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله
وهذا التوحيد هو أول واجب على المكلف ، لا النظر ، ولا القصد إلى النظر ، ولا الشك في الله ، كما هى أقوال لمن لم يدر لما بعث الله به رسوله ، من معاني الكتاب والحكمة
فهو أول واجب ، وآخر واجب ، وأول ما يدخل به الإسلام و آخر ما يخرج به من الدنيا ، وقد أفصح القرآن الكريم ، عن هذا النوع كل الافصاح ، وبدأ فيه وأعاد ، وضرب لذلك الأمثال ، بحيث أن كل سورة في القرآن فيها الدلالة على
هذا التوحيد ، ويسمى هذا النوع :
توحيد الالهية ، لأنه مبنى على إخلاص التأله ، وهو أشد المحبة الله وحده ،
وذلك يستلزم إخلاص العبادة
وحده
وتوحيد العبادة لذلك
وتوحيد الارادة ، لأنه مبنى على إرادة وجه الله بالأعمال الصالحة
وتوحيد القصد ، لأنه مبنى على اخلاص القصد المستلزم لاخلاص العبادة الله
وتوحيد العمل ، لأنه مبنى على اخلاص العمل لله تعالى وحده
So this is the first command in the Noble Quran. And He, glorified be He, said: 'And indeed We sent Noah to his people, so he said: O my people, worship Allah; you have no god besides Him.' So this is the call of the first messenger after the occurrence of polytheism. And Hud said to his people: 'Worship Allah; you have no god besides Him.' And Salih said to his people: 'Worship Allah; you have no god besides Him.' And Shu'ayb said to his people: 'Worship Allah; you have no god besides Him.' And Ibrahim, peace be upon him, said to his people: 'Indeed I have turned my face toward He who created the heavens and the earth, inclining toward truth, and I am not of the polytheists.' And He, exalted be He, said: 'And We sent not before you any messenger except that We revealed to him that there is no god except Me, so worship Me.' And He said: 'And ask those We sent before you of Our messengers: Did We appoint besides the Most Merciful gods to be worshipped?' And He, glorified be He, said: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me.' And Heraclius said to Abu Sufyan when he asked him about the Prophet: What does he say to you? He said: He says: Worship Allah and associate nothing with Him, and leave what your fathers say. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to Mu'adh, may Allah be pleased with him: 'You will come to a people of the Book, so let the first thing you call them to be the testimony that there is no god except Allah.' And this tawheed is the first obligation upon the legally responsible person, not reflection, nor the intention to reflect, nor doubt about Allah, as are the sayings of those who have not understood what Allah sent His messenger with of the meanings of the Book and wisdom. So it is the first obligation and the last obligation, and the first thing by which one enters Islam and the last thing by which one departs from the world. And the Noble Quran has fully expressed this type, beginning and repeating it, and striking examples of it, such that every surah in the Quran contains evidence of this tawheed. And this type is called: Tawheed of Divinity (tawheed al-ilahiyyah), because it is based on sincerity of devotion (ikhlas al-ta'alluh), which is the most intense love of Allah alone, and that necessitates sincerity of worship (ikhlas al-'ibadah) to Him alone. And therefore it is called Tawheed of Worship (tawheed al-'ibadah), and Tawheed of Will (tawheed al-iradah) because it is based on desiring the face of Allah through righteous deeds, and Tawheed of Intention (tawheed al-qasd) because it is based on sincerity of intention which necessitates sincerity of worship to Allah, and Tawheed of Action (tawheed al-'amal) because it is based on sincerity of action for Allah, exalted be He, alone.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a proof from revelation that the first and foremost obligation upon every legally responsible person (mukallaf) is tawheed—specifically tawheed of worship (tawheed al-uluhiyyah). Al-Ghazali begins by citing multiple Quranic verses and a prophetic hadith to demonstrate that the call of all messengers, from Noah to Muhammad, was centered on the command to worship Allah alone and abandon all false deities. He highlights that this command is the very first command in the Quran and the essence of every messenger's message. After establishing this scriptural evidence, he explicitly refutes the view that the first duty is rational reflection (al-nazar) or intending to reflect or even doubting about Allah—opinions held by some theologians who missed the true import of revelation. Instead, tawheed is both the first and last obligation: it is the entry point into Islam and the final attachment at death. Al-Ghazali then enumerates the names of this tawheed: Tawheed of Divinity (because it is based on sincere devotion—the most intense love for Allah alone), Tawheed of Worship (since devotion necessitates exclusive worship), Tawheed of Will (because it involves directing all actions toward Allah's pleasure), Tawheed of Intention (as sincerity of purpose is required), and Tawheed of Action (since deeds must be done purely for Allah). Thus, the passage establishes the primacy of practical monotheism over speculative theology.

Key Terms
أول واجب
المكلف
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some scholars hold that the first duty is rational speculation (nazar) or doubt.
Correction: Al-Ghazali explicitly refutes this, stating that the first obligation is tawheed of worship, as proven by the Quran and Sunnah.

Checkpoint
What is the first obligation upon a legally responsible person according to Al-Ghazali?
A) Rational speculation (al-nazar)
B) Intending to speculate (al-qasd ila al-nazar)
C) Doubt about Allah (al-shakk fi Allah)
D) Tawheed, i.e., worshiping Allah alone

Key Takeaway
The first and most important duty in Islam is to worship Allah alone, as commanded by every prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your very first duty as a Muslim is not to think deeply or doubt, but to worship Allah alone? What in your life might be competing with that exclusive worship right now?
?
The Prophet ﷺ prioritized the testimony of tawheed when sending Mu'adh to the People of the Book. If you were to invite someone to Islam today, would tawheed be your starting point, or would you be tempted to begin with something else? Why?
?
This lesson ends by saying tawheed is the first and last thing in this life. When you face death, will your last words be 'There is no god but Allah'? What habits can you build today to make that your final utterance?

The Many Names of Tawheed of Worship
Ghazali enumerates the various names of tawheed of worship and explains them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different names given to tawheed of worship (tawheed al-uluhiyyah) and how each name highlights a specific aspect of sincere devotion to Allah alone.

Core Idea
Tawheed of worship is known by many names, each reflecting a dimension of sincerity.

After establishing that tawheed is the first obligation, Al-Ghazali elaborates on its nature. He explains that this tawheed is called by several names, each emphasizing a different facet: devotion, worship, will, intention, and action. All of them converge on the single reality of exclusive sincerity to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of names
Al-Ghazali gives five names for this tawheed: Tawheed of Divinity, Worship, Will, Intention, and Action.
Each name is followed by a brief rationale.
2
Understand the core concept: sincerity of devotion
He defines it as the most intense love for Allah alone, which necessitates exclusive worship.
This is the foundation.
3
Connect the names to their meanings
For example, Tawheed of Will is based on desiring Allah's face through righteous deeds.
Each name highlights a different aspect of the same reality.

وَلَقَدْ أَرْسَلْنَا نُوحاً إِلَى قَوْمِهِ فَقَالَ يَا قَوْم اعْبُدُوا اللهَ مَا لَكُمْ مِنْ إله

وقال هرقل لأبي سفيان لما سأله عن النبي ما يقول لكم ؟
قال يقول : اعبدوا الله ولا تشركوا به شيئا ، واتركوا ما يقول آباؤكم

وتوحيد القصد ، لأنه مبنى على اخلاص القصد المستلزم لاخلاص العبادة الله

So this is the first command in the Noble Quran. And He, glorified be He, said: 'And indeed We sent Noah to his people, so he said: O my people, worship Allah; you have no god besides Him.' So this is the call of the first messenger after the occurrence of polytheism. And Hud said to his people: 'Worship Allah; you have no god besides Him.' And Salih said to his people: 'Worship Allah; you have no god besides Him.' And Shu'ayb said to his people: 'Worship Allah; you have no god besides Him.' And Ibrahim, peace be upon him, said to his people: 'Indeed I have turned my face toward He who created the heavens and the earth, inclining toward truth, and I am not of the polytheists.' And He, exalted be He, said: 'And We sent not before you any messenger except that We revealed to him that there is no god except Me, so worship Me.' And He said: 'And ask those We sent before you of Our messengers: Did We appoint besides the Most Merciful gods to be worshipped?' And He, glorified be He, said: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me.' And Heraclius said to Abu Sufyan when he asked him about the Prophet: What does he say to you? He said: He says: Worship Allah and associate nothing with Him, and leave what your fathers say. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to Mu'adh, may Allah be pleased with him: 'You will come to a people of the Book, so let the first thing you call them to be the testimony that there is no god except Allah.' And this tawheed is the first obligation upon the legally responsible person, not reflection, nor the intention to reflect, nor doubt about Allah, as are the sayings of those who have not understood what Allah sent His messenger with of the meanings of the Book and wisdom. So it is the first obligation and the last obligation, and the first thing by which one enters Islam and the last thing by which one departs from the world. And the Noble Quran has fully expressed this type, beginning and repeating it, and striking examples of it, such that every surah in the Quran contains evidence of this tawheed. And this type is called: Tawheed of Divinity (tawheed al-ilahiyyah), because it is based on sincerity of devotion (ikhlas al-ta'alluh), which is the most intense love of Allah alone, and that necessitates sincerity of worship (ikhlas al-'ibadah) to Him alone. And therefore it is called Tawheed of Worship (tawheed al-'ibadah), and Tawheed of Will (tawheed al-iradah) because it is based on desiring the face of Allah through righteous deeds, and Tawheed of Intention (tawheed al-qasd) because it is based on sincerity of intention which necessitates sincerity of worship to Allah, and Tawheed of Action (tawheed al-'amal) because it is based on sincerity of action for Allah, exalted be He, alone.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a proof from revelation that the first and foremost obligation upon every legally responsible person (mukallaf) is tawheed—specifically tawheed of worship (tawheed al-uluhiyyah). Al-Ghazali begins by citing multiple Quranic verses and a prophetic hadith to demonstrate that the call of all messengers, from Noah to Muhammad, was centered on the command to worship Allah alone and abandon all false deities. He highlights that this command is the very first command in the Quran and the essence of every messenger's message. After establishing this scriptural evidence, he explicitly refutes the view that the first duty is rational reflection (al-nazar) or intending to reflect or even doubting about Allah—opinions held by some theologians who missed the true import of revelation. Instead, tawheed is both the first and last obligation: it is the entry point into Islam and the final attachment at death. Al-Ghazali then enumerates the names of this tawheed: Tawheed of Divinity (because it is based on sincere devotion—the most intense love for Allah alone), Tawheed of Worship (since devotion necessitates exclusive worship), Tawheed of Will (because it involves directing all actions toward Allah's pleasure), Tawheed of Intention (as sincerity of purpose is required), and Tawheed of Action (since deeds must be done purely for Allah). Thus, the passage establishes the primacy of practical monotheism over speculative theology.

إخلاص التأله
توحيد العبادة
توحيد الإرادة
توحيد القصد
توحيد العمل

The passage says every surah points to this tawheed. If your life were a 'surah', would it clearly point to your devotion to Allah alone? What needs to change for your life to become a consistent testimony of tawheed?

The Innovation of the Mutakallimun and Its Harm
Ghazali declares the mutakallimun's inquiries a bid‘a and gives evidence from the Salaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Ghazali considers the methods of the mutakallimun a harmful innovation, and the two proofs he offers from observation and the practice of the Salaf.

Core Idea
The Salaf's silence on kalam was not weakness but wisdom.

Imam al-Ghazali was a master of kalam himself, yet he deeply criticized its later practitioners. In this passage, he distinguishes between beneficial theological reflection and the harmful innovation of the mutakallimun. He offers two compelling proofs that this discipline was not part of the early Muslim tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own forceful tone come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali uses two distinct proofs.
2
Identify Ghazali's main claim
Find the sentence where Ghazali explicitly labels the mutakallimun's work as bid‘a (innovation). What exactly is he calling an innovation?
Look for 'فهو بدعة'.
3
Examine the two proofs
Ghazali gives two proofs. The first is based on observation and experience. The second is based on the conduct of the Salaf. Write down each proof in your own words.
The second proof includes a powerful 'law' (if it were beneficial) statement.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ghazali insists the Salaf were fully capable. Ask yourself: what does this tell us about the value of the mutakallimun's approach? How does it refute the idea that the Salaf were ignorant?
This connects to the common misunderstanding that the Salaf lacked intellectual ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعليه فما أحدثه المتكلمون من تفسير وسؤال وتوجيه إشكال، ثم اشتغالهم بحل ذلك كله، فهو بدعة، وضرره في حق عموم الخلق ظاهر، فهذا الذي ينبغي أن يتوقى، والدليل على تضرر الخلق به المشاهدة والتجربة، وما أثار من الفتن بين الخلق منذ نبغ المتكلمون، وفشا صناعة الكلام مع سلامة العصر الأول من مثل ذلك. ودليله: أنهم ما خاضوا في ذلك، ولا سلكوا مسلك المتكلمين في تقسيماتهم، وتدقيقاتهم، لا لعجز منهم عن ذلك، ولو علموا أن ذلك نافع لأطنبوا فيه، وخاضوا في تحرير الأدلة خوضا يزيد على خوضهم في مسائل الفرائض، ولكن شيئا من ذلك لم يحدث ولن يحصل. هذا بالنسبة لما أحدثه المتكلمون.
Based on this, what the mutakallimun introduced of interpretation, questioning, and directing objections, and then their occupation with resolving all of that, is an innovation (bid‘a), and its harm with respect to the general public is apparent. This is what should be guarded against. The proof that the public is harmed by it is observation and experience, and the tribulations it stirred among the people since the mutakallimun emerged and the craft of kalam spread, while the first generation was free of anything like that. And its second proof: that they (the Salaf) did not engage in that, nor did they follow the path of the mutakallimun in their divisions and fine points, not due to inability from them, and had they known that it was beneficial, they would have expanded upon it and delved into refining the proofs with a delving surpassing their delving into matters of inheritance (fara’id). But nothing of that occurred nor will it occur. This is what concerns what the mutakallimun introduced.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a condemnation of the methods of the mutakallimun. Ghazali argues that the questions, interpretations, and objections they raised, and their preoccupation with solving them, are an innovation (bid‘a). He states that their harm is evident, and it should be avoided. He provides two proofs: first, empirical observation shows the tribulations and conflicts that arose among people since the advent of the mutakallimun, whereas the early generation (Salaf) was free of such disputes. Second, the Salaf did not engage in these detailed divisions and fine points—not because they were incapable (they were highly intelligent and deeply devoted), but because they knew it was harmful. Had it been beneficial, they would have pursued it with even greater zeal than they did for inheritance laws. Since they did not, it remains an innovation that never benefited anyone and never will.

Key Terms
بدعة
المتكلمون
صناعة الكلام
الخوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Salaf avoided kalam because they lacked intellectual ability or sophistication.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that the Salaf did not engage in these methods 'not due to inability from them.' They were capable but chose not to because they knew it was harmful.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof Ghazali gives against the mutakallimun's innovations?
A) The Salaf were incapable of such reasoning
B) The Salaf did not engage in these methods despite being fully capable
C) The mutakallimun agreed it was harmful
D) The Quran explicitly forbids it

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ghazali argues that the mutakallimun's approach was a harmful innovation rejected by the Salaf, who were capable but chose silence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been in a religious discussion where the pursuit of answers to objections led to confusion or division among those present? How does Ghazali's warning about tribulations from kalam speak to the state of your heart in that moment? What would it take for you to trust Allah's revelation enough to let go of the need to explain every objection?
?
The Salaf avoided these inquiries despite their deep intelligence. Is there a topic of faith you have 'over-intellectualized' to the point that it has become a barrier to simple obedience? What step can you take this week to leave behind a theological habit that feeds doubt rather than faith?
?
If the Salaf's silence was wisdom, what does your own eagerness to debate or defend every point say about your trust in Allah and His Messenger? Are you willing to imitate their stillness, even if it feels like vulnerability? Commit to one conversation you will avoid or simplify for the sake of harmony.

Ghazali's Two Proofs Against Kalam
Ghazali presents two proofs against kalam: empirical harm and Salaf's avoidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two-fold argument that kalam has historically caused harm and that the early Salaf, despite their intellectual capability, avoided it, demonstrating its lack of benefit.

Core Idea
Kalam's harm is proven by history and by the Salaf's deliberate avoidance.

Ghazali offers a powerful critique of kalam, grounded not in speculative reasoning but in observable history and the authoritative example of the early Salaf. He presents two converging lines of evidence that the practice of kalam is not only unnecessary but actually harmful to the community. This passage invites us to reconsider the value of theological disputation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow of Ghazali's argument.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the first proof
Find where Ghazali says the proof of harm is 'observation and experience' and the trials that arose after the Mutakallimun emerged.
This is an empirical, historical argument.
3
Identify the second proof
Find where Ghazali cites the Salaf's avoidance of kalam, emphasizing they were capable but chose not to engage.
He contrasts their deep engagement in inheritance rulings with their absence from kalam.
4
Understand the implication
Reflect on why Ghazali's argument that the Salaf did not engage in kalam 'not due to inability' is crucial. It shows their avoidance was intentional, based on knowledge.
If they had seen benefit, they would have done it more than the secondary matters of inheritance.
5
Conclude with Ghazali's verdict
The final sentence says 'nothing of that occurred and will not occur' – indicating kalam is an innovation with no benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلق ظاهر ، فهذا الذي ينبغى أن يتوقى ، والدليل على تضرر الخلق به المشاهدة والتجربة ، وما أثار من الفتن بين الخلق منذ نبغ المتكلمون ، وفشا صناعة الكلام مع سلامة العصر الأول من مثل ذلك
ودليله : أنهم ما خاضوا في ذلك ، ولا سلكوا مسلك المتكلمين في تقسيماتهم ، وتدقيقاتهم ، لا لعجز منهم عن ذلك ، ولو علموا أن ذلك نافع لأطنبوا فيه ، وخاضوا في تحرير الأدلة خوضا يزيد على خوضهم في مسائل الفرائض ، ولكن شيئا من ذلك لم يحدث ولن يحصل
The harm to creation is apparent, so this is what should be guarded against. The proof of harm to creation by it is observation and experience, and what it stirred of trials among creation since the Mutakallimun emerged and the craft of Kalam spread, while the first age was safe from the like of that. And its evidence is that they did not delve into that, nor did they follow the path of the Mutakallimun in their divisions and their minutiae, not due to inability on their part, for if they had known that it was beneficial they would have elaborated upon it and delved into refining proofs with a delving exceeding their delving into matters of inheritance, but nothing of that occurred and will not occur.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a proof and argument. Ghazali first states that the harm of Kalam to creation (the community) is evident, so it is something to be cautious of. He then provides two proofs: first, empirical evidence from the fitan (trials) that arose when the Mutakallimun appeared and Kalam became widespread, contrasting it with the safety of the early period (the age of the Salaf). Second, he argues that the Salaf themselves did not engage in Kalam, not because they were incapable (they were more knowledgeable), but because they saw no benefit in it. If it were beneficial, they would have pursued it even more than they did the detailed rulings of inheritance. Their avoidance proves it is harmful or at least not required. Ghazali concludes that such Kalam never arose among them and never will (i.e., it is an innovation).

Key Terms
الكلام
الفتن
السلف
المتكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The early Salaf avoided kalam because they were intellectually weak or incapable of its arguments.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states they did not engage 'not due to inability on their part'. They were more knowledgeable and capable; their avoidance was a deliberate choice based on knowledge of its lack of benefit.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why did the early Salaf not engage in kalam despite their capability?
A) They were afraid of the rulers.
B) They were not capable of its arguments.
C) They knew it was not beneficial and avoided it.
D) They were too busy with worship.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that the historical harm caused by kalam and the intentional avoidance by the early Salaf prove that kalam is not beneficial and should be avoided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological debate that excites your intellect, do you pause to consider the historical fruit of such discussions—have they brought unity or division? What harm might you be contributing to if you engage without caution? This lesson demands you weigh your curiosity against the example of the Salaf. Will you let the lesson change your approach, or will you continue as before?
?
Imagine you are convinced that a certain theological point is true and you feel a strong urge to debate it publicly. But the Salaf, who knew more than you, remained silent on that very point. Can you bear the discomfort of leaving it unspoken? This lesson says their silence was not weakness but wisdom. What does that say about your perceived need to speak?
?
The Salaf delved deeply into inheritance laws because those rulings had clear benefit. What are you delving deeply into now? Is it bringing you closer to Allah and benefiting others, or is it feeding your ego and stirring hidden divisions? The proof from this passage is experiential—look at your own life. What trials have arisen from your involvement in debates? Will you now guard your tongue and heart?

First Four Duties: Tanzih, Tasdiq, I'tiraf, and Sukut
Ghazali enumerates the first four duties and explains each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first four duties—transcendence, affirmation, acknowledgment of incapacity, and silence—that Ghazali prescribes for common believers.

Core Idea
Common believers must affirm God's transcendence and the Prophet's truth, then acknowledge their own incapacity and remain silent.

Imam al-Ghazali presents a practical creed for laypeople, focusing on what they must do rather than what they must know. In this passage, he enumerates the first four of seven duties: tanzih (declaring God free from corporeality), tasdiq (believing the Prophet's words), i'tiraf bil-'ajz (admitting one's inability to grasp deeper meanings), and sukut (refraining from questioning or meddling). These duties protect the common believer from innovation and doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening list of seven duties
Start by reading the Arabic and translation of the introductory sentence and the list. Notice that Ghazali emphasizes this is the clear truth among people of insight.
The list sets the stage for the detailed explanations.
2
Understand tanzih (transcendence)
Tanzih means declaring Allah exalted above corporeality and all its accompaniments. This is the first duty because it clears misconceptions about God.
3
Understand tasdiq (affirmation)
Tasdiq means believing everything the Prophet said exactly as he said and intended, without addition or subtraction.
This duty affirms trust in prophetic revelation.
4
Examine i'tiraf and sukut
I'tiraf is acknowledging one's incapacity to know God's intention in ambiguous matters. Sukut means not asking about meanings or delving into them, as it leads to innovation and heresy.
These two duties cultivate humility and caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحق الصريح الذي لامراء فيه عند أهل البصائر ، أن عوام الخلق يجب عليهم في معتقدهم أمور وهي : التقديس ثم التصديق ، ثم الاعتراف بالعجز ، ثم السكوت ، ثم الكف ، ثم الإمساك ، ثم التسليم لأهل المعرفة والبصائر . أما التقديس : فالمراد به ، تنزيه الله تعالى عن الجسمية وتوابعها . وأما التصديق : فهو الايمان بما قاله سيدنا رسول الله ، وأن ما ذكره حق ، وهو فيما قاله صادق ، وأنه حق على الوجه الذي قاله وأراده . وأما الاعتراف بالعجز ، فهو أن يقر بأن معرفة مراده ليس على قدر طاقته ، وأن ذلك ليس من شأنه ، ولا من حرفته . وأما السكوت : فإنه لا يسأل عن معناه ، ولا يخوض فيه ، ويعلم أنه سؤاله عنه بدعة ، وأنه في خوضه فيه مخاطر بدينة ، وأنه يوشك أن يلحد ان خاض فيه حيث لا يشعر . وأما الامساك : فهو أن لا يتصرف في تلك الألفاظ الواردة بالتصريف والتبديل بلغة أخرى ، والزيادة فيه ، والنقصان منه ، والجمع والتفريق ، بل لا ينطق الا بذلك اللفظ فيه . وأما الكف : فعليه أن يكف باطنه عن البحث عنه والتفكر والتصرف . وأما التسليم لأهله : فعليه أن يعتقد أن ذلك وان خفى عليه لعجزه فقد لا يخفى على الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ، أو على الأنبياء ، أو على الصديقين والأولياء . هذه وظائف هامة اعتقد كافة أهل البصائر وجوبها على كل العوام وعلى من في معناهم.
The clear truth about which there is no doubt among people of insight is that the common people have obligations in their belief, which are: [1] transcendence, [2] affirmation, [3] acknowledgment of incapacity, [4] silence, [5] restraint, [6] holding back, and [7] submission to the people of knowledge and insight. As for transcendence: what is meant by it is declaring Allah exalted above corporeality and its accompaniments. As for affirmation: it is belief in what our master the Messenger of Allah said, that what he mentioned is truth, and that he is truthful in what he said, and that it is truth in the manner he said and intended. As for acknowledgment of incapacity: it is that he acknowledges that knowing His intention is beyond his capacity, and that this is not his affair nor his craft. As for silence: he does not ask about its meaning, nor does he delve into it, and he knows that asking about it is an innovation, and that in delving into it he risks grave dangers, and that he may fall into heresy if he delves into it without realizing. As for holding back: he does not manipulate those transmitted expressions by conjugating, substituting with another language, adding, subtracting, joining, or separating, but speaks only with the same wording. As for restraint: he must restrain his inner self from investigating it, reflecting, and speculating. As for submission to its people: he must believe that even if it is hidden from him due to his incapacity, it may not be hidden from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), or from the prophets, or from the truthful ones and the saints. These are important duties that all people of insight believe are obligatory upon every commoner and those like them.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of seven duties incumbent upon commoners in matters of belief. Ghazali begins by stating that the clear truth (al-haqq al-sarih) among people of insight is that common people (al-'awamm) must observe certain obligations concerning their creed (mu'taqad). He lists seven: (1) tanzih (transcendence), (2) tasdiq (affirmation), (3) i'tiraf bil-'ajz (acknowledgment of incapacity), (4) sukut (silence), (5) kaff (restraint), (6) imsak (holding back), and (7) taslim (submission) to the people of knowledge. He then explains each in order. Tanzih means declaring Allah absolutely free from corporeality and all that accompanies it. Tasdiq means believing in what the Prophet said, that his words are true and he is truthful in them, accepting them as true exactly as he stated and intended. I'tiraf bil-'ajz means acknowledging that one cannot comprehend the intended meaning (of ambiguous verses) by one's own effort. Sukut means refraining from asking about the meaning or engaging in discussion, and recognizing such questioning is an innovation (bid'ah) and fraught with danger. Imsak means not altering the transmitted expressions by changing wording, adding or deleting, but using only the original phrasing. Kaff means restraining one's inner thoughts from investigation and reflection on these matters. Taslim means accepting that what is obscure to the commoner due to his incapacity is not necessarily obscure to the Prophet, other prophets, the truthful, and the saints. Ghazali concludes that these duties are universally accepted by people of insight as obligatory for every common person.

Key Terms
التقديس
التصديق
الاعتراف بالعجز
السكوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Common believers should investigate and try to understand all theological meanings themselves.
Correction: Ghazali teaches that commoners must acknowledge their incapacity and remain silent, leaving deeper knowledge to the learned.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does 'sukut' (silence) require of the common believer?
A) To ask scholars about meanings
B) To not ask about meaning nor delve into it
C) To reflect deeply on ambiguous verses
D) To express the creed in paraphrased words

Key Takeaway
The first four duties—tanzih, tasdiq, i'tiraf, and sukut—build a foundation of reverence, trust, humility, and caution for the common believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a verse of the Quran whose meaning troubles you, do you automatically try to rationalize it yourself, or do you practice 'sukut' and trust that the Prophet's intention is true even if you don't grasp it? Which is harder for you, and why?
?
You say you believe everything the Prophet said, but if you're honest, are there any teachings of Islam that you mentally reinterpret because they conflict with your desires? How would your faith change if you truly accepted them 'in the manner he said and intended' without twisting?
?
Admitting 'I do not know' is humiliating in a world that prizes certainty. Where in your religious life are you faking knowledge or pretending to understand deeper theological issues just to appear learned? What would it cost you to confess your incapacity like Ghazali commands?

Last Three Duties: Kaff, Imsak, and Taslim
Ghazali explains the fifth, sixth, and seventh duties and concludes their importance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the last three duties—restraint, holding back, and submission—and how they complete the common believer's responsibilities.

Core Idea
The common believer must restrain inner speculation, preserve scriptural wording, and defer to the learned.

After covering the first four duties, Ghazali now explains the remaining three: kaff (restraining the mind from investigating), imsak (holding back from altering scripture), and taslim (submitting to the knowledge of prophets and saints). These duties ensure that the commoner remains within the bounds of safe belief, avoiding dangerous speculation and innovation.

Guided Path
1
Understand kaff (restraint)
Kaff means restraining the inner self from investigating, reflecting, or speculating about ambiguous matters. It is an inward duty.
This complements sukut, which is outward.
2
Understand imsak (holding back)
Imsak means not altering the transmitted expressions by changing wording, adding, subtracting, joining, or separating. The commoner must use only the exact wording.
This preserves the revealed text from distortion.
3
Understand taslim (submission)
Taslim means believing that what is obscure to the commoner due to incapacity may be known to the Prophet, prophets, truthful ones, and saints.
This fosters respect for scholars and humility.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Objection: Why Commoners Cannot Avoid Reasoning
Objection that commoners must engage in theological reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the powerful objection that if commoners are forbidden from research and reasoning, they would be ignorant of essential theological truths, thus seemingly obligating all Muslims to engage in rational theology.

Core Idea
Without reasoning, commoners would be ignorant of God's existence, unity, and attributes.

This passage presents a strong objection against the view that commoners can attain faith without theological reasoning. The objector argues that since God commands all people to know Him in specific ways, and knowledge requires evidence, everyone must engage in research—even the commoner. Otherwise, they would be left ignorant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'ولكن قد يقال' up to 'وهو بعينه نظر في علم الكلام'. Then read its translation. Notice the logical chain: command to know → knowledge requires evidence → commoner must reason.
The objection is comprehensive, covering both knowledge of God and belief in the Prophet.
2
Identify the four objects of knowledge
The objector lists four things commanded: faith in existence, transcendence, oneness, and attributes. These are demanded sciences. Recognize that each requires evidence.
These are the 'madlulat' (objects of knowledge).
3
Trace the argument to full kalam
The objector argues that understanding evidence requires knowing conditions of proofs, arranging premises, etc., leading inevitably to the entire science of kalam. This is meant to show that forbidding reasoning is impossible.
Notice the phrase 'يستجر بالضرورة شيئا فشيئا' (it inevitably leads little by little).
4
Extend to prophethood
The objection also covers belief in the Prophet, which requires evidence to distinguish true from false prophets. This again leads to full kalam. See how the objector broadens the necessity.
The objection aims to show that reasoning is unavoidable for every Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن قد يقال : إن العامي إذا منع من البحث والنظر ولم يعرف الدليل كان جاهلاً بالمدلول ، والله تبارك وتعالى أمر الناس جميعا بمعرفته، بالایمان به ، والتصديق بوجوده أولا ، وبتقديسه عن سمات الحوادث ومشابهة غيره ثانيا ، وبوحدانيته سبحانه وتعالى ثالثا ، وبصفاته من العلم والقدرة ونفوذ المشيئة وغيرها رابعا . وهذه أمور علمية مطلوبة ، وكل علم مطلوب ولا سبيل الى اقتناصه وتحصيله إلا بالادلة ، فلا بد اذن من النظر في الأدلة والتفطن لوجه دلالتها على المطلوب ، وكيفية انتاجها له ، وذلك لا يتم إلا بمعرفة شروط البراهين ، وكيفية ترتيب المقدمات ، واستنتاج النتائج ، وذلك يستجـر بالضرورة شيئا فشيئا ، إلى تمام البحث واستيفاء علم الكلام إلى آخر النظر في علم المعقولات . وليس هذا فحسب، بل إنه يجب كذلك على العامي أن يصدق الرسول في كل ما جاء به، وهو بشر كسائر الخلق ، فلابد من دليل يميزه عن من تحدى بالنبوة كاذبا ، ولا يمكن رد ذلك إلا بالنظر في معجزاته ومعرفة حقيقة المعجزة ، وشروطها إلى آخر النظر في النبوات وهو بعينه نظر في علم الكلام . والجواب عن ذلك كله : أن الواجب على الخلق ، الإيمان بهذه الأمور والإيمان عبارة عن تصديق جازم لا تردد فيه ، ولا يشعر صاحبه بجواز وقوع الخطأ فيه ، وهذا التصديق يحصل على مراتب : الاولى : وهو أقصاها ، ما يحصل بالبرهان المستقصى المستوفي بشروطه ، المحرر بأصوله ومقدماته درجة درجة ، كلمة كلمة ، حتى لا يبقى مجال احتمال وذلك هو الغاية القصوى، وربما يتفق في كل عصر واحد وممكن التباس
But it may be said: if the commoner is prevented from research and reasoning and does not know the evidence, he would be ignorant of the object of knowledge, and Allah the Blessed and Exalted commanded all people to know Him: firstly, by faith in Him and affirmation of His existence; secondly, by declaring Him transcendent above the marks of contingent things and resemblance to others; thirdly, by His oneness (He is Glorious and Exalted); fourthly, by His attributes of knowledge, power, effective will, and others. These are demanded scientific matters, and every demanded science can only be attained and acquired through evidence. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the evidence and understand the manner of its indication of the sought object, and how it yields it. That can only be done by knowing the conditions of proofs, how to arrange premises and deduce conclusions. This inevitably leads little by little to complete investigation and covering the whole science of kalam up to the end of inquiry into rational sciences. Moreover, it is also obligatory for the commoner to believe the Messenger in everything he brought, and he is a human like other creatures, so there must be evidence to distinguish him from one who falsely claims prophethood. That cannot be achieved except by examining his miracles and knowing the reality of miracles and their conditions, up to the end of inquiry into prophethood, which is exactly inquiry into the science of kalam. The answer to all that is: the obligation upon people is faith (iman) in these matters. Iman is a firm conviction with no hesitation, and the one possessing it does not feel the possibility of error in it. This conviction occurs on levels: the first, which is its highest, is what is attained by complete demonstration that fulfills its conditions, constructed with its principles and premises step by step, word by word, until no room for possibility remains. That is the ultimate goal, and perhaps there occurs in every age one [person] and possible confusion.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents an objection followed by Ghazali's answer. The objection argues that if commoners are forbidden from research and reasoning (bahth wa nazar) and do not know the evidence (dalil), they would be ignorant of the object of knowledge (madlul). Yet God commanded all people to know Him in several respects: first, faith in His existence; second, declaring Him transcendent (tanzih); third, His oneness; fourth, His attributes (knowledge, power, will, etc.). These are demanded matters of knowledge, and knowledge can only be reached through evidence. Therefore, one must examine evidence, understand how it indicates, and how proofs are constructed. This requires knowing the conditions of demonstrations (shurut al-barāhin), arranging premises, and deducing conclusions. This inevitably leads to the full science of kalam. Furthermore, the commoner must believe the Prophet, but since the Prophet is human, one needs evidence to distinguish him from false claimants. That requires investigating miracles and the science of prophethood, which is precisely inquiry in kalam. The objection thus asserts that commoners must engage in theological reasoning. Ghazali's answer: The obligation is faith (iman). Iman is a firm conviction (tasdiq jazim) with no hesitation, and the person does not feel any possibility of error. This conviction has degrees. The highest level is attained by a complete demonstration (burhan mustaqsa) that satisfies all conditions, arranged with its principles and premises step by step, until no possibility of doubt remains. That is the ultimate goal, and perhaps only one person in each age achieves it, and there may be confusion (iltibas). Ghazali implies that lower levels of conviction suffice for the commoner; he does not need full demonstrative proof.

Key Terms
البحث والنظر
الدليل
المدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what does the commoner lack if prevented from reasoning?
A) The ability to perform good deeds
B) Knowledge of the object of knowledge
C) Certainty about the afterlife
D) The need to follow a scholar

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that since God commands knowledge of Himself and His Prophet, and since knowledge requires evidence, commoners must engage in theological reasoning—otherwise they remain ignorant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on someone else's faith without personally investigating the evidence for God's existence, oneness, and attributes? What excuse will you give Allah if you remain ignorant of these essentials?
?
The objection states that 'every demanded science can only be attained through evidence.' In your daily life, do you actively seek evidence for your beliefs about God and His Prophet, or do you accept them without verification? What step will you take today to change that?
?
If you were to die tonight, could you confidently say you have fulfilled God's command to know Him through the four aspects mentioned (existence, transcendence, oneness, attributes) by personal reasoning? If not, what is one specific evidence you will study to strengthen your faith?

The Answer: Faith is Firm Conviction with Degrees
Ghazali's answer defining faith as firm conviction with degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's answer that faith (iman) is firm conviction without feeling of possible error, and that this conviction has degrees—the highest being through complete demonstration, but lower degrees suffice for the commoner.

Core Idea
True faith is firm conviction, not necessarily full demonstrative proof.

Imam al-Ghazali responds to the objection by redefining the obligation. Rather than requiring theological reasoning for everyone, he clarifies that faith is simply a firm conviction that does not admit any possibility of error. This conviction can occur at various levels, from simple belief up to the highest demonstrative proof. The commoner is not obliged to attain the highest level.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's answer
Read from 'والجواب عن ذلك كله' to the end of the passage. Focus on the definition of iman and the description of degrees.
Note that Ghazali directly addresses the objection by redefining the required state.
2
Understand the definition of iman
Iman is defined as 'firm conviction with no hesitation, and the person does not feel the possibility of error.' This is a subjective state, not an objective proof.
This is the core of the answer: it changes the criterion from having proof to being certain.
3
Identify the highest degree
The highest level is attained by complete demonstration (burhan mustaqsa) that satisfies all conditions, constructed step by step, leaving no room for possibility.
This is rare, perhaps one person per age.
4
Reflect on the implication
Since lower degrees exist and are valid, commoners can have iman without full reasoning. The objection is answered by showing that certainty, not proof, is what matters.
The final phrase 'وممكن التباس' suggests even the highest level may have some confusion.

ولكن قد يقال : إن العامي إذا منع من البحث والنظر ولم يعرف الدليل كان جاهلاً بالمدلول ، والله تبارك وتعالى أمر الناس جميعا بمعرفته، بالایمان به ، والتصديق بوجوده أولا ، وبتقديسه عن سمات الحوادث ومشابهة غيره ثانيا ، وبوحدانيته سبحانه وتعالى ثالثا ، وبصفاته من العلم والقدرة ونفوذ المشيئة وغيرها رابعا . وهذه أمور علمية مطلوبة ، وكل علم مطلوب ولا سبيل الى اقتناصه وتحصيله إلا بالادلة ، فلا بد اذن من النظر في الأدلة والتفطن لوجه دلالتها على المطلوب ، وكيفية انتاجها له ، وذلك لا يتم إلا بمعرفة شروط البراهين ، وكيفية ترتيب المقدمات ، واستنتاج النتائج ، وذلك يستجـر بالضرورة شيئا فشيئا ، إلى تمام البحث واستيفاء علم الكلام إلى آخر النظر في علم المعقولات . وليس هذا فحسب، بل إنه يجب كذلك على العامي أن يصدق الرسول في كل ما جاء به، وهو بشر كسائر الخلق ، فلابد من دليل يميزه عن من تحدى بالنبوة كاذبا ، ولا يمكن رد ذلك إلا بالنظر في معجزاته ومعرفة حقيقة المعجزة ، وشروطها إلى آخر النظر في النبوات وهو بعينه نظر في علم الكلام . والجواب عن ذلك كله : أن الواجب على الخلق ، الإيمان بهذه الأمور والإيمان عبارة عن تصديق جازم لا تردد فيه ، ولا يشعر صاحبه بجواز وقوع الخطأ فيه ، وهذا التصديق يحصل على مراتب : الاولى : وهو أقصاها ، ما يحصل بالبرهان المستقصى المستوفي بشروطه ، المحرر بأصوله ومقدماته درجة درجة ، كلمة كلمة ، حتى لا يبقى مجال احتمال وذلك هو الغاية القصوى، وربما يتفق في كل عصر واحد وممكن التباس
But it may be said: if the commoner is prevented from research and reasoning and does not know the evidence, he would be ignorant of the object of knowledge, and Allah the Blessed and Exalted commanded all people to know Him: firstly, by faith in Him and affirmation of His existence; secondly, by declaring Him transcendent above the marks of contingent things and resemblance to others; thirdly, by His oneness (He is Glorious and Exalted); fourthly, by His attributes of knowledge, power, effective will, and others. These are demanded scientific matters, and every demanded science can only be attained and acquired through evidence. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the evidence and understand the manner of its indication of the sought object, and how it yields it. That can only be done by knowing the conditions of proofs, how to arrange premises and deduce conclusions. This inevitably leads little by little to complete investigation and covering the whole science of kalam up to the end of inquiry into rational sciences. Moreover, it is also obligatory for the commoner to believe the Messenger in everything he brought, and he is a human like other creatures, so there must be evidence to distinguish him from one who falsely claims prophethood. That cannot be achieved except by examining his miracles and knowing the reality of miracles and their conditions, up to the end of inquiry into prophethood, which is exactly inquiry into the science of kalam. The answer to all that is: the obligation upon people is faith (iman) in these matters. Iman is a firm conviction with no hesitation, and the one possessing it does not feel the possibility of error in it. This conviction occurs on levels: the first, which is its highest, is what is attained by complete demonstration that fulfills its conditions, constructed with its principles and premises step by step, word by word, until no room for possibility remains. That is the ultimate goal, and perhaps there occurs in every age one [person] and possible confusion.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents an objection followed by Ghazali's answer. The objection argues that if commoners are forbidden from research and reasoning (bahth wa nazar) and do not know the evidence (dalil), they would be ignorant of the object of knowledge (madlul). Yet God commanded all people to know Him in several respects: first, faith in His existence; second, declaring Him transcendent (tanzih); third, His oneness; fourth, His attributes (knowledge, power, will, etc.). These are demanded matters of knowledge, and knowledge can only be reached through evidence. Therefore, one must examine evidence, understand how it indicates, and how proofs are constructed. This requires knowing the conditions of demonstrations (shurut al-barāhin), arranging premises, and deducing conclusions. This inevitably leads to the full science of kalam. Furthermore, the commoner must believe the Prophet, but since the Prophet is human, one needs evidence to distinguish him from false claimants. That requires investigating miracles and the science of prophethood, which is precisely inquiry in kalam. The objection thus asserts that commoners must engage in theological reasoning. Ghazali's answer: The obligation is faith (iman). Iman is a firm conviction (tasdiq jazim) with no hesitation, and the person does not feel any possibility of error. This conviction has degrees. The highest level is attained by a complete demonstration (burhan mustaqsa) that satisfies all conditions, arranged with its principles and premises step by step, until no possibility of doubt remains. That is the ultimate goal, and perhaps only one person in each age achieves it, and there may be confusion (iltibas). Ghazali implies that lower levels of conviction suffice for the commoner; he does not need full demonstrative proof.

Key Terms
إيمان
تصديق جازم
برهان
مراتب التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that true faith (iman) requires full demonstrative proof.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that iman is firm conviction without hesitation, and it has degrees. The highest level involves complete proof, but lower levels are valid. The commoner's simple certainty suffices.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the definition of iman (faith)?
A) Knowledge of the evidence
B) A firm conviction with no hesitation and no feeling of possible error
C) Acting in accordance with all commands
D) Acceptance of only the ambiguous verses

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's answer: The obligation is faith, defined as firm conviction without hesitation, and this conviction has degrees; the commoner's certainty is valid even without full demonstrative proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali defines iman as 'firm conviction with no hesitation' and says the one with it does not feel the possibility of error. When you examine your own heart, do you truly have that level of certainty about the truths of Islam? If not, what specific steps will you take today to strengthen your conviction?
?
The highest level of certainty is rare and only for the few. Are you content to remain at a lower level of faith, or do you yearn for the ultimate certainty that leaves no room for doubt? What price are you willing to pay to pursue it?
?
You have read that iman means you do not feel the possibility of error. In moments of temptation or when faced with contrary ideas, do you feel that possibility? What does that reveal about the state of your iman, and how can you address it right now?

The Objection of Needing Kalam and the Five Levels of Faith
Ghazali presents an objection and answers by enumerating five levels of tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that kalam is required for all people and his answer through five levels of tasdiq that make faith accessible to everyone.

Core Idea
Faith is firm affirmation, not just demonstrative proof; it can be achieved at many levels.

This passage presents a powerful objection: if common people cannot engage in kalam, how can they fulfill the duty to know Allah? Ghazali answers by redefining faith as firm affirmation and showing that this affirmation arises through multiple legitimate levels, not only demonstrative proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: common people need proofs to know Allah and the Messenger, implying kalam is obligatory for all. Note the specific duties mentioned.
The objection is not Ghazali's own view but a dialectical setup.
2
Define iman as Ghazali does
Ghazali defines iman as 'tasdiq jazim la taradduda fih' (firm affirmation without hesitation). This definition is key to the entire answer.
3
Explore the five levels of tasdiq
Ghazali lists five levels of affirmation. Read each one and note how they differ: from demonstrative proof to trust in authority.
The fifth level is incomplete in the text.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Ghazali introduces these levels. How does this answer the objection? What does it mean for the common person's duty?
Salvation is not restricted to the highest level.
