⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن قد يقال : إن العامي إذا منح من البحث والنظر ولم يعرف الدليل كان جاهلاً بالمدلول ، والله تبارك وتعالى أمر الناس جميعا بمعرفته، بالایمان به ، والتصديق بوجوده أولا ، وبتقديسه عن سمات الحوادث ومشابهة غيره ثانيا ، وبوحدانيته سبحانه وتعالى ثالثا ، وبصفاته من العلم والقدرة ونفوذ المشيئة وغيرها رابعا وهذه أمور علمية مطلوبة ، وكل علم مطلوب ولا سبيل الى اقتناصه وتحصيله إلا بالادلة ، فلا بد اذن من النظر في الأدلة والتفطن لوجه دلالتها على المطلوب ، وكيفية انتاجها له ، وذلك لا يتم إلا بمعرفة شروط البراهين ، وكيفية ترتيب المقدمات ، واستنتاج النتائج ، وذلك يستجـر بالضرورة شيئا فشيئا ، إلى تمام البحث واستيفاء علم الكلام إلى آخر النظر في علم المعقولات . وليس هذا فحسب، بل إنه يجب كذلك على العامي أن يصدق الرسول في كل ما جاء به، وهو بشر كسائر الخلق ، فلابد من دليل يميزه عن من تحدى بالنبوة كاذبا ، ولا يمكن رد ذلك إلا بالنظر في معجزاته ومعرفة حقيقة المعجزة ، وشروطها إلى آخر النظر في النبوات وهو بعينه نظر في علم الكلام ؟ . والجواب عن ذلك كله : أن الواجب على الخلق ، الإيمان بهذه الأمور والإيمان عبارة عن تصديق جازم لا تردد فيه ، ولا يشعر صاحبه بجواز وقوع الخطأ فيه ، وهذا التصديق يحصل على مراتب : الاولى : وهو أقصاها ، ما يحصل بالبرهان المستقصى المستوفي بشروطه ، المحرر بأصوله ومقدماته درجة درجة ، كلمة كلمة ، حتى لا يبقى مجال احتمال وذلك هو الغاية القصوى، وربما يتفق في كل عصر واحد وممكن التباس ، واثنان ممن ينتهي إلى تلك الدرجة، وقد يخلو العصر عنه ، ولو كانت النجاة مقصورة على مثل تلك المعارف، لقلّت النجاة ، وقلّ الناجون الثانية : أن يحصل بالأدلة الرسمية الكلامية المبنية على أمور مسلمة مصدق بها ، لاشتهارها بين أكابر العلماء ، وشفاعة إنكارها ، ونفرة النفوس عن إبداء المزيد فيها ، وهذا الجنس أيضا يفيد في بعض الأمور ، وفي حق الناس تصديقا جازما بحيث لا يتغير صاحبه بإمكان خلافه أصلا الثالثة : أن يحصل التصديق بالأدلة الخطابية التي جرت العادة باستعمالها في المحاورة ، والمخاطبات الجارية في العادات وذلك يفيد في حق الأكثرين تصديقا بادىء الرأى ، وسابق الفهم ، إذ لم يكن الباطل مشحونا بالتعصب ، وبرسوخ اعتقاد ، على خلاف مقتضى الدليل، ولم يكن المستمع مشغوفا بتكلف المماراة والتشكيك ، ومنهاجه بجدال المجادلين في العقائد ، وأكثر أدلة القرآن من هذا الجنس ، من الدليل الظاهر المفيد للتصديق ، والدليل المستوفي هو الذي يفيد التصديق بعد تمام الأسئلة وجوابها ، بحيث لا يبقى للسؤال مجال ، والتصديق يحصل قبل ذلك الرابعة : التصديق بوجود السماع ممن حسن فيه الاعتقادة بسبب كثرة ثناء الخلق ، فإن من حَسُنَ اعتقاده في أبيه وأستاذه ، أو حسن اعتقاده في رجل من الأفاضل المشهورين ، قد يخبر عن شيء ، فيسبق إليه اعتقاد جازم ، وتصديق بما أخبر عنه ، بحيث لا يبقى مجال لغيره في قلبه ، ومستنده حسن اعتقاده فيه وكذلك اعتقاد الصبيان في آبائهم ومعلمهم فلا غرابة يسمعون الاعتقادات ويصدقونه ويستمرون عليه من غير حاجة إلى دليل ومناقشة الخامسة : التصديق الذي يسبق إليه عند سماع الشيء مع قرائن الأحوال لا
But it may be said: If the common person is prevented from investigation and reflection and does not know the proof, he will be ignorant of the proven. And Allah, blessed and exalted, has commanded all people to know Him, to believe in Him, to affirm His existence first, to sanctify Him from the attributes of contingencies and similarity to others second, to affirm His oneness, glorified and exalted, third, and to affirm His attributes of knowledge, power, executive will, and others fourth. These are required matters of knowledge. Every required knowledge cannot be acquired and attained except through proofs. Therefore, there must be investigation of proofs, discernment of their indication of what is sought, and how they produce it. This is only accomplished by knowing the conditions of demonstrations, how to arrange premises, and deduce conclusions. This necessarily leads gradually to the completion of investigation and full treatment of the science of kalam (ʿilm al-kalām) and the final consideration of rational sciences. Not only this, but also the common person must affirm the Messenger in everything he brought, and he is a human like other creatures. So there must be a proof to distinguish him from one who falsely claims prophethood. This cannot be refuted except by considering his miracles, knowing the reality of the miracle, its conditions, and the full treatment of prophecies; this is exactly consideration in the science of kalam. The answer to all of this: that the obligation upon creation is belief in these matters. Belief (īmān) is a firm affirmation (taṣdīq) with no hesitation, and its possessor does not perceive the possibility of error in it. This affirmation occurs in levels: The first, which is its highest, is what occurs through the exhaustive, complete demonstration (burhān) fulfilling its conditions, refined in its principles and premises, step by step, word by word, until no room for possibility remains. That is the ultimate goal. It may occur in every age one person, and possibly two, who attain that level, and the age may be empty of him. If salvation were restricted to such knowledge, salvation would be rare and the saved few. The second: that it occurs through formal theological proofs (adilla rasmiyya kalāmiyya) built upon matters that are accepted and believed due to their fame among great scholars, the shame of denying them, and the souls' aversion to further elaboration. This kind also yields firm affirmation in some matters and with respect to people, such that its possessor is not changed by the possibility of its contrary at all. The third: that affirmation occurs through rhetorical proofs (adilla khiṭābiyya) customarily used in everyday conversation and address. This yields for the majority a first-glance affirmation and initial understanding, if falsehood is not charged with fanaticism and entrenched belief contrary to the proof's implication, and if the listener is not enamored with deliberate argumentation and skepticism, and his method is not that of disputants about beliefs. Most proofs of the Qur'an are of this kind: the apparent proof that yields affirmation. The complete proof is what yields affirmation after all questions and answers are completed, such that no room for questioning remains, while affirmation occurs before that. The fourth: affirmation through hearing from someone whose belief is sound due to much praise from people. For one whose belief in his father or teacher is sound, or whose belief in a man among the famous virtuous is sound, may report something, and a firm conviction and affirmation of what he reported will precede in the heart, such that no room for anything else remains in his heart, and his basis is his sound belief in him. Similarly, the belief of children in their parents and teacher is not strange: they hear beliefs and affirm them and continue upon them without need for proof or discussion. The fifth: affirmation that precedes upon hearing something with circumstantial indications (qarāʾin al-aḥwāl) without...
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. Ghazali first presents an objection from an imagined interlocutor: If a common person cannot investigate proofs, he remains ignorant, yet Allah has commanded all people to know Him, believe in His existence, sanctify Him from contingency, affirm His oneness, and know His attributes. These are required items of knowledge, and knowledge can only be attained through proofs. Thus, the common person must engage in investigation of proofs, including understanding conditions of demonstrations and arranging premises. Furthermore, the common person must also believe in the Messenger, which requires distinguishing him from false claimants through miracles, which is exactly the subject of kalam. The objection implies that kalam is obligatory for everyone. Ghazali then gives the answer: The obligation is belief (īmān), which is defined as a firm affirmation (taṣdīq) with no hesitation. This affirmation occurs in multiple levels, not only the highest demonstrative one. He enumerates five levels: 1) The highest, attained through exhaustive demonstrative proof (burhān) leaving no possibility of error, but this is rare and not required of everyone. 2) Affirmation through formal kalam proofs based on accepted premises, which yields firm conviction. 3) Affirmation through rhetorical proofs common in everyday discourse, which suffices for most people unless hindered by fanaticism. He notes that most Quranic proofs are of this type. 4) Affirmation based on trust in a reliable authority, such as a father or teacher, where the listener's good opinion leads to firm belief without proof. 5) Affirmation from hearing something accompanied by circumstantial indications, though the text breaks off here. The overall argument is that the common person can attain valid belief without engaging in full-fledged kalam; the required taṣdīq can be achieved at multiple levels.

īmān
burhān
adilla rasmiyya kalāmiyya
adilla khiṭābiyya
qarāʾin al-aḥwāl

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only the highest level of demonstrative proof constitutes valid faith.
Correction: Ghazali shows that firm affirmation (tasdiq) can be achieved through multiple levels, and the lower levels are sufficient for salvation.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the definition of iman (belief)?
A) Knowledge through demonstrative proof
B) Firm affirmation (tasdiq) with no hesitation
C) Acceptance of authority without question
D) Rational inquiry into theological matters

Key Takeaway
Faith is firm affirmation, and it can be attained through multiple paths, not only demonstrative proof, making salvation accessible to all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the five levels of tasdiq best describes the basis of your current faith? Are you content to remain there, or does this lesson compel you to seek a higher level through deeper study and reflection?
?
If your faith relies heavily on trusting parents, teachers, or scholars (level 4), have you ever honestly examined that trust against the truth claims themselves? What would it cost you to question it?
?
Al-Ghazali says the highest level is rare. Do you envy those who have it? Or does your heart secretly feel proud that you have 'arrived' intellectually? Examine yourself: do you look down on those with simpler faith?

Imam Shafi'i's Prohibition of Kalam and Its Rationale
Imam Ghazali explains the Salaf's prohibition of kalam and its contextual rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Shafi'i's prohibition of kalam was contextual, aimed at specific harmful uses, and that its underlying purpose was to protect the Sunni creed from heretical confusion.

Core Idea
The Salaf's ban on kalam is a targeted safeguard, not a blanket rejection.

Imam Ghazali presents a nuanced defense of Imam Shafi'i's and the Salaf's prohibition of kalam. He clarifies that the prohibition was not absolute but directed at those who misuse kalam or are harmed by it, and that the real motive was to preserve the authentic creed delivered by the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali starts with the fact of prohibition and then immediately qualifies it.
The passage is a single continuous argument; pay attention to the flow.
2
Identify the four categories
Find the four types of people to whom the prohibition is directed. Understand each category: the fanatic, the incapable, the corrupter, and the unnecessary philosopher.
These categories show the prohibition is not for everyone.
3
Grasp the ultimate reason
The last part of the passage reveals the real purpose: preserving the Sunni creed from the confusion of innovators. Reflect on how this connects to the broader theme of protecting orthodoxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا نهى الامام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه المسلمين، ونهى كافة أهل السلف عن الخوض في علم الكلام، والتجرد منه، لما فيه من الضرر الذي نبهنا عليه، وكانت أقوالهم في النهي عن ذلك محمولة على نهي المتعصب في الدين، أو القاصر عن تحصيل اليقين، أو القاصد إفساد عقائد المسلمين، أو الخائض فيما لا يفتقر إليه من غوامض المتفلسفين، وإلا فلا يتصور من شريف تلك الحضرات، وقوع المنع فيما هو أصل الواجبات وأساس المشروعات. والسبب الباعث لهذا كله هو: حفظ عقيدة أهل السنة والجماعة، وحراستها مما يتوقع أن يحدق بها من تشويش أهل البدع، فقد ألقى الله تعالى، إلى عباده على لسان رسوله، عقيدة هي الحق على ما فيه صلاح دينهم ودنياهم، كما نطق بمقدماته القرآن الكريم، والأخبار الصحيحة الصادقة من السنة النبوية الشريفة
And for this reason Imam al-Shafi‘i, may Allah be pleased with him, forbade the Muslims—and forbade all the Salaf—from delving into the science of kalam and from occupying themselves with it, because of the harm that we have pointed out. And their statements prohibiting it are to be interpreted as a prohibition for the fanatic in religion, or the one incapable of attaining certainty, or the one intending to corrupt the beliefs of the Muslims, or the one delving into the abstruse matters of the pseudo-philosophers that are not needed. Otherwise, it is inconceivable from those noble personages that they would forbid something that is the basis of obligations and the foundation of legislated matters. The motivating reason for all this is: to preserve the creed of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama‘ah and to guard it from what may be expected to befall it of the confusion of the people of innovation. For Allah, exalted, cast to His servants upon the tongue of His Messenger a creed that is the truth, containing what is beneficial for their religion and world, as its premises were expressed by the Noble Qur’an and the authentic, truthful reports from the noble prophetic Sunnah.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the Salaf's prohibition of kalam. Imam Ghazali first states the fact: Imam Shafi‘i and all the Salaf forbade engaging in kalam due to its harm. He then clarifies that this prohibition is not absolute; it applies to specific categories: the religious bigot, the one incapable of certainty, the one who seeks to corrupt beliefs, and the one who delves into unnecessary pseudo-philosophical complexities. It would be inconceivable for these great imams to forbid something that is the very basis of religious obligations (i.e., knowledge of Allah). The real reason for the prohibition is to protect the Sunni creed from the confusion sown by heretical innovators. The creed is already perfectly delivered through the Qur’an and authentic hadith.

Key Terms
علم الكلام
الإمام الشافعي
السلف
أهل البدع
عقيدة أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Shafi'i and the Salaf banned kalam absolutely for all Muslims.
Correction: The prohibition is contextual, aimed at those who misuse it, as Ghazali explains; it does not apply to the foundational knowledge of Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Ghazali, what is the primary reason for the Salaf's prohibition of kalam?
A) Kalam is intrinsically sinful
B) Kalam is unnecessary because the Quran and Sunnah are sufficient
C) To preserve the creed of Ahl al-Sunnah from the confusion of the people of innovation
D) Kalam leads to atheism

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of kalam by the Salaf was a protective measure for the Sunni creed, not a rejection of all theological discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four prohibited categories do you most resemble when you discuss theological matters? Are you a fanatic, incapable of certainty, intending to corrupt, or delving into unnecessary complexities? What will you change today?
?
Have you ever engaged in a theological debate that left you or others more confused about the core creed from the Qur'an and Sunnah? What will you do to ensure your study of religion strengthens your faith rather than weakens it?
?
The Salaf prohibited kalam to protect the creed. Is your pursuit of deeper knowledge drawing you closer to the authentic sources (Qur'an and authentic hadith) or pulling you into speculative philosophy? Be honest with yourself and commit to one concrete step to realign your studies.

Miracles as Proof of Prophethood
Definition of miracle and its proof for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles serve as the primary proof for the truthfulness of prophets according to Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Miracles are the definitive proof that a prophet is truthful.

This passage establishes that Allah supports prophets with extraordinary miracles that challenge others, making it obligatory for people to believe them. The commentary by Imam al-Haramayn emphasizes that miracles are the only absolute proof of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic definition of miracle and the commentary. Note the conditions: it must be extraordinary, accompanied by challenge, match the claim, and have no counter-evidence.
Pay attention to the definition of 'mu'jiza'.
2
Understand the obligation to believe
The passage states that people must believe the prophets because of these miracles. Reflect on why this is necessary for faith.
3
Consider Imam al-Haramayn's statement
He says no other proof can establish prophethood absolutely. Discuss why miracles are considered the strongest evidence.
This is a key point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأظهر صدقهم بالمعجزات الظاهرة (۱) ، فبلغوا أمره ونهيه ووعده ووعيده ، على الخلق تصديقهم فيما جاءوا به(۲) معنى
(۱) وهي الأمور الخوارق للعادات المقرونة بالتحدي والموافقة للدعوى السالمة من المعارض على يد من يدعي النبوة .
وقول إمام الحرمين : إنه لا يمكن نصب دليل على النبوة سوى المعجزة محمول على ما يصلح دليلاً على الاطلاق والعموم ، ويصلح أن يكون حجة على المنكرين .
(۲) وهذه المسألة معروفة بالتحسين والتقبيح العقليين قالت الأشاعرة : لا تحسين ولا تقبيح عقلا ، أي إن الافعال إنما توصف بالحسن والقبح من حيث تعلق خطاب الشرع بها ؛ ودليله السمعي قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَمَا كُنَّا مُعَذِّبِينَ حَتَّى نَبْعَثَ رَسُولاً ) وبه تمسك المحدث أيضاً
وأما الصوفي : فإنه يقول : الافعال كلها نسبتان نسبة التكوين ونسبة التكليف ، أما نسبة التكوين فعامة ، لأن الافعال كلها لله تعالى ، وبهذه النسبة لا توصف بحسن ولا قبح لاستواء الإيجاد، بل هي حسنة من حيث علم الفاعل ، وارادته ، وأما نسبة التكليف وهي الطلب ولا علم بالحقيقة مختصة بأفعال المكلف ، ومن المعلوم أن الطلب للشيء فرع العلم به ، إلا الله تعالى ، فلا تكليف ولا طلب إلا لله تعالى ؛ وأيضاً فإن تعلق الطلب بفعل أو ترك غيب فلا يعلم إلا بالتوفيق السمعي النبوي
فهي
فإذا الحسن والقبح لا يدرك بمجرد العقل فلا حسن ولا قبح عقلا ، وهو المطلوب
·
And He made their truthfulness evident through manifest miracles (1), so they conveyed His command, His prohibition, His promise, and His threat; it is obligatory upon the creation to believe them regarding what they brought (2). (1) They are matters that transcend customary [laws], accompanied by challenge, conforming to the claim, and free from any counter-evidence, at the hand of one who claims prophethood. And the statement of Imam al-Haramayn: 'It is not possible to establish a proof for prophethood other than the miracle' is interpreted as referring to what serves as a definitive proof in an absolute and universal sense, and which can serve as an argument against deniers. (2) This issue is known as rational estimation of good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqliyyan). The Ash'aris said: There is no rational estimation of good or evil, meaning actions are only described as good or evil in terms of the attachment of the address of the Law to them; and its evidence from revelation is His statement, Exalted: 'And We were not to punish [anyone] until We sent a messenger' – and the Hadith specialist also held to it. As for the Sufi, he says: All actions have two aspects: the aspect of creation (takwin) and the aspect of obligation (taklif). As for the aspect of creation, it is general, because all actions belong to Allah, and in this aspect they are not described as good or evil because existence is uniform; rather, they are good in terms of the knowledge and will of the Agent. As for the aspect of obligation, which is a demand – and there is no knowledge in reality that is specific to the actions of the obligated person – and it is known that the demand for something is a branch of knowledge of it; except Allah, there is no obligation or demand except from Allah. Moreover, the attachment of demand to an act or omission is unseen, so it is not known except through prophetic revelation. Then, good and evil are not perceived by mere reason; there is no rational good or evil, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It begins by stating that Allah made the truthfulness of the prophets evident through manifest miracles, and that people are obliged to believe them. The first footnote defines miracles as extraordinary events that challenge others and match the claim, and quotes Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) saying that miracles are the only proof for prophethood in an absolute sense. The second footnote introduces the issue of rational good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqli). The Ash'ari position is that actions are not inherently good or evil by reason; they are described as such only by divine law. The proof is the Quranic verse 'We were not to punish until We sent a messenger.' Then the Sufi view is presented: actions have two aspects – creation (all from Allah, thus no moral judgment) and obligation (only from Allah, requiring revelation). Since demand depends on knowledge, and unseen obligations cannot be known by reason alone, rational good and evil are impossible. The conclusion is that good and evil are not perceived by mere intellect; rather, they require revelation. The passage guards against the error of relying on unaided reason for moral judgment in matters of faith.

Key Terms
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prophethood can be proven through rational arguments alone without miracles.
Correction: The passage states that miracles are the only definitive proof for prophethood, as per Imam al-Haramayn.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn as quoted in the passage, what is the only proof that can establish prophethood in an absolute sense?
A) Rational arguments
B) Miracles
C) Consensus
D) Scripture alone

Key Takeaway
Miracles are the definitive proof of prophethood, defined by specific conditions that distinguish them from other events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the prophet's truthfulness is made evident through clear miracles, do you actually feel that the Quran is a miracle enough for you? Or do you demand more signs in your heart? What does that reveal about your iman?
?
The definition of a miracle includes being 'free from any counter-evidence'. Is there any area of your faith where you are making excuses or looking for counter-evidence to avoid submitting? How can you remove that barrier today?
?
If you were to see a miracle with your own eyes—like the splitting of the moon—would you immediately believe, or would you rationalize it away? What does that say about your readiness to accept the truth, even when it challenges your habits or opinions?

The Refutation of Rational Good and Evil
Refutation of rational estimation of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari and Sufi theologians argue that moral values cannot be known by reason alone but require divine revelation.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot determine good and evil; revelation is necessary.

This passage presents the Ash'ari and Sufi arguments against the idea that human reason can independently judge actions as good or evil. Both schools conclude that moral judgment is rooted in divine law, not rational inference. The key proof is the Quranic verse that punishment only occurs after a messenger is sent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris state that actions are not inherently good or evil; they are described as such based on the attachment of divine law. Note the Quranic proof from Surah al-Isra'.
This is a foundational principle in Ash'ari ethics.
2
Read the Sufi position
The Sufi explains that actions have two aspects: creation (belonging to Allah) and obligation (demand from Allah). In the creation aspect, no moral judgment applies; in the obligation aspect, it requires revelation because demand depends on knowledge of unseen.
Notice the distinction between takwin and taklif.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that good and evil are not perceived by mere reason, hence no rational good and evil.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

The Stages of Teaching Creed to Children
Ghazali outlines the stages of learning the creed: memorization, understanding, belief, and affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sequential method Ghazali prescribes for teaching the Islamic creed to children, from memorization to conviction, and the role of divine grace in initial faith.

Core Idea
Faith begins with memorization, unfolds with understanding, and is confirmed by divine grace.

Ghazali provides a practical pedagogy for transmitting the creed to the young. He emphasizes that the process is gradual: first the child memorizes the words, then the meaning unfolds naturally over time, and finally conviction and certainty arise—often without the need for rational proof. This lesson explores that method and its theological basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of stages
Read the passage carefully and list the stages Ghazali mentions: memorization (ḥifẓ), understanding (fahm), conviction (iʿtiqād), certainty (īqān), and affirmation (taṣdīq). Notice the order and the phrase 'little by little'.
The stages are hierarchical; each builds on the previous.
2
Understand the role of divine grace
Ghazali states that belief occurs in the child without proof (burhān) due to Allah's grace. Reflect on why this is significant for early religious education.
This contrasts with the later need for rational arguments in mature faith.
3
Connect pedagogy and theology
Consider how the method combines memorization (a practical step) with divine favor (a theological principle). How does this shape the approach to teaching children?
The method respects the child's cognitive development while relying on divine assistance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلم أن ما ذكرناه في ترجمة العقيدة ينبغي أن يقدم الى الصبي في أول نشوه ليحفظه حفظا ثم لا يزال ينكشف له معناه في كبره شيئا فشيئا فابتداؤه الحفظ، ثم الفهم، ثم الإعتقاد والإيقان والتصديق به، وذلك مما يحصل في الصبي بغير برهان؛ فمن فضل الله سبحانه على قلب الإنسان أن شرحه في أول نشوة للإيمان من غير حاجة إلى حجة وبرهان، وكيف ينكر ذلك
Know that what we have mentioned in the translation of the creed should be presented to the boy in the beginning of his upbringing so that he memorizes it by heart. Then its meaning will gradually unfold to him as he grows older, little by little. So its beginning is memorization, then understanding, then conviction, certainty, and affirmation of it. And that is something that occurs in the boy without proof; for it is from the grace of Allah, exalted is He, upon the human heart that He opened it in the first stage of faith without need for evidence and proof. And how can that be denied?
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a methodological instruction about teaching the creed (aqidah) to children. Ghazali begins by stating that the creed should be presented to the child at the very start of his upbringing. The first step is memorization (hifz) – the child should commit the words to memory securely. Then, as he grows older, the meaning (ma'na) of those words should gradually unfold to him, step by step. The sequence is: first memorization, then understanding (fahm), then conviction (i'tiqad), certainty (iqan), and affirmation (tasdiq) of it. He notes that belief and affirmation often occur in the child without the need for rational proof (burhan); this is from Allah's grace, who opens the heart to faith initially without requiring evidence. Ghazali then rhetorically asks how anyone could deny this, implying that the innate disposition (fitra) and divine favor are sufficient for the initial acceptance of faith. The passage emphasizes that the process is gradual and hierarchical, with understanding preceding conviction and affirmation.

فهم
اعتقاد
إيقان
تصديق
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that faith requires full rational proof from the start, even for children.
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes that initial belief can be based on divine grace and memorization, with understanding developing later. Rational proof is not necessary at the early stage.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the first stage in teaching the creed to a child?
A) Understanding the meaning
B) Conviction and certainty
C) Memorization
D) Rational proof

Key Takeaway
Ghazali’s method teaches that the creed is first memorized, then gradually understood, leading to conviction—a process supported by divine grace rather than early rational proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When teaching someone about Islam, do you demand they understand everything before they believe? How can you follow Ghazali's example of gradual unfolding from memorization to conviction?

The Sequence of Teaching the Creed
Imam Ghazali states the proper educational sequence for teaching the creed to a child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper sequence for teaching the Islamic creed to a child, starting with memorization and progressing to understanding and belief, and the role of divine grace in initial faith.

Core Idea
Faith begins with memorization, unfolds through understanding, and is rooted in Allah's grace — no proof needed at first.

Imam Ghazali presents a profound pedagogical insight: the creed should be taught to children first through memorization, then gradually understood as they grow. This lesson explores the sequence and the theological basis for why initial belief does not require proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the sequence of steps
The passage lists memorization, understanding, belief, certainty, and affirmation. Find each term and notice how they build on each other.
3
Examine the claim about proof
Notice that Imam Ghazali says belief occurs in the child without proof (bighayri burhan). Ask yourself why this is significant.
4
Reflect on divine grace
The passage ends by attributing this initial faith to Allah's grace. Consider how this connects to the sequence.

أعلم أن ما ذكرناه في ترجمة العقيدة ينبغي أن يقدم الى الصبي في أول نشوه ليحفظه حفظا ثم لا يزال ينكشف له معناه في كبره شيئا فشيئا فابتداؤه الحفظ ثم الفهم ثم الإعتقاد والإيقان والتصديق به وذلك مما يحصل في الصبي بغير برهان فمن فضل الله سبحانه على قلب الإنسان أن شرحه في أول نشوة للإيمان من غير حاجة إلى حجة وبرهان

How to Strengthen Faith: Beyond Kalam
Imam Ghazali on strengthening belief without kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Ghazali's method for strengthening the faith of the common person without relying on dialectical theology.

Core Idea
Belief strengthened by Quran and worship, not by kalam.

Imam Ghazali addresses a critical question: how does a simple believer attain firm faith? He acknowledges that initial belief through imitation is fragile, but the remedy is not academic theology. Instead, he prescribes engaging with the Qur'an, hadith, and worship. This lesson explores his practical path to spiritual certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let Ghazali's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the rhetorical question opening the passage.
2
Identify the weakness of taqlid belief
Ghazali states that belief based solely on imitation is initially weak and susceptible to being overturned. Find the sentence that describes this vulnerability.
3
Understand the prescribed strengthening method
Ghazali explicitly rejects learning kalam as the method. Instead, he recommends three activities: Quran recitation and interpretation, hadith study, and acts of worship. Consider how each contributes to firmness.
The phrase 'لا بل' (but rather) signals the correction.

وكيف ينكر ذلك وجميع عقائد العوام مباديها التلقين المجرد والتقليد المحض نعم يكون الإعتقاد الحاصل بمجرد التقليد غير خال عن نوع من الضعف في الإبتداء على معنى يقبل الإزالة بنقيضه لو ألقى إليه فلا بد من تقويته وإثباته في نفس الصبي والعامي حتى يترشح ولا يتزلزل وليس الطريق في تقويته وإثباته أن يعلم صنعة الجدل والكلام بل يشتغل بتلاوة القرآن وتفسيره وقراءة الحديث ومعانيه ويشتغل بوظائف العبادات فلا يزال إعتقاده يزداد رسوخاً بما يقرع سمعه من أدلة القرآن وحججه وبما يرد عليه من شواهد الأحاديث
And how can that be denied while all the beliefs of the common people have as their principles mere instruction and pure imitation? Yes, the belief obtained by mere imitation is not free from a kind of weakness at the beginning, in the sense that it is susceptible to removal by its opposite if that opposite is presented to him. So it is necessary to strengthen and establish it in the soul of the child and the common person so that it becomes firm and does not waver. And the way to strengthen and establish it is not that he learn the art of dialectic and kalam, but rather he should occupy himself with recitation of the Qur'an and its interpretation, reading hadith and their meanings, and occupy himself with acts of worship. Then his belief will continue to increase in firmness by what reaches his ears of the proofs and evidences of the Qur'an and by what comes to him of the testimonies of hadith.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature and strengthening of belief in the common person. The author begins with a rhetorical question: 'And how can that be denied while all the beliefs of the common people have as their principles mere instruction and pure imitation?' This links to the previous discussion about faith coming without proof. He then acknowledges that belief based solely on imitation (taqlid) is initially weak and susceptible to being overturned if the opposite is presented. Therefore, it must be strengthened and firmly established in the soul of the child and the common person so that it becomes steadfast and not shaken. Crucially, the method for strengthening is not to learn the craft of dialectic (jadal) and kalam (speculative theology). Instead, the correct path is to engage with revelation: reciting the Qur'an and its interpretation (tafsir), reading hadith and their meanings, and performing acts of worship. Through these practices, the believer's conviction will become increasingly rooted as he hears the decisive proofs and evidences from the Qur'an and the supporting testimonies from hadith.

Misconception: Learning kalam (dialectical theology) is necessary to strengthen one's belief.
Correction: Imam Ghazali explicitly states that the way to strengthen belief is not through kalam but through engagement with the Qur'an, hadith, and worship.

Strengthening a Layperson's Belief: Avoiding Kalam, Embracing Quran and Worship
Ghazali prescribes strengthening faith without kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's method for strengthening the faith of common folk and why he warns against engaging them in theological debate.

Core Idea
Strengthen faith through Quran and worship, not through theological debate.

Ghazali addresses a pastoral concern: how to solidify the belief of those who accept faith through imitation. He prescribes a simple, spiritual regimen and strongly cautions against exposing them to kalam. This passage reveals his pedagogical wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without pausing. Let the flow of Ghazali's argument settle in your mind.
Notice the metaphors: seed, tree, iron mallet.
2
Identify the initial weakness
Ghazali says belief from pure imitation is weak and can be removed by its opposite. Understand why this is a problem.
3
Find the prescribed method
He lists several activities: reciting Quran and tafsir, reading hadith, performing worship, sitting with the righteous. List them in your own words.
These are all acts of devotion, not intellectual debate.
4
Reflect on the seed analogy
The initial talqin is like a seed, and the practices are like watering. Imagine how this growth occurs gradually.
The goal is a firmly rooted tree (faith).
5
Consider the warning
Ghazali says jadal and kalam disturb more than they settle. He compares using them to striking a tree with a mallet. Why would that be harmful?
He advises guarding the layperson's hearing from these disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجميع عقائد العوام مباديها التلقين المجرد والتقليد المحض (۱) ، نعم يكون الإعتقاد أنه الحاصل بمجرد التقليد غير خال عن نوع من الضعف في الإبتداء ، على معنى يقبل الإزالة بنقيضه لو ألقى إليه فلا بد من تقويته وإثباته في نفس الصبي والعامي ، حتى يترشح ولا يتزلزل(۲) وليس الطريق في تقويته وإثباته أن يعلم صنعة الجدل والكلام(۳) بل يشتغل بتلاوة القرآن وتفسيره (٤) ، وقراءة الحديث ومعانيه (٥) ، ويشتغل بوظائف العبادات (٦) ، فلا يزال إعتقاده يزداد رسوخاً بما يقرع سمعه من أدلة القرآن وحججه (۷) وبما يرد عليه من شواهد الأحاديث قوم قصد ذلك المسمى ، فيمكنه أن يعتقد كونه كاذباً مخبراً عنه على خلاف ما هو عليه ، ويمكنه أن يعتقد كونه صادقاً مخبراً عنه على ما هو عليه ، فهذا معقول على سبيل الإجمال يمكن أن يفهم هذه الألفاظ أموراً جملية غير مفصلة ويمكنه التصديق بها . وفوائدها ، وبما يسطع عليه من أنوار العبادات ووظائفها(۱) ، وبما يسري إليه من مشاهدة الصالحين ومجالستهم (۲) ، وسيماهم وسماعهم وهيآتهم في الخضوع الله عز وجل ، والخوف منه ، والإستكانة له (۳) ، فيكون أول التلقين كالقاء بذر في له(۳) الصدر ، وتكون هذه الأسباب كالسقي والتربية له (٤) ، حتى ينمو ذلك البذر ويقوى ، ويرتفع شجرة طيبة راسخة أصلها ثابت وفرعها في السماء(٥) وينبغي أن يحرس سمعه من الجدل والكلام غاية الحراسة ، فإن ما يشوشه أكثر مما يمهده ، وما يفسده أكثر مما يصلحه (٦) بل تقويته بالجدل تضاهي ضرب الشجرة بالمدقة من الحديد، رجاء تقويتها بأن تكثر أجزاؤها وربما يفتتها ذلك (٧) ويفسدها وهو الأغلب ، والمشاهدة تكفيك في هذا بيانا ، فناهيك الجدل بالعيان برهانا (۸)
And all the beliefs of the common folk, their starting points are pure oral instruction and pure imitation (taqlīd). Yes, the belief that is obtained by mere imitation is not free from a kind of weakness in the beginning, in the sense that it is susceptible to removal by its opposite if it were presented to him. So it is necessary to strengthen it and establish it in the soul of the boy and the commoner, so that it becomes firmly rooted and does not waver. And the way to strengthen it and establish it is not that he learns the craft of dialectic (jadal) and theology (kalām), but rather he occupies himself with recitation of the Qur'an and its interpretation (tafsīr), and reading of hadith and their meanings, and occupies himself with the duties of worship. Then his belief will continue to increase in firmness by what strikes his ears from the proofs and arguments of the Qur'an, and by what comes to him from the evidences of hadith. [A] people who intend that named [thing] can believe him to be lying, reporting about it contrary to what it is, and can believe him to be truthful, reporting about it as it is. This is intelligible in a summary way; he can understand these phrases as non-detailed general matters and can affirm them. And their benefits, and by what shines upon him from the lights of worship and its duties, and by what transfers to him from seeing the righteous and sitting with them, their marks, their speech, and their states in humility to Allah Almighty, fear of Him, and submission to Him. So the first oral instruction is like casting a seed into his breast, and these causes are like watering and nurturing it, until that seed grows and strengthens, and becomes a goodly tree, firmly rooted, with its branches in the sky. And he should guard his hearing from dialectic and theology with utmost guarding, for what they disturb is more than what they settle, and what they spoil is more than what they set right. Rather, strengthening by dialectic resembles striking the tree with an iron mallet, hoping to strengthen it so that its parts multiply, but perhaps that shatters it and spoils it, and that is the more common. And direct observation suffices you as explanation for this, so let [the example of] dialectic be sufficient as a clear proof by ocular evidence.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a continuous explanatory argument about the nature of common people's faith and the proper method of strengthening it, ending with a strong warning against dialectical theology. Imam Ghazali begins by stating that the beliefs of the common folk ('awamm) originate from pure oral instruction (talqīn) and pure imitation (taqlīd). He acknowledges that belief obtained through imitation is initially weak, as it can be removed by its opposite if presented. Therefore, it is necessary to strengthen and firmly establish this belief in the soul of a child or common person so that it becomes stable and does not waver. The crucial point is that this strengthening is not achieved by teaching them the craft of jadal (dialectic) and kalam (theology). Instead, the proper method is to engage them in reciting the Qur'an and its tafsir, reading hadith and understanding their meanings, and performing acts of worship. Through these practices, their belief becomes deeply rooted by the proofs and arguments of the Qur'an, the evidences of hadith, the lights of worship, and the company of righteous people. Ghazali then uses a powerful analogy: the initial talqīn is like planting a seed in the heart, and these spiritual practices are like watering and nurturing that seed until it grows into a firmly rooted tree. He then issues a stern warning: one must guard the common person's ears from dialectic and theology with utmost care, because these disciplines disturb more than they settle and spoil more than they set right. He compares using dialectic to strengthen faith to striking a tree with an iron mallet in hopes of making it stronger; more often, it shatters the tree. The conclusion is that direct observation of the effects of worship and righteous company is a sufficient proof, and the harm of jadal is evident to anyone who witnesses it.

Correction: Ghazali argues that jadal and kalam disturb more than they settle and spoil more than they fix, often shattering the fragile faith of a layperson.

Nurturing Faith and the Danger of Kalam
Analogy of planting a tree to describe nurturing faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Ghazali's analogy of faith as a seed that grows through worship and righteous company, and why theological debate weakens rather than strengthens faith.

Core Idea
Faith grows like a seed; debate shatters it like a hammer.

Imam Ghazali uses a vivid agricultural analogy to explain how faith is cultivated and protected. Just as a seed needs gentle watering and care, not hammer blows, faith grows through worship and righteous company, not theological argument. This lesson explores that analogy and its profound warning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the imagery of seed, tree, and hammer settle in your mind.
Notice the extended metaphor: talqeen (instruction) as sowing, 'ibadat (worship) as watering.
2
Understand the seed-to-tree analogy
Identify how Ghazali describes the beginning of faith: a seed cast into the heart, nurtured by worship and righteous company until it becomes a firmly rooted tree.
3
Examine the warning against debate
Find where Ghazali warns against exposing the novice to kalam and debate. Note his comparison: debate is like striking the tree with an iron hammer, which shatters more often than it strengthens.
Contrast the gentle nurturing of worship with the violent striking of debate.
4
Reflect on the role of observation
Ghazali concludes that observation (mushahada) is sufficient proof. Consider what he means by this and how it relates to the analogy.
Observation here refers to witnessing the effects of worship and the lives of the righteous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفوائدها ، وبما يسطع عليه من أنوار العبادات ووظائفها ، وبما يسري إليه من مشاهدة الصالحين ومجالستهم ، وسيماهم وسماعهم وهيآتهم في الخضوع لله عز وجل ، والخوف منه ، والإستكانة له ، فيكون أول التلقين كالقاء بذر في لُبّ الصدر ، وتكون هذه الأسباب كالسقي والتربية له ، حتى ينمو ذلك البذر ويقوى ، ويرتفع شجرة طيبة راسخة أصلها ثابت وفرعها في السماء وينبغي أن يحرس سمعه من الجدل والكلام غاية الحراسة ، فإن ما يشوشه أكثر مما يمهده ، وما يفسده أكثر مما يصلحه بل تقويته بالجدل تضاهي ضرب الشجرة بالمدقة من الحديد، رجاء تقويتها بأن تكثر أجزاؤها وربما يفتتها ذلك ويفسدها وهو الأغلب ، والمشاهدة تكفيك في هذا بيانا ، فناهيك بالعيان برهانا
And its benefits, and by what shines upon it from the lights of acts of worship and their functions, and by what reaches it from observing the righteous and sitting with them, and their appearance and listening to them and their states in humility to Allah Almighty, and fear of Him, and being submissive to Him. So the first instruction is like casting a seed into the core of the chest, and these causes are like watering and nurturing it, until that seed grows and becomes strong, rising as a good tree, firmly rooted, its root fixed and its branch in the sky. And he should guard his hearing from debate and kalam with utmost guarding, for what it disturbs is more than what it settles, and what it corrupts is more than what it reforms. Rather, strengthening it with debate resembles striking the tree with an iron hammer, hoping to strengthen it by increasing its parts, but perhaps that shatters it and corrupts it, and that is the more common. And observation suffices you in this as an illustration, so sufficient is the eye as a proof.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage uses an extended analogy to describe how faith is implanted and nurtured in a novice. The author begins by stating that the benefits of righteous company and worship act like light and water for the seed of faith planted in the heart. The seed is the first instruction (talgheen), and the subsequent causes (asbab) are like watering and nurturing, until the faith becomes a strong, firmly rooted tree. Then the author warns against exposing the novice to theological debate and kalam, because it disturbs more than it settles and corrupts more than it reforms. He compares strengthening faith through debate to striking a tree with an iron hammer in hopes of strengthening it by splitting its parts, but the result is often shattering and corruption. He concludes by saying that empirical observation (mushahada) is sufficient proof of this.

Firm vs Unstable Creed: Commoners and Theologians
Ghazali compares the steadfast creed of common pious people with the unstable creed of theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's comparison between the steadfast creed of common pious people and the unstable creed of theologians who rely on dialectical argumentation without true independent reasoning.

Core Idea
A creed built on taqlid is stable; one built on disputation without reflection is fragile.

Imam al-Ghazali contrasts two types of belief: that of the common pious people, which is firm as a mountain, and that of the theologians who engage in dialectical argumentation, which is unstable as a thread in the wind. He warns that merely memorizing proofs without personal reflection is still a form of taqlid and does not produce certainty. This passage invites us to reflect on the foundations of our own faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the vivid imagery: a lofty mountain vs a thread in the wind. What do these images convey about the strength of belief?
The mountain represents unshakeable faith; the thread represents vulnerability to doubt.
2
Identify the two groups compared
Identify the 'people of righteousness and piety' (commoners) and the 'theologians and disputers'. How does Ghazali describe each group's creed?
3
Understand the exception
Ghazali makes an exception for theologians who simply repeat proofs as taqlid. Why does he say they are no different from commoners in this regard?
Because both are following authority without personal reasoned conviction.
4
Reflect on the deeper lesson
Consider Ghazali's point: teaching a proof is not the same as independent reasoning (istidlal bi al-nazar). What does this imply about acquiring true certainty?
True certainty requires personal reflection, not just memorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقس عقيدة أهل الصلاح والتقى ، من عوام الناس ، بعقيدة المتكلمين والمجادلين ، فترى إعتقاد العامي في الثبات كالطود الشامخ ، لا تحركه الدواهي والصواعق ، وعقيدة المتكلم الحارس إعتقاده بتقسيمات الجدل (۱) ، كخيط مرسل في الهواء تفيئه الرياح مرة هكذا ومرة هكذا ، إلا من سمع منهم دليل الإعتقاد فتلقفه (۲) تقليدا ، كما تلقف نفس الإعتقاد تقليدا ، إذ لا فرق في التقلد بين تعلم الدليل أو تعلم المدلول (۳) ، فتلقين الدليل شيء والإستدلال بالنظر شيء آخر بعيد عنه
Compare the creed of the people of righteousness and piety, from the common people, to the creed of the theologians and disputers. You see the belief of the common person in steadfastness like a lofty mountain, which calamities and thunderbolts do not move. And the creed of the theologian, who guards his belief with the divisions of argumentation (1), is like a thread sent into the air, which the winds sway once this way and once that way—except for one who hears from them the proof of belief and receives it (2) as taqlid, just as he received the belief itself as taqlid. For there is no difference in taqlid between learning the proof or learning the thing proven (3). So teaching the proof is one thing, and reasoning by consideration is another thing far from him.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a comparison between two types of creed: that of the common pious people and that of the theologians (mutakallimun). Ghazali uses vivid imagery: the commoner's belief is like a lofty mountain, unmoved by calamities. In contrast, the theologian's belief, which he guards with the divisions of dialectical argumentation (taqsimat al-jadal), is likened to a thread in the air, blown about by winds. The exception is a theologian who merely repeats the proofs he heard as taqlid (imitation), without true independent reasoning (istidlal bi al-nazar). Such a person, according to Ghazali, is no different from the commoner in terms of taqlid, because learning the proof by rote is not the same as arriving at certainty through personal reflection.

Key Terms

عامي
متكلم
جدل
تقليد
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Engaging in theological argumentation (kalam) always strengthens one's faith.
Correction: Ghazali warns that without proper independent reasoning, reliance on disputation can lead to instability, as the theologian's belief is like a thread in the wind.

Checkpoint

A) A thread in the wind
B) A lofty mountain
C) A flowing river
D) A blazing fire

Key Takeaway
True steadfastness in creed comes from heartfelt conviction, not from mere dialectical skill or memorized proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Danger of Kalam for the Common Person
Ghazali recommends a hierarchy of occupations to avoid kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's practical advice that for a layperson, even worldly pursuits or play are safer than engaging in theological speculation (kalam).

Core Idea
For the common person, even play is safer than kalam.

Imam Ghazali offers a striking piece of pastoral advice: for a child nurtured on simple faith, it is better to occupy him with any lawful activity—even play—than to let him delve into theological debates. This passage from the Ihya' outlines a hierarchy of occupations, each preferable to the dangerous 'sea' of kalam. As you read, notice how Ghazali prioritizes spiritual and worldly benefits over intellectual exploration when faith is fragile.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the structured list of options Ghazali presents, from worship down to reflecting on the Hereafter.
The passage is a single paragraph but contains a clear hierarchy.
2
Identify the hierarchy of occupations
List the occupations in order: 1) worship, Quran, dhikr; 2) worldly sciences (grammar, arithmetic, etc.); 3) crafts; 4) play/amusement; 5) reflecting on the terrors of the Hereafter. Understand that each is recommended if the previous is not possible.
Note that even play is included as a valid option.
3
Understand the comparison between play and kalam
Ghazali says that play is safer than kalam because kalam leads to fisq (immorality/heresy) while play leads at worst to shirk (which is forgivable if repented). This highlights the extreme danger of theological speculation for the unqualified.
The Quranic verse supports that shirk is unforgivable only if unrepented.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verse
The verse 'Allah does not forgive shirk...' is cited to show that even the worst outcome of play (shirk) is potentially forgivable, whereas the harm from kalam is deeper and may lead to permanent deviation.
Ghazali uses this to emphasize the severity of kalam's harm.
5
Apply the advice to modern contexts
Consider how this advice applies today: Should we encourage new Muslims or young believers to engage in advanced theological debates, or focus on basic worship and useful knowledge?
Think about the principle of protecting faith from confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصبي اذا وقع نشوه على هذه العقيدة ، إن اشتغل بكسب الدنيا ، ثم أجاب قائلا : طريقه أن يشغل نفسه بالعبادة وقراءة القرآن والذكر ، وإن لم يقدر فيعلم آخر لا يناسب هذا الجنس من لغة ، أو نحو ، أو حساب ، أو طب ، أو فقه ، فإن لم يمكنه فبحرفة أو صناعة ؛ ولو الحراثة أو الحياكة ، فإن لم يقدر فيلعب ، أو لهو ، فإن لم يقدر فيحدث نفسه ، هول القيامة والحشر والنشر والحساب، وكل ذلك خير له من الغوص في هذا البحر البعيد عمقه ، العظيم خطره وضرره ، بل لو اشتغل العامي باللهو لا بالعبادات البدنية ربما كان أسلم له يخوض في البحث عن معرفة الله تعالى ، فإن ذلك عاقبته الفسق ، وهذا عاقبته الشرك ، و « إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء » .
If a child has grown up with this creed [i.e., the simple belief], if he occupies himself with earning a living, then [Ghazali] answered, saying: His way is to occupy himself with worship, reciting the Quran, and remembrance. If he is not able, then he should learn something else not of this kind, such as language, grammar, arithmetic, medicine, or jurisprudence. If that is not possible, then with a craft or trade, even plowing or weaving. If he is not able, then he should play or amuse himself. If he is not able, then he should speak to himself about the terrors of the Day of Judgment, the resurrection, the gathering, and the reckoning. All of that is better for him than diving into this sea whose depth is far, whose danger and harm are great. Indeed, if the common person occupied himself with amusement rather than physical acts of worship, it might be safer for him than delving into the search for knowledge of Allah the Exalted. For that [kalam] results in immorality, and this [amusement] results in polytheism. And 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills.'
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage provides practical advice for a child who has grown up with a simple, sound creed. Ghazali offers a hierarchy of occupations to keep such a person away from engaging in kalam. The best is worship and remembrance of Allah. If that is not possible, then learning useful worldly sciences like language, grammar, etc. If not, then a craft. If not, even play or idle talk is better. Finally, if nothing else, reflecting on the horrors of the Hereafter. Ghazali emphasizes that even sinless amusement is safer than engaging in theological speculation about Allah, because the latter can lead to heresy (fisq) while amusement at worst leads to shirk, but shirk can be forgiven if repented from, whereas the harm from kalam is severe. He quotes the Quranic verse to underscore that shirk is unforgivable if unrepented, but lesser sins are forgivable. The point is to highlight the extreme danger of kalam for the common person.

Key Terms
صبي
عقيدة
عبادة
لهو
فسق
شرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that any discussion about Allah is praiseworthy and beneficial, regardless of one's level of knowledge.
Correction: Ghazali warns that for the common person, engaging in theological speculation (kalam) can lead to fisq (heresy) and is more dangerous than even engaging in amusement, because it may confuse and corrupt one's faith.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is better for a common person than diving into the ocean of kalam?
A) Studying logic
B) Playing or amusing themselves
C) Debating with scholars
D) Writing theological works

Key Takeaway
Ghazali teaches that for the common believer, avoiding theological speculation is more important than many other activities, and even lawful play is safer than kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about the last time you heard or participated in a debate about Allah in which you felt uncertain afterward. Did it strengthen your faith or create doubts? What step can you take today to anchor yourself in simple, sincere worship instead of speculation?
?
If you are responsible for teaching someone new to Islam, how would Ghazali's hierarchy shape what you recommend they spend their time on? Are you willing to set aside topics that seem important but might harm them?
?
Consider your daily habits: do you prioritize activities that build certainty (like Quran and dhikr) or activities that entertain you? Where does your use of social media or theological discussions fit? What one change would you commit to this week to avoid the 'deep sea' Ghazali warns about?

The Fruit of Spiritual Striving and the Secret of Abu Bakr
Al-Ghazali describes the fruit of spiritual striving and the secret of Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how spiritual striving (mujāhada) leads to divine illumination and the highest level of faith, as exemplified by Abu Bakr al-Ṣiddīq.

Core Idea
Spiritual struggle opens the heart to divine light, revealing the deepest truths of faith.

Al-Ghazali explains that the path to true understanding of the creed is not through intellectual study alone but through active spiritual discipline. He describes the journey of the seeker who, with divine aid, engages in piety and self-restraint, leading to a divine light in the heart. This fruit of striving, he says, is the same secret that elevated Abu Bakr above others. This passage ties together action, divine guidance, and the highest station of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the sequence from striving to illumination.
2
Identify the seeker's actions
List the actions that the successful seeker undertakes: engaging in action, adhering to piety, forbidding the soul from whims, and occupying oneself with spiritual discipline and striving.
These are prerequisites for divine light.
3
Understand the divine light
Note that the result of these actions is not intellectual knowledge but a divine light cast into the heart. This is the fulfillment of Allah's promise in Quran 29:69.
This light reveals the realities of the creed.
4
Connect to the highest faith
Al-Ghazali identifies this light as the precious jewel that is the ultimate goal of the ṣiddīqīn and muqarrabīn. It is also the secret that distinguished Abu Bakr.
This shows the experiential nature of true faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سالكي طريق الآخرة ، وساعده التوفيق ، حتى اشتغل بالعمل ، ولازم التقوى ، ونهى النفس عن الهوى ، واشتغل بالرياضة والمجاهدة ، انفتحت له أبواب من الهداية ، تكشف عن حقائق هذه العقيدة (۱) ، بنور الهي يقذف في قلبه بسبب المجاهدة تحقيقا لوعده عز وجل إذ قال : وَالَّذِينَ جَاهَدُواْ فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا وَإِنَّ اللَّهَ لَمَعَ الْمُحْسِنِينَ ) (۲) وهو الجوهر النفيس الذي هو غاية إيمان الصديقين والمقربين ، وإليه الإشارة بالسر الذي وقر في صدر أبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه ، حيث فضل به الخلق (۳) .
For those who travel the path of the Hereafter, and success aids them, until they engage in action, adhere to piety, forbid the soul from whims, and occupy themselves with spiritual discipline (riyāḍa) and striving (mujāhada), the doors of guidance open for them, revealing the realities of this creed (1), by a divine light cast into their hearts through striving, fulfilling His promise, Exalted is He, when He said: 'And those who strive in Our (cause), We will surely guide them to Our ways. And indeed Allah is with the doers of good' (2). And it is the precious jewel which is the ultimate goal of the faith of the ṣiddīqīn (truthful ones) and the muqarrabīn (those brought near), and to it refers the secret that settled in the heart of Abū Bakr al-Ṣiddīq (may Allah be pleased with him), whereby he was favored over creation (3).
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive statement about the path to higher truth. Al-Ghazali begins by describing the traveler on the path of the Hereafter who is aided by divine success (tawfīq). He then enumerates the steps: engaging in action, adherence to piety, restraining the soul from whims, and occupying oneself with spiritual discipline (riyāḍa) and striving (mujāhada). As a result, the doors of guidance open, revealing the realities of the creed. This is not by intellectual effort but by a divine light (nūr ilāhī) cast into the heart. This is explained as a fulfillment of Allah's promise in the Qur'an (29:69). Al-Ghazali then identifies the fruit of this striving as the precious jewel (al-jawhar al-nafīs) which is the pinnacle of faith of the ṣiddīqīn and muqarrabīn. He connects this to the secret (sirr) that distinguished Abū Bakr al-Ṣiddīq, indicating that this hidden gnosis is the highest attainment.

المقربين (muqarrabīn)

Misconception: Many assume that higher faith and understanding of the creed come from theological study alone.
Correction: Al-Ghazali emphasizes that true understanding requires spiritual struggle (mujāhada) and divine illumination, not merely intellectual effort.

Two Extreme Views on Learning Kalam
Ghazali presents the two extreme views on learning kalam: forbidden vs obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two extreme positions on learning kalam and how they frame the debate.

Core Idea
Learning kalam is debated: some view it as a sin, others as a sacred duty.

Ghazali begins his discussion of kalam by presenting the two radical views that existed in his time. Before offering his own balanced position, he lays out these extremes to show the confusion in the community. This passage sets the stage for a nuanced ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two opposing views on learning kalam.
Notice the hyperbolic language used by each side.
2
Identify the first extreme
Find the view that considers kalam a forbidden innovation (bid'ah) and worse than any sin except shirk.
3
Identify the second extreme
Find the view that considers kalam an obligatory duty and the best of deeds.
4
Contrast the extremes
Notice the stark difference between the two positions and the absolute language used.
5
Anticipate the middle path
Reflect on why these extremes set the stage for Ghazali's own moderate position to follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسئلة
فإن قلت: تعلم الجدل والكلام مذموم كتعلم النجوم ، أو هو مباح (۱) ، أو مندوب إليه ؟
فاعلم أن للناس في هذا غلوا وإسراف (۲) في أطراف : فمن قائل إنه بدعة وحرام (۳) ، وأن العبد إن لقي الله عز وجل بكل ذنب سوى(٤) الشرك ، خير له من أن يلقاه بالكلام(٥)
ومن قائل أنه واجب ، وفرض إما على الكفاية (٦) أو على الأعيان ، وأنه أفضل الأعمال وأعلى القربات، فإنه تحقيق لعلم التوحيد (۷) ، ونضال عن دين الله تعالى (۸) .
Question: If you say: Learning dialectic and kalam is blameworthy, like learning astrology, or is it permissible (1), or recommended? Know that people have gone to extremes and excess (2) in the matter: some say it is an innovation (bid'ah) and forbidden (3), and that if a servant meets Allah Almighty with every sin except (4) shirk, it is better for him than to meet Him with kalam (5). And some say it is obligatory, and a communal (6) or individual obligation, and that it is the best of deeds and the highest of acts of worship, for it is the realization of the science of tawhid (7) and defense of the religion of Allah Almighty (8).
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of two extreme views on the ruling of learning kalam (dialectical theology). It begins with a hypothetical question: Is learning kalam blameworthy like astrology, or is it permissible, or even recommended? The author then states that people have gone to extremes: the first extreme considers kalam an innovation (bid'ah) and forbidden, to the extent that meeting Allah with every sin except shirk is better than meeting Him with kalam. The second extreme considers kalam obligatory (either as a communal or individual duty), and the best of deeds because it realizes the science of tawhid and defends the religion. The passage sets up the debate without yet giving the author's own position, but it immediately follows the introduction of the two extremes.

Misconception: One might think Ghazali endorses one of these extremes, but he is only presenting them; his own view follows later.
Correction: Ghazali is setting up the extremes to later introduce a balanced, moderate position on learning kalam.

Checkpoint

B) It is better than shirk.

Key Takeaway
The passage presents two contradictory extremes on learning kalam, showing the need for a balanced ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Would you be content that you took the balanced path, or would you have to admit you fell into one extreme? Commit now to one concrete step to move toward the middle.

The Two Extremes on Learning Kalam
Ghazali presents the question and the two extreme positions on learning kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two extreme positions regarding learning kalam (dialectical theology) as presented by Imam al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Kalam is either wholly forbidden or wholly obligatory—both extremes need balance.

Imam al-Ghazali introduces a key debate: Is learning kalam blameworthy, permissible, or recommended? He presents two opposing camps: one condemns it as an innovation, the other elevates it as a duty. This passage sets the stage for a nuanced ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translation. Notice the dialectical style starting with 'فإن قلت' (if you say).
The passage is a question followed by a description of two extreme views.
2
Identify the two extremes
After reading the translation, identify the two positions: one saying kalam is bid'ah and haram, the other saying it is wajib and the best of deeds.
Each extreme is introduced with 'فمن قائل' (some say) and 'ومن قائل' (and some say).
3
Understand the reasoning
Note the reasons given for each extreme: the first cites a report about meeting Allah; the second cites tawhid verification and defense of religion.
Ghazali does not endorse either yet; he is setting up the problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسئلة
فإن قلت: تعلم الجدل والكلام مذموم كتعلم النجوم، أو هو مباح، أو مندوب إليه؟
فاعلم أن للناس في هذا غلوا وإسراف في أطراف: فمن قائل إنه بدعة وحرام، وأن العبد إن لقي الله عز وجل بكل ذنب سوى الشرك، خير له من أن يلقاه بالكلام
ومن قائل أنه واجب، وفرض إما على الكفاية أو على الأعيان، وأنه أفضل الأعمال وأعلى القربات، فإنه تحقيق لعلم التوحيد، ونضال عن دين الله تعالى.
Question: If you say: Learning disputation and kalam is blameworthy like learning astrology, or it is permissible, or recommended? Then know that people have excess and extravagance in this matter at extremes: some say it is an innovation and forbidden, and that the servant if he meets God Almighty with every sin except polytheism, it is better for him than to meet Him with kalam. And some say it is obligatory, a duty either as collective obligation or individual obligation, and that it is the best of deeds and the highest of acts of worship, for it is the verification of the science of tawhid and a defense of the religion of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical question-and-answer. Ghazali begins by posing a question about the ruling on learning jadal (disputation) and kalam (dialectical theology): is it blameworthy like learning astrology, or is it permissible, or even recommended? He then states that people have gone to extremes: one faction declares it an innovation (bid'ah) and forbidden (haram), citing the famous report that meeting God with any sin except polytheism is better than meeting Him with kalam. The other faction declares it obligatory (wajib), either as a collective duty (fard kifayah) or an individual duty (fard 'ayn), and deems it the best of deeds and the highest act of worship because it verifies the science of tawhid and defends the religion of God. Ghazali presents both extremes without endorsement, setting up the need for a balanced ruling.

جدل
كلام
بدعة
حرام
واجب
فرض كفاية
فرض عين
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That it is a collective obligation.

C) That it is the best of deeds.
D) That it is recommended.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on learning kalam is contested: some deem it forbidden, others obligatory; Ghazali presents both extremes before offering a balanced view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
