Kalam Condemned by Early Imams
Ghazali quotes reports from Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal condemning kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that early Sunni imams like al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal strongly condemned the study of kalam (speculative theology) and the reasons for their condemnation.

Core Idea
Early imams saw kalam as a dangerous innovation that leads away from the Book and Sunnah.

This lesson presents firsthand reports from two of the most authoritative early Sunni imams—al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal—showing their extreme opposition to the study of kalam. Imam al-Ghazali collects these narrations to demonstrate that this field of theology was not only discouraged but actively suppressed by the early community. As you read, pay attention to the severity of their language and the specific practices they condemned.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the different narrators and the variety of incidents reported.
This passage is a collection of reports, not a single continuous narrative.
2
Focus on al-Shafi'i's statements
Identify al-Shafi'i's general condemnations and the specific incident with Hafs al-Fard. What did al-Shafi'i say about people who debate whether the name is the named?
The Hafs incident shows al-Shafi'i's extreme stance, declaring him an unbeliever.
3
Examine Ahmad ibn Hanbal's reaction
Ahmad ibn Hanbal not only condemned kalam in general but also boycotted al-Harith al-Muhasibi for writing a refutation. Why did he do this?
Ahmad feared that describing innovations would lead people to study them.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that these reports prove al-Shafi'i was extremely prohibitive of kalam. Consider why the early imams were so harsh, even though later scholars like Ghazali himself engaged in kalam within limits.
This tension between prohibition and later practice is a key theme.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ايضاً : لو علم الناس ما في الكلام من الأهواء لفروا منه فرارهم من الاسد وقال أيضا إذا سمعت الرجل يقول : الاسم هو المسمى أو غير المسمى فاشهد بأنه من أهل الكلام ولا دين له قال الزعفراني قال الشافعي حكمى في أصحاب الكلام أن يضربوا ويطاف في القبائل والبشائر ، ويقال هذا جزاء من ترك الكتاب والسنة وأخذ في الكلام . حضرت الشافعي، وحفص المفرد سأل الشافعي ، فاحتج عليه بأن كلام الله غير مخلوق ، وكفر حفص المفرد ، قال الربيع : ولقيته فقال أراد الشافعي قتلي وقال أحمد بن حنبل : لا يفلح صاحب الكلام ابداً ، ولا تكاد ترى أحدا نظر في الكلام إلا وفي قلبه دغل وبالغ في ذمه حتى هجر الحارث المحاسبي ، مع زهده وورعه بسبب تصنيفه كتابا في الرد على المبتدعة ، وقال له ويحك ألست تحكى بدعتهم أولا ، ثم ترد عليهم ؟ ألست تحمل الناس بتصنيفك على مطالعة البدعة والتفكر قوله بالجريد : ويحملوا على الإبل . وقال أبو نعيم بن عدي وغيره : قال داود بن سليمان عن الكرابيسي ، سمع الشافعي يقول : ( حكمي في أهل الكلام حكم عمر في ضبيغ ) وأخرج إبن أبي حاتم عن الربيع قال : رأيت الشافعي وهو نازل من الدرجة ، وقوم في المسجد يتكلمون بشيء من الكلام فصاح فقال : إما أن تجاورونا بخير ، وإما أن تقوموا عنا . وهذه الآثار وغيرها تدل دلالة قاطعة على أن الإمام الشافعي كان شديد النهي عن علم الكلام
And he said also: If people knew what is in kalam of whims, they would flee from it as they flee from a lion. And he said also: If you hear a man saying: 'The name is the named or other than the named,' then testify that he is from the people of kalam and has no religion. Al-Za'farani said: Al-Shafi'i said: My judgment concerning the people of kalam is that they be beaten and paraded among the tribes and the bearers of good news, and it be said: This is the recompense of one who abandoned the Book and the Sunnah and took up kalam. I attended Al-Shafi'i, and Hafs al-Fard asked Al-Shafi'i, and he argued against him that the speech of Allah is uncreated, and Al-Shafi'i declared Hafs al-Fard an unbeliever. Al-Rabi' said: I met him and he said: Al-Shafi'i wanted to kill me. And Ahmad ibn Hanbal said: The practitioner of kalam will never succeed, and you almost never see anyone who has looked into kalam except that there is treachery in his heart. And he went to extremes in condemning him until he boycotted al-Harith al-Muhasibi, despite his asceticism and piety, because of his authorship of a book in refutation of the innovators, and he said to him: Woe to you! Do you not first narrate their innovation, then refute them? Do you not burden the people by your authorship to study innovation and reflect? His saying 'bi'l-jarid': and they are carried on camels. And Abu Nu'aym ibn 'Adi and others said: Dawud ibn Sulayman narrated from al-Karabisi, I heard al-Shafi'i say: My judgment concerning the people of kalam is like the judgment of 'Umar concerning Dubaygh. And Ibn Abi Hatim narrated from al-Rabi' who said: I saw al-Shafi'i descending from the staircase, and a group in the mosque were discussing something of kalam, and he shouted: Either you keep company with us in goodness, or you leave us. And these reports and others indicate conclusively that Imam al-Shafi'i was extremely prohibitive of the science of kalam.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of reports from early Sunni imams—particularly al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal—condemning the study of kalam (speculative theology). The author, Imam al-Ghazali, presents these narrations to demonstrate the severity of their prohibition. The passage begins with al-Shafi'i warning that kalam is filled with whims (ahwa') and that people would flee from it if they knew its danger. He then identifies a telltale sign of a kalam practitioner: someone who debates whether the name (al-ism) is the named (al-musamma) or other than the named—a classic theological dispute. Al-Shafi'i declares such a person to be from 'the people of kalam' and without religion. Next, al-Za'farani reports that al-Shafi'i ruled that kalam advocates should be physically beaten and paraded as a public deterrent. The passage then relates a specific confrontation between al-Shafi'i and Hafs al-Fard, a known mutakallim who argued that the Qur'an is created (kalam Allah ghayr makhluq). Al-Shafi'i argued against him and declared him an unbeliever (kafir), and Hafs later claimed that al-Shafi'i intended to kill him. The narrative shifts to Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who states that a practitioner of kalam will never succeed and that kalam inevitably corrupts the heart. He went so far as to boycott al-Harith al-Muhasibi, a renowned ascetic, because al-Muhasibi wrote a book refuting innovators—Ahmad's reasoning being that simply describing the innovations in writing exposes people to them. A final report from al-Shafi'i compares his judgment on kalam people to 'Umar's harsh treatment of Dubaygh, and another shows him physically ejecting people from a mosque for discussing kalam. Ghazali concludes that these reports conclusively prove al-Shafi'i's extreme prohibition of kalam. The passage serves as a cautionary foundation, though the author himself will later engage in kalam within defined limits.

Key Terms
الكلام
الأهواء
الاسم
المسمى
كلام الله غير مخلوق
الحارث المحاسبي
ضبيغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think early imams prohibited all theology, including simple creedal statements.
Correction: The passage shows they prohibited speculative kalam, not foundational beliefs. They themselves affirmed core tenets like the uncreatedness of the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Shafi'i say about someone who says 'the name is the named or other than the named'?
A) He is a believer with correct theology.
B) He is from the people of kalam and has no religion.
C) He should be beaten and paraded.
D) He is an unbeliever because of this statement.

Key Takeaway
Early Sunni imams considered kalam a dangerous innovation that warranted severe measures, including physical punishment and social exclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about religious beliefs, do you ever examine your own heart to see if you are driven by a love for truth or by the excitement of debate? If an imam today warned you as al-Shafi'i warned Hafs al-Fard, would you have the humility to stop and repent?
?
Have you ever spent time reading or watching content about theological controversies, only to find that it planted doubts or distractions in your heart? What would it look like for you to 'flee from kalam as from a lion'—what practical steps would you take?
?
If you were in the position of al-Harith al-Muhasibi, sincerely trying to correct falsehood, and a righteous scholar like Ahmad ibn Hanbal boycotted you for your method, would you submit to his criticism or defend your approach? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to your own methods over obedience to scholars?

The Permissibility of New Terminology in Kalam
Ghazali presents the second group's defense of kalam's terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second group's argument that the use of novel terms like 'jawhar' and 'arad' in kalam is permissible because all sciences develop their own technical vocabulary, and the correctness of the meaning is what matters.

Core Idea
New terms are permissible if meanings are sound, just as all sciences coin terms.

Imam al-Ghazali now presents the defense of the second group: those who argue that the only novelty in kalam is its terminology. They point out that every field—hadith, tafsir, fiqh—has coined its own terms. So if the meaning is correct, new words are like new vessels for permissible use. This lesson unpacks that analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument. Notice the examples of sciences that also have special terms.
2
Identify the objection and the defense
The objection is about the strange terms used in kalam. The defense argues that every science has its own terms, so this is not a problem.
Look for the key terms: الجوهر, العرض, etc.
3
Examine the analogy with vessel
The passage ends with an analogy: introducing a new expression for a correct meaning is like making a new vessel for a permissible use. Consider how this analogy supports the argument.
The analogy highlights permissibility based on the purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفرقة الأخرى فاحتجوا بأن قالوا: إن المحذور من الكلام إن كان هو لفظ الجوهر والعرض، وهذه الإصطلاحات الغربية التي لم تعهدها الصحابة رضي الله عنهم فالأمر فيه قريب، إذ ما من علم إلا وقد أحدث فيه إصطلاحات لأجل التفهيم، كالحديث والتفسير، والفقه، ولو عرض عليهم عبارة النقض والكسر والتركيب والتعدية وفساد الوضع، إلى جميع الأسئلة التي تورد على القياس، لما كانوا يفقهونه. فاحداث عبارة للدلالة بها على مقصود صحيح كاحداث آنية على هيئة جديدة لإستعمالها في مباح.
And as for the other group, they argued by saying: The objectionable thing in kalam, if it is the term substance (jawhar) and accident (arad), and these strange terminologies that the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, were not accustomed to, then the matter is easy, for there is no science except that new terminologies have been introduced in it for the sake of explanation, like hadith, tafsir, and fiqh. And if there were presented to them the expressions of refutation, destruction, composition, transitivity, and corruption of placement, along with all the questions that are raised concerning analogy, they would not understand them. So introducing an expression to indicate a correct meaning is like introducing a vessel in a new form for use in permissible things.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the response of the second group—those who defend the permissibility of kalam. They argue that if the only objection to kalam is its use of novel terms like 'substance' (jawhar) and 'accident' (arad) which the Companions were not familiar with, then this is not a valid objection. Every science (including hadith, tafsir, and fiqh) has developed its own technical terminology to facilitate understanding. For instance, the technical terms used in analogy (qiyas) such as 'refutation', 'composition', etc., would not have been understood by the Companions if presented to them, yet these are accepted. Therefore, creating new terms to convey correct meanings is analogous to creating new vessels for permissible uses—it is inherently permissible as long as the content is sound.

The Permissibility of Kalam: Refuting Three Objections
Al-Ghazali refutes three objections against kalam by distinguishing terminology, content, and consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ghazali's method of refuting three objections against the discipline of kalam by distinguishing between terminology, content, and consequences.

Core Idea
Kalam's new terms are tools; its meaning is divine proof; its misuse does not forbid the science.

This passage from Al-Ghazali's Qawa'id al-Aqa'id presents the defense of kalam against its critics. Al-Ghazali's supporters address three possible objections: the unfamiliar terminology used in kalam, the content itself, and the negative consequences that can arise. By separating these, they show that the objections do not invalidate the science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three objections
Read the Arabic and translation to see the three-part structure. Notice how each objection is introduced with 'If the objectionable aspect is...' and then answered.
The passage is a clear enumeration; watch for the three conditions.
2
Examine the rebuttal of the terminology objection
The first objection targets terms like 'substance' and 'accident'. The rebuttal argues that all sciences coin new terms for clarity, using analogical reasoning (qiyas) as an example. The companions would not have understood those terms either.
3
Examine the rebuttal of the meaning objection
The second objection targets the content itself. The rebuttal clarifies that the intended meaning is simply knowledge of proofs for God's existence and attributes, as found in revelation. Asking for proof is not forbidden.
4
Examine the rebuttal of the consequences objection
The third objection targets negative outcomes like disputation. The rebuttal admits such outcomes are forbidden but argues that other sciences also have negative potentials and are not forbidden. Quranic verses are cited to support seeking proof.
5
Synthesize the overall argument
Reflect on how these three rebuttals collectively defend kalam by showing that objections are either about superficial aspects, misconstrued content, or collateral effects that do not negate the science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونهاهم عن الكلام في القدر وقال : « أمسكوا عن القدر» وعلى هذا استمر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم فالزيادة على الأستاذ طغيان وظلم ، وهم الأستاذون والقدوة ، ونحن الأتباع والتلامذة . وأما الفرقة الأخرى فاحتجوا بأن قالوا : إن المحذور من الكلام إن كان هو لفظ الجوهر والعرض ، وهذه الإصطلاحات الغربية التي لم تعهدها الصحابة رضي الله عنهم فالأمر فيه قريب ، إذ ما من علم إلا وقد أحدث فيه إصطلاحات لأجل التفهيم ، كالحديث والتفسير ، والفقه ، ولو عرض عليهم عبارة النقض والكسر والتركيب والتعدية وفساد الوضع ، الى جميع الأسئلة التي تورد على القياس ، لما كانوا يفقهونه فاحداث عبارة للدلالة بها على مقصود صحيح كاحداث آنية على هيئة جديدة لإستعمالها في مباح وإن كان المحذور هو المعنى فنحن لا نعني به إلا معرفة الدليل على حدوث العالم ووحدانية الخالق وصفاته ، كما جاء في الشرع ، فمن أين تحرم معرفة الله تعالى بالدليل ؟ وإن كان المحذور هو التشغب والتعصب والعداوة والبغضاء وما يفضي إليه الكلام ، فذلك محرم ، ويجب الإحتراز عنه ، كما أن الكبر والعجب والرياء وطلب الرئاسة مما يفضي إليه علم الحديث والتفسير والفقه ، وهو محرم يجب الإحتراز عنه ، ولكن لا يمنع من العلم لأجل آدئه إليه، وكيف يكون ذكر الحجة والمطالبة بها والبحث عنها محظوراً ، وقد قال الله تعالى قُلْ هَاتُوا بُرْهَانَكُمْ وقال عز وجل : لِيَهْلِكَ مَنْ هَلَكَ عَنْ بَيِّنَةٍ ، وَيَحْيَى مَنْ حَيَّ وقال تعالى : ﴿ إِنْ عِنْدَكُمْ مِّنْ سُلْطَانٍ بِهَذا) ـ أي حجة وبرهان
And he forbade them from speaking about destiny and said: 'Refrain from destiny.' And upon this the Companions, may God be pleased with them, continued. So exceeding the teacher is transgression and injustice, and they are the teachers and the role models, and we are the followers and the students. As for the other group, they argued by saying: If the objectionable aspect of kalam is the term 'substance' and 'accident' and these unfamiliar terminologies that the Companions, may God be pleased with them, did not encounter, then the matter is close [to being permissible], for there is no science except that it introduced terminologies for the purpose of clarification, like hadith, exegesis, and jurisprudence. And if the expressions of refutation, breaking, composition, transitive verbs, invalidity of construction, and all the questions that are raised concerning analogy were presented to them, they would not have understood them. So introducing a phrase to indicate a correct meaning is like introducing a vessel in a new form for its use in what is permissible. And if the objectionable aspect is the meaning, then we intend by it nothing but knowledge of the proof for the temporal origination of the world, the oneness of the Creator, and His attributes, as came in the Sacred Law. So from where is knowledge of God Almighty by proof forbidden? And if the objectionable aspect is contentiousness, partisanship, enmity, hatred, and what kalam leads to, then that is forbidden, and it is obligatory to guard against it, just as arrogance, vanity, hypocrisy, and seeking leadership are among what the sciences of hadith, exegesis, and jurisprudence lead to, and they are forbidden and must be guarded against, but that does not prevent [engaging in] knowledge because of what it leads to. And how can mentioning the proof, demanding it, and researching it be prohibited, while God Almighty said: 'Say: Bring your proof' (Surah 2:111), and He, mighty and majestic, said: 'So that he who perishes might perish upon clear evidence, and he who lives might live upon clear evidence' (Surah 8:42), and He, exalted, said: 'You have no authority [sultan] for this' (Surah 10:68) — meaning proof and evidence.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of those who permit kalam (theological discourse) in response to those who prohibit it. It begins by referencing a group that forbade discussion of destiny (qadar) and notes that the Companions followed this prohibition, establishing them as teachers and role models. Then the 'other group' (those permitting kalam) rebuts by considering three possible objections. First, if the objection is to the unfamiliar terminology used in kalam—such as 'substance' (jawhar) and 'accident' (arad)—they argue that every science, including hadith, exegesis, and jurisprudence, introduces new terms for clarification. They point out that even the technical language of analogical reasoning would have been unfamiliar to the Companions, so creating new expressions for sound meanings is permissible, like making new vessels for lawful use. Second, if the objection is to the meaning itself, they clarify that the intended meaning is simply knowledge of proofs for the world's temporal origination (huduth al-ʿalam), God's oneness, and His attributes as found in revelation. They ask rhetorically how knowledge of God through proof could be forbidden. Third, if the objection is to the negative consequences of kalam—contentiousness (tashaghghub), partisanship, enmity, and hatred—they concede that such outcomes are forbidden and must be avoided. However, they draw an analogy: other sciences like hadith, exegesis, and jurisprudence can also lead to arrogance, vanity, hypocrisy, and desire for leadership, yet that does not forbid the sciences themselves. The passage concludes with Quranic evidence: God commands bringing proof (Surah 2:111), makes perishing and living contingent on clear evidence (Surah 8:42), and denounces claims without authority (Surah 10:68), establishing that seeking and presenting proofs is not prohibited but encouraged.

العرض (arad)
الإصطلاح (istilah)
التشغب (tashaghghub)
الحجة (hujja)
البرهان (burhan)
السلطان (sultan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That kalam is forbidden because it introduces new terminology or because it can lead to disputation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that terminology innovation is permissible as it occurs in all sciences, and the potential misuse of kalam does not forbid the science itself, just as other sciences with negative potentials are not forbidden.

Checkpoint
According to the second group, what is the answer if the objection to kalam is based on its use of unfamiliar terms like 'jawhar' and 'arad'?
A) Such terms are forbidden because the Companions did not use them.
B) Every science develops new terminology for explanation, so it is permissible.
C) Only Arabic terms from the Quran are allowed.
D) The terms must be changed to those used by the Companions.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of kalam is defended by showing that its terminology is a legitimate tool, its content is the true knowledge of God, and its potential misuse does not forbid the science itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever refrained from studying or discussing deep theological proofs because you feared it would lead to arguments or division? How does al-Ghazali's distinction between the science and its consequences challenge you to reconsider that hesitation?
?

Ghazali's Refutation: Analyzing the Object of Prohibition
Ghazali distinguishes between meaning and discord as possible objections, showing meaning is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's method of refuting the prohibition of kalam by analyzing the object of prohibition and distinguishing between essential meaning and negative consequences.

Core Idea
The true object of kalam cannot be forbidden because it is obligatory.

This passage from Ghazali's Qawaid al-Aqa'id addresses a common objection to the study of kalam. Critics argue that kalam leads to disputes and discord, thus should be prohibited. Ghazali responds by analyzing what exactly is objectionable. He shows that if the objection is to the essential meaning—knowledge of divine proofs—then it is obligatory and cannot be forbidden. If the objection is to the negative consequences, those are indeed forbidden, but that does not make the science itself forbidden, just as other sciences have similar pitfalls. He then supports his position with Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities for المحذور
Read the first part of the passage where Ghazali presents two possible interpretations of what is objectionable (المحذور) in kalam. Notice the two alternatives: the meaning (المعنى) and disputation (التشغب).
This sets up the entire argument.
2
Understand the first possibility: meaning
Ghazali defines the meaning of kalam as knowledge of proofs for the world's creation, God's oneness, and His attributes. Since this knowledge is obligatory, it cannot be forbidden. Ask yourself: why would obligatory knowledge be prohibited?
3
Examine the second possibility: disputation
If the objection is disputation, partisanship, and hatred, Ghazali agrees these are forbidden. But he argues that this does not make the science itself forbidden, using an analogy with other sciences like hadith, tafsir, and fiqh, which also can lead to pride and showing off.
Notice the analogy: the forbidden consequences do not nullify the science.
4
Consider the Quranic support
Read the Quranic verses Ghazali cites: 'Bring your proof', 'that he who perishes might perish after clear evidence', 'You have no authority for this', and 'To Allah belongs the conclusive argument'. Reflect on how these verses encourage seeking proof.
These verses show that seeking proof is divinely mandated.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Synthesize Ghazali's argument: the essential purpose of kalam is praiseworthy and obligatory; the negative consequences are blameworthy but do not justify prohibiting the science itself. This is a key methodological principle in defending Islamic theology.
This lesson helps you see how Ghazali balances the benefits and harms of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان المحذور هو المعنى (١) فنحن لا نعني به إلا معرفة الدليل على حدوث العالم ووحدانية الخالق وصفاته ، كما جاء في الشرع ، فمن أين تحرم معرفة الله تعالى بالدليل ؟(٢) وإن كان المحذور هو التشغب (٣) والتعصب والعداوة والبغضاء وما يفضي إليه الكلام ( ٤ ) ، فذلك محرم (٥) ، ويجب الإحتراز عنه ، كما أن الكبر والعجب والرياء (٤) وطلب الرئاسة مما يفضي إليه علم الحديث والتفسير والفقه ، وهو محرم يجب الإحتراز عنه ، ولكن لا يمنع من العلم (٦) لأجل آدئه إليه، وكيف يكون ذكر الحجة والمطالبة بها والبحث عنها محظوراً ، وقد قال الله تعالى قُلْ هَاتُوا بُرْهَانَكُمْ ) (٧) وقال عز وجل : لِيَهْلِكَ مَنْ هَلَكَ عَنْ بَيِّنَةٍ ، وَيَحْيَى مَنْ حَيَّ (٨) وقال تعالى : ﴿ إِنْ عِنْدَكُمْ مِّنْ سُلْطَانٍ بِهَذا) (١) ـ أي حجة وبرهان وقال تعالى : ﴿ قُلْ فَلِلَّهِ الْحُجَّةُ الْبَالِغَةُ ﴾
And if the objectionable thing is the meaning (1) then we mean by it nothing but knowledge of the proof for the temporal origination of the world, the oneness of the Creator, and His attributes, as came in the Law; so from where would knowledge of Allah the Exalted by proof be forbidden? (2) And if the objectionable thing is disputation (3), partisanship, enmity, hatred, and what kalam leads to (4), then that is forbidden (5) and it is necessary to avoid it, just as pride, vanity, showing off (4), and seeking leadership are among the things that the science of hadith, tafsir, and fiqh lead to, and they are forbidden, it is necessary to avoid them, but they do not prevent from the science (6) because of what leads to it. How can the mention of proof, demanding it, and searching for it be prohibited, when Allah the Exalted has said: 'Say: Bring your proof' (7); and He, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'So that he who perishes might perish after clear evidence, and he who lives might live after clear evidence' (8); and He said: 'You have no authority for this' (9) – that is, proof and evidence; and He said: 'Say: To Allah belongs the conclusive argument.'
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that kalam (dialectical theology) is prohibited. Ghazali begins by analyzing what exactly is meant by 'المحذور' (the objectionable thing). He posits two possibilities: first, that the objectionable thing is the 'meaning' (المعنى), i.e., the essential purpose of kalam. He clarifies that the meaning of kalam is nothing other than knowledge of the proofs for the temporal creation of the world, the oneness of the Creator, and His attributes as revealed in the Law. Since knowing Allah through proof is an obligation, it cannot be forbidden. Second, if the objectionable thing is the disputation, partisanship, enmity, and hatred that kalam sometimes produces, then those are indeed forbidden and must be avoided. However, Ghazali argues that this does not make the science itself forbidden, using an analogy: other sciences like hadith, tafsir, and fiqh also sometimes lead to pride, vanity, showing off, and seeking leadership – which are forbidden – yet the sciences themselves are not prohibited. Finally, he supports his point by citing Quranic verses that command producing proof and mention that perishing and living are based on clear evidence, and that Allah possesses the conclusive argument. Thus, seeking proof cannot be prohibited.

Key Terms
المحذور
التشغب
الحجة
البرهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that if kalam leads to disputes, it should be entirely forbidden.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that it is only the negative consequences that are forbidden, not the science itself. The essential meaning of kalam is obligatory knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the essential meaning of kalam that cannot be forbidden?
A) Disputation and partisanship
B) Knowledge of proofs for the world's origination, God's oneness, and His attributes
C) Pride and showing off
D) Seeking leadership in religious sciences

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the prohibition of kalam by showing that its essential purpose is obligatory knowledge, while its negative consequences, though forbidden, do not outlaw the science itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any obligations in your faith—like seeking knowledge of Allah's proofs—that you have avoided because you feared they might lead to arguments? What would change if you pursued that knowledge while guarding your intention from partisanship?
?
When you engage in religious discussions, do you catch yourself seeking victory rather than truth? How can you, right now, commit to distinguishing between the meaning (truth) and the discord (disputation) in your next conversation?
?
The Quran commands you to bring proof. Have you ever been content with blind imitation without understanding the evidence for your belief in Allah? What concrete step will you take this week to learn one proof from the Quran or reason and reflect on it until it becomes conviction?

The Quran as a Book of Argument
Ghazali establishes that the Quran is a continuous argument against disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's thesis that the Quran is fundamentally a book of argument (muhājaja) centered on tawḥīd and nubuwwa.

Core Idea
The Quran is a continuous debate with disbelievers, arguing for tawḥīd and nubuwwa.

Imam al-Ghazali opens his discussion of Islamic creed by highlighting the argumentative nature of the Quran. He is not merely describing a feature but establishing a methodological principle: the Quran's core purpose is to engage in rational debate with those who reject monotheism and prophethood. This lesson examines his evidence and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic excerpt from Ghazali's text, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the three Quranic verses he cites.
For a note: The verses are from Surah Al-An'am (6:83), Surah Hud (11:32), and Surah Ash-Shu'ara (26:23-30).
2
Identify the three Quranic examples
Notice the three stories: Abraham arguing with his people, Noah disputing with his people, and Moses confronting Pharaoh. These illustrate prophetic argumentation.
3
Understand Ghazali's sweeping conclusion
Ghazali says 'On the whole, the Quran from its beginning to its end is a debate with the disbelievers.' This is a strong claim about the Quran's central purpose.
4
Grasp the logical deduction about content
Ghazali reasons that since prophetic debates were not about detailed legal rulings, they must have been about the core beliefs of tawḥīd and nubuwwa.
This frames the Quran's main subject matter.
5
Reflect on the implication for Aqidah
Considering the Quran as argumentative means that faith is established through reasoning and evidence, not blind acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال عز وجل: (وَتِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا آتَيْنَاهَا إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَى قَوْمِهِ) وقال تعالى: (قَالُوا يَا نُوحُ قَدْ جَادَلْتَنَا فَأَكْثَرْتَ جِدَالَنَا) وقال تعالى في قصة فرعون: (وَمَا رَبُّ الْعَالَمِينَ) إلى قوله: (أَوَلَوْ جِئْتُكَ بِشَيْءٍ مُّبِينٍ). وعلى الجملة فالقرآن من أوله إلى آخره محاجة مع الكفار. والمراد: أنه معلوم أن مجادلة الرسول مع الكفار لا تكون في تفاصيل الأحكام الشرعية، فلم يبق إلا أنها كانت في التوحيد والنبوة.
And He, Mighty and Majestic, said: "And that is Our argument which We gave to Abraham against his people." And He, exalted, said: "They said, O Noah, you have disputed with us and made much dispute with us." And He, exalted, said concerning the story of Pharaoh: "And what is the Lord of the worlds?" until His saying: "Or have I come to you with something clear?" And on the whole, the Qur'an from its beginning to its end is a debate (muhājaja) with the disbelievers. And the intent is that it is known that the disputation of the Messenger with the disbelievers is not about the details of legal rulings, so nothing remains except that it was about tawḥīd and nubuwwa.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage classifies the nature of the Quran as a book of argument. Ghazali cites three verses: Abraham's argument against his people, Noah's people accusing him of excessive disputation, and Moses' dialogue with Pharaoh. He then states that the entire Quran is a continuous debate (muhājaja) with disbelievers. He clarifies that the prophetic debates were not about branches of law but about the core doctrines of tawḥīd (divine oneness) and nubuwwa (prophethood).

Key Terms
محاجة (muhājaja)
جدال (jidāl)
توحيد (tawḥīd)
نبوة (nubuwwa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran contains arguments only incidentally or sporadically.
Correction: Ghazali asserts that the Quran is inherently and continuously a book of argument (muhājaja) from beginning to end, not merely containing arguments here and there.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the nature of the Quran from beginning to end?
A) A book of legal rulings
B) A continuous debate with disbelievers
C) A collection of stories
D) A code of ethics

Key Takeaway
The Quran is a continuous rational argument (muhājaja) aimed at establishing tawḥīd and nubuwwa against disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you sense that it is personally arguing with you about your beliefs in tawḥīd and prophethood? If not, what changes in your reading would let you hear that challenge?
?
The Quran's arguments are not about fine legal details but about the foundations: Who is your Lord? Do you accept His oneness and the prophethood of His messengers? Answer honestly: have you settled these questions by rational conviction or by habit?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment, and He says, 'My Book debated you clearly—did you engage with it or turn away?' What will you say? Will you commit today to reading one passage of the Quran as a direct argument to your soul?

Al-Razi's Testimony on the Quran's Sufficiency
Ghazali introduces and quotes Imam al-Razi's testimony on the Quran's superiority over kalām and philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's critique of kalām and philosophy and his conclusion that the Quran provides the most direct path to knowledge.

Core Idea
The Quran, not kalām, quenches the intellectual thirst.

Imam al-Ghazali begins by stating that all true proofs are contained in the Quran and traditions. He then admires Imam al-Razi's personal testimony, who after studying kalām and philosophy found them unsatisfying and turned to the Quranic method. This lesson explores al-Razi's journey and his key insight.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's introduction
Notice Ghazali's claim that the Quran and traditions contain all proofs, and anything else is blameworthy.
2
Understand al-Razi's search
Al-Razi studied kalām and philosophy but found them unquenching. Reflect on what that means.
He calls them 'not quenching a thirst'.
3
Examine al-Razi's Quranic method
He points to verses of affirmation (ithbāt) and negation (nafy). Identify the two categories.
Affirmation: 'To Him ascends...' and 'The Most Merciful established...'; Negation: 'There is nothing like unto Him'.
4
Appreciate the experiential conclusion
Al-Razi says whoever has his experience will have his knowledge. What does this imply about learning?
It suggests a personal realization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل ما يشتمل عليه الكلام من الأدلة فالقرآن والأخبار مشتملة عليه، وما خرج عنها فهو إما مجادلة مذمومة، وإما مشاغبة بالتعلق بمناقضات الفرق، وتطويل بنقل المقالات التي أكثرها توهمات. ويعجبني ما ذكره الإمام الفخر الرازي في كتابه: ( أقسام اللذات ) إذ يقول: لقد تأملت الكتب الكلامية، والمناهج الفلسفية، فما رأيتها تروي غليلاً، ورأيت أقرب الطريق، طريق القرآن، إقرأ في الأثبات: « إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب »، « الرحمن على العرش استوى » وإقرأ في النفي: « ليس كمثله شيء » ومن جرب مثل تجربتي، عرف مثل معرفتي. أهـ. ويعلق الإمام ابن القيم على هذا فيقول: وهذا الذي أشار إليه بحسب ما فتح له من دلالة القرآن بطريق الخبر، وإلا فدلالته البرهانية العقلية التي يشير إليها، ويرشد إليها، فتكون دليلاً سميعاً عقلياً، أمر تميز به القرآن، وصار العالم به من الراسخين في العلم، وهو العلم الذي يطمئن إليه القلب، وتسكن عنده النفس، ويزكو به العقل، وتستنير به البصيرة، وتقوى به الحجة، ولا سبيل لأحد من العالمين، إلى قطع.
And the summary of what the discourse comprises of proofs—the Qur'an and the traditions contain it, and what falls outside them is either blameworthy disputation or wrangling by clinging to the contradictions of sects and prolonging the transmission of statements, most of which are illusions. And I admire what Imam Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī mentioned in his book "Aqsām al-Ladhdhāt": he said: I have contemplated the books of kalām and the philosophical methods, and I did not see them quenching a thirst. And I found the nearest path is the path of the Qur'an. Read in affirmation: "To Him ascends the good word," "The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne." And read in negation: "There is nothing like unto Him." Whoever has an experience like my experience, will know a knowledge like my knowledge. End quote. And Imam Ibn al-Qayyim comments on this, saying: This which he pointed to is according to what was opened to him of the indication of the Qur'an by way of transmission (khabar); otherwise, its demonstrative rational indication which it points to and directs to—so it becomes a transmitted yet rational proof—is a matter by which the Qur'an is distinguished. And the one who is deeply rooted in knowledge becomes knowledgeable of it. And it is the knowledge by which the heart is assured, the soul is tranquil, the intellect is purified, the insight is illuminated, and the argument is strengthened. And no one from the worlds has a way to cut it off.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage starts with a summary that all proofs are in the Quran and traditions; anything else is blameworthy. Then Ghazali quotes Imam al-Razi, who found kalam books unsatisfying and turned to the Quranic method of combining affirmation and negation verses. Ibn al-Qayyim adds that the Quran contains not only textual proofs but also rational demonstrative proofs that appeal to both revelation and reason. This knowledge brings certainty and tranquility.

Key Terms
فخر الرازي (Fakhr al-Rāzī)
كلم طيب (kalim ṭayyib)
أثبات (ithbāt)
نفي (nafy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think rational proofs are separate from Quranic proofs.
Correction: Al-Razi shows the Quran itself provides both affirmation and negation as a rational method.

Checkpoint
What did al-Razi find after studying kalām and philosophy?
A) He found them satisfying.
B) He found them not quenching a thirst.
C) He preferred philosophy.
D) He rejected all knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi's testimony shows that the Quran provides a superior, direct path to knowledge that combines affirmation and negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have books of knowledge in your home—do they truly quench your spiritual thirst, or are they just accumulating? What would it take for you to turn to the Quran as your primary source, as al-Razi did?
?
Al-Razi says 'whoever has an experience like my experience will know a knowledge like my knowledge.' When was the last time you experienced the Quran quenching a thirst in your heart? Are you willing to test this path yourself?
?
The Quran affirms and negates—do you live in that balance, or are you prone to either over-affirming (anthropomorphism) or over-negating (ta'til)? Which error are you more inclined to, and how can you correct it using the verses cited?

Ibn al-Qayyim on the Quran's Demonstrative Rational Proofs
Ibn al-Qayyim comments on al-Razi's testimony, elaborating on the Quran's demonstrative rational proofs and their benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Qayyim's concept that the Quran contains both transmitted and rational demonstrative proofs that bring certainty and tranquility.

Core Idea
The Quran's proofs are both transmitted and rational, giving heart and mind certainty.

Ibn al-Qayyim builds on al-Razi's testimony, explaining that the Quran's proofs are not just textual but also rational in nature. He highlights that this knowledge brings tranquility to the heart and strengthens the argument. This lesson unpacks his profound insight.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn al-Qayyim's comment
He first acknowledges al-Razi's point was about transmitted proofs, then expands.
2
Identify the dual nature
Ibn al-Qayyim says the Quran has 'demonstrative rational indication' that is both transmitted and rational.
It is a 'transmitted yet rational proof'.
3
List the benefits of this knowledge
He enumerates: heart assured, soul tranquil, intellect purified, insight illuminated, argument strengthened.
Each benefit shows the comprehensive effect.
4
Understand the final claim
No one can cut off this knowledge. What does that imply about its certainty?
It is unassailable.

?

The Salaf's Limited Engagement in Kalam
Ghazali describes how the Salaf engaged in kalam only briefly and when necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Salaf engaged in kalam only when necessary, with brevity, and not as a specialized discipline.

Core Idea
The Salaf used kalam briefly and only when needed, not as a profession.

This passage from Imam Ghazali uses a story of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud to illustrate the early Muslim approach to theological debate. It shows that kalam was permissible when required to correct error, but was never a regular practice or discipline. This sets the stage for understanding the later development of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Ibn Mas'ud and Yazid
Start by reading the narrative about the debate on faith. Notice how Ibn Mas'ud initially made a slip and was corrected.
This story is the foundation for the lesson that follows.
2
Understand the point about faith
Reflect on why Ibn Mas'ud said declaring oneself a believer implies certainty of Paradise, and how Yazid corrected him by distinguishing faith from certainty of salvation.
3
Grasp Ghazali's principle
After the story, Ghazali draws a general rule: the Salaf engaged in kalam little, briefly, and only when necessary. Identify the reasons he gives.
The reasons are lack of need due to absence of innovation, and the goal being to silence the opponent.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this principle contrasts with later practices of making kalam a specialized field. Ask yourself: What does this teach us about the proper place of theological debate?
This connects to the broader theme of methodology in Aqeedah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وناظر عبد الله بن مسعود رضي الله عنه يزيد بن عميرة في الإيمان ، فقال عبد الله : لو قلت إني مؤمن لقلت إني في الجنة ، فقال له يزيد بن عميرة : يا صاحب رسول الله هذه زلة منك ، وهل الإيمان إلا أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله والبعث والميزان وتقيم الصلاة والصوم والزكاة ، ولنا ذنوب لو نعلم أنها تغفر لنا لعلمنا أننا من أهل الجنة ، فمن أجل ذلك نقول إنا مؤمنون ولا نقول إنا من أهل الجنة ، فقال ابن مسعود : صدقت والله إنها مني زلة . فينبغي أن يقال كان خوضهم فيه قليلا لا كثيرا وقصيرا لا طويلا ، وعند الحاجة لا بطريق التنصيف والتدريس وإتخاذه صناعة ، فيقال أما قلة خوضهم فيه فإنه كان لقلة الحاجة إذ لم تكن البدعة تظهر في ذلك الزمان وأما القصر فقد كان الغاية إفحام الخصم ، واعترافه وإنكشاف الحق وإزالة الشبهة فلو طال إشكال الخصم أو لجاجة لطال لا محالة إلزامهم
And Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) debated with Yazid ibn Umayrah about faith. Abdullah said: 'If I said I am a believer, I would say I am in Paradise.' Yazid ibn Umayrah said to him: 'O companion of the Messenger of Allah, this is a slip of yours. Is faith anything but that you believe in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, the resurrection, and the scale, and you perform prayer, fasting, and zakat? And we have sins - if we knew that they would be forgiven for us, we would know that we are among the people of Paradise. For that reason we say we are believers, but we do not say we are among the people of Paradise.' Ibn Mas'ud said: 'You have spoken the truth, by Allah; it was a slip of mine.' So it should be said that their engagement in it (kalam) was little, not much, and short, not long, and only when necessary, not in the manner of specialization, teaching, or taking it as a craft. It may be said: as for the paucity of their engagement in it, it was due to the lack of need, since innovation had not appeared in that time. As for brevity, the goal was to silence the opponent, his admission, the unveiling of truth, and the removal of doubt. So if the opponent's objection or obstinacy were prolonged, their refutation would inevitably be prolonged as well.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents a narrative followed by a general principle. It first recounts a debate between Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and Yazid ibn Umayrah on the topic of faith (iman). Ibn Mas'ud initially stated that declaring oneself a believer would imply certainty of Paradise, but Yazid corrected him by explaining that faith is the belief in the articles of faith and performance of obligations, but sins prevent certainty of Paradise. Ibn Mas'ud accepted the correction, calling it a 'slip.' This story illustrates that the Companions engaged in theological discussion when needed. The author then draws a normative conclusion: the practice of the Salaf (early Muslims) regarding kalam was characterized by limited quantity and brevity. They only engaged in such debates when necessary—to refute an innovator or remove a doubt—and not as a regular discipline or profession. The reason for the rarity of such discussions was that bid‘a (innovation) had not yet spread. When they did engage, the purpose was to silence the opponent and clarify truth, not to prolong debate. However, if the opponent persisted, they would also continue their refutation. Thus, the passage establishes that kalam is permissible when needed, but was not a habitual practice of the early community.

Key Terms
كلام (kalam)
بدعة (bid'a)
إفحام الخصم (silencing the opponent)
تنصيف (specialization)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Salaf completely avoided all theological discussion.
Correction: This passage shows they engaged when necessary, but did not make it a regular practice.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Ghazali, under what condition did the Salaf engage in kalam?
A) They engaged in kalam as a regular teaching subject.
B) They engaged only when necessary, such as to refute an innovator.
C) They never engaged in kalam at all.
D) They engaged in kalam to establish a new school of thought.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf used kalam only when forced by necessity, not as a regular discipline.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about faith, is your goal truly to uncover truth, or do you find yourself wanting to win arguments? Examine your heart: are you more concerned with silencing others or being corrected yourself, as Ibn Mas'ud was? What concrete step can you take to prioritize truth over victory?
?
The Salaf engaged in kalam only out of necessity, not as a habit. Think about your own conversations about religion: are they driven by genuine need to clarify truth, or have you made debating a pastime? Would you be willing to stop debating when the need is gone?
?
Ibn Mas'ud admitted his slip publicly and immediately. Recall a time you were wrong in a theological discussion. Did you admit it? If not, what holds you back from acknowledging your mistakes? How can you cultivate humility like the Salaf?

The Salaf's Practice in Kalam Debates
Ghazali describes the Salaf's kalam practice and defends its later systematization via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the early Muslims' limited engagement in theological debate was not a prohibition but a reflection of minimal need, and that preparing arguments for potential debates is permissible analogically.

Core Idea
The Salaf's silence on kalam was due to lack of need, not a ban — preparation is like sharpening weapons before battle.

This passage from Imam al-Ghazali addresses a common question: if the pious predecessors rarely engaged in kalam, why do later scholars systematize it? Ghazali clarifies that their practice was contextual — innovations were rare, so debate was rare. Furthermore, he draws an analogy to fiqh: if it is allowed to compile rare legal cases for future reference, then compiling arguments against potential doubts is similarly justified. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read and observe the description of the Salaf's practice
Notice Ghazali's three-part summary: little, short, only upon need. This sets up the question he will answer.
2
Understand the rationale behind each aspect
Ghazali explains why it was little (no innovations), why short (goal to silence opponent), and why not systematized (same as early fiqh/tafsir/hadith).
3
Analyze the analogy to fiqh and the conclusion
Ghazali argues that if preparation for rare legal cases is allowed, then preparing for rare theological doubts is also allowed — like storing weapons before battle. This justifies the later development of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فينبغي أن يقال كان خوضهم فيه قليلا لا كثيرا وقصيرا لا طويلا ، وعند الحاجة لا بطريق التنصيف والتدريس وإتخاذه صناعة ، فيقال أما قلة خوضهم فيه فإنه كان لقلة الحاجة إذ لم تكن البدعة تظهر في ذلك الزمان وأما القصر فقد كان الغاية إفحام الخصم ، واعترافه وإنكشاف الحق وإزالة الشبهة فلو طال إشكال الخصم أو لجاجة لطال لا محالة إلزامهم، وما كانوا يقدرون قدر الحاجة بميزان ولا مكيال بعد الشروع فيها وأما عدم تصديهم للتدريس والتصنيف فيه، فهكذا كان دأبهم في الفقه والتفسير والحديث أيضاً، فإن جاز تصنيف الفقه ووضع الصور النادرة التي لا تتفق إلا على الندور، أما إدخاراً ليوم وقوعها وإن كان نادراً، أو تشحيذا للخواطر، فنحن أيضا نرتب طرق المجادلة لتوقع وقوع الحاجة بثوان شبهة، أو هيجان مبتدع، او لتشحيذ الخاطر أو لادخار الحجة حتى لا يعجز عنها عند الحاجة على البديهة والإرتجال كمن يعد السلام قبل القتال ليوم القتال. فهذا ما يمكن أن يذكر للفريقين التحقيق في حكم الجدل
So it should be said: their engagement in it was little, not much, and short, not long, and upon need, not by way of specification, teaching, and making it a profession. As for the paucity of their engagement in it, it was due to lack of need, since innovation (bid'a) was not appearing in that time. As for brevity, the goal was to silence the opponent, his admission, the uncovering of truth, and removal of doubt. So if the opponent's confusion or obstinacy was prolonged, their refutation would inevitably be prolonged. They did not measure the extent of need with a scale or measure after embarking on it. As for their not undertaking teaching and classification in it, this was also their practice in jurisprudence (fiqh), exegesis (tafsir), and hadith. If it is permissible to classify jurisprudence and set down rare cases that only occur rarely—either as a storage for the day of their occurrence, even if rare, or as sharpening of minds—then we also arrange the methods of debate (al-mujadala) in anticipation of the occurrence of need due to a doubt, or the eruption of an innovator, or to sharpen the mind, or to store the argument so that one is not unable when need arises spontaneously and extemporaneously, like one who prepares weapons before battle for the day of battle. This is what can be mentioned for the two groups concerning the investigation into the ruling on debate (al-jadal).
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage explains the approach of the early Muslims (Salaf) towards engaging in theological debate (kalam). Ghazali summarizes that their involvement was minimal and brief, only when necessary, and not as a systematic discipline or profession. The reason for scarcity of debate was the absence of innovation (bid'a). Brevity was because the goal was simply to refute the opponent and clarify truth, not to prolong discussion; if needed, they would respond at length. They did not classify debate as a subject of teaching or writing, just as they initially did not formalize fiqh, tafsir, or hadith. However, Ghazali argues that if it is permissible to record rare legal cases for future reference or mental sharpening, then it is similarly permissible to arrange methods of debate in anticipation of future doubts or innovators, storing arguments to be ready when needed—just as one prepares weapons before battle. Thus, the early practice should not be used as an absolute prohibition; rather, it shows that debate for necessity is acceptable, and preparing arguments is analogous to preparing for war.

Key Terms
الخوض
البدعة
الجدل
المجادلة
الإفحام
التشحيذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The early Muslims never engaged in kalam, so it must be a forbidden innovation.
Correction: Ghazali shows they did engage when necessary, but it was rare because innovations were rare. Their practice does not forbid preparing arguments for future needs, just as preparing for rare legal cases is allowed.

Checkpoint
Why did the early Muslims engage in theological debate only rarely and briefly, according to Ghazali?
A) Because they were incapable of profound discussion.
B) Because innovations (bida') had not yet appeared.
C) Because they feared punishment from rulers.
D) Because debate is always forbidden in Islam.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf's minimal engagement in kalam was contextual and not a prohibition; preparing arguments for potential debates is permissible by analogy to preparing for rare legal cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided a theological discussion because you felt you lacked arguments, hiding behind 'the Salaf didn't do it'? What would it take for you to prepare arguments as Ghazali suggests — like storing weapons before battle?
?
When you encounter a doubt about Islam, do you actively sharpen your mind with sound arguments, or do you rely on the hope that doubts will never come? How does the analogy of rare legal cases push you to take responsibility?
?
If you were challenged by an innovator tomorrow, would your refusal to engage be a sincere act of piety or a cover for unpreparedness? What step will you take today to store one argument for that day?

The Conditional Ruling on Jadal
Ghazali rules that jadal's ruling is conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Ghazali rules that theological debate (jadal) is neither absolutely praiseworthy nor blameworthy, but requires detailed consideration based on circumstances and methods.

Core Idea
Jadal is a tool; its ruling depends on how and why it is used.

Imam al-Ghazali presents a balanced view on theological debate. He rejects both extremes—total condemnation and total endorsement—and instead calls for detailed assessment. In this passage, he explains the criteria that determine whether jadal is acceptable or blameworthy, grounding his ruling in the practice of the early Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the opening question and Ghazali's response.
The passage uses a hypothetical question format common in Islamic scholarly works.
2
Identify the ruling
Find where Ghazali states that jadal is not absolutely blameworthy or praiseworthy. Note the phrase 'إطلاق القول بذمه في كل حال أو بحمده في كل حال خطأ'.
This is the core thesis of the passage.
3
Examine the examples of acceptable jadal
List the three examples Ghazali gives: establishing prophethood, divinity, and resurrection. Recognize that these are core beliefs defended with Quranic proofs.
The examples show jadal was used for essential doctrines, not speculative matters.
4
Understand the boundaries
Notice that the early Muslims did not go beyond Quranic proofs in these matters. If someone was not convinced, they turned to sword (defensive jihad) after clarifying the Quran. They avoided stubborn rational speculation.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how Ghazali's ruling distinguishes between necessary defense of faith and excessive rational debate. Ask yourself how this applies to contemporary discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : فما المختار عندك فيه فاعلم أن الحق فيه أن إطلاق القول بذمه في كل حال أو بحمده في كل حال خطأ، بل لا بد فيه من تفصيل محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، وإلى إثبات الألهية مع الأصنام، وإلى إثبات البعث مع منكريه ، ثم ما زادوا في هذه القواعد التي هي أمهات العقائد على أدلة القرآن ، فمن اتبعهم في ذلك قبلوه ، ومن لم يقنع ، وعدلوا إلى السيف والسنان بعد إفشاء أدلة القرآن ، وما ركبوا ظهر اللجاج في وضع المقاييس العقلية ، وترتيب المقدمات واستنباطها وتحرير طرق المجادلة
If you say: 'Then what is your chosen view on it?' Know that the truth in it is that absolute pronouncement of its blame in every case or its praise in every case is an error. Rather, a detailed consideration is necessary: [for] Muhammad (peace be upon him), and for establishing divinity against idols, and for establishing resurrection against its deniers. Then they did not add to these principles, which are the mothers of the creeds, beyond the proofs of the Qur'an. Whoever followed them in that accepted it, and whoever was not convinced, they turned to the sword and spear after making the proofs of the Qur'an clear. And they did not ride the back of stubbornness in laying down intellectual syllogisms (al-maqayis al-'aqliyya), arranging premises, deriving them, and refining the methods of debate (al-mujadala).
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage contains Ghazali's ruling on jadal (theological debate). He begins with a hypothetical question asking for his chosen view. He responds that it is wrong to either absolutely condemn jadal or absolutely praise it; rather, a detailed ruling is required. He then references the practice of the early Muslims: they used jadal for essential proofs such as establishing the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him), the divinity of Allah against idolaters, and the resurrection against its deniers. However, they did not go beyond the Qur'anic proofs in these core doctrines. If someone was not convinced by the Qur'an, they turned to the sword (i.e., defensive jihad) after clarifying the Qur'anic evidence. They did not engage in stubborn intellectual speculation by laying down logical syllogisms (maqayis 'aqliyya) or refining methods of debate as a discipline. Thus, jadal is justified only for defending fundamental beliefs using primarily revealed proofs, not as an independent speculative science.

التفصيل
المقاييس العقلية
طرق المجادلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jadal is always forbidden or always praiseworthy.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that jadal is neither absolutely blameworthy nor praiseworthy; it can be acceptable when used to defend core beliefs with Quranic proofs, but blameworthy when it involves excessive rational speculation beyond revelation.

Checkpoint

A) It is always forbidden.
B) It is always recommended.
C) It is neither absolutely blameworthy nor praiseworthy; it requires detailed consideration.
D) It is permissible only for scholars of kalam.

Key Takeaway
Jadal is a neutral tool; its moral value depends on the intention, method, and scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you discuss Islam with someone who disagrees, do you find yourself more focused on being right or on conveying the truth? Examine your last debate: was there any trace of stubbornness (lajaj) in your heart? What will you do to ensure your intention is pure next time?

Ghazali emphasizes that the early Muslims relied primarily on Quranic proofs for core beliefs. In your own defense of Islam, do you trust the Qur'an enough to present it as the primary evidence, or do you lean on rational arguments out of fear that the Quran will not be enough? What does that reveal about your confidence in Allah's word?
?
The early Muslims turned to the sword only after making the proofs clear. Have you ever given up on someone after a failed debate instead of persisting with patience and clearer evidence? How can you embody the prophetic method of balanced jadal in your relationships?

Preparing Arguments Like Weapons
Ghazali defends arranging dialectical arguments analogously to preparing weapons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's defense of systematizing kalam arguments through the analogy of preparing weapons for battle and the permissibility of classifying rare legal cases.

Core Idea
Theological arguments are weapons; they must be prepared in advance.

Some critics argued that early Muslims did not engage in kalam, so later scholars should not either. Ghazali responds by pointing out that the early Muslims also did not systematically compile fiqh, tafsir, or hadith—yet it became permissible to do so. He extends the same logic to kalam: just as rare legal cases are recorded for future need, arguments are prepared to meet doubts and innovators.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with attention
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the flow: Ghazali first addresses the objection, then gives an analogy from fiqh, then applies it to kalam.
2
Identify the core analogy
Ghazali compares preparing kalam arguments to preparing weapons before battle. Find the exact phrase and reflect on its implications.
The Arabic phrase is 'كمن يعد السلاح قبل القتال ليوم القتال'.
3
Understand the justification
Notice that Ghazali uses the precedent of classifying rare cases in fiqh as a justification. Why is this a strong argument?
It shows that preparation is accepted even in other Islamic sciences.
4
Apply to your own learning
Consider how this lesson applies to your own study of aqidah. Why is it important to have arguments ready before encountering doubts?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقدرون قدر الحاجة بميزان ولا مكيال بعد الشروع فيها. وأما عدم تصديهم للتدريس والتصنيف فيه، فهكذا كان دأبهم في الفقه والتفسير والحديث أيضاً، فإن جاز تصنيف الفقه ووضع الصور النادرة التي لا تتفق إلا على الندور، أما ادخاراً ليوم وقوعها وإن كان نادراً، أو تشحيذاً للخواطر، فنحن أيضاً نرتب طرق المجادلة لتوقع وقوع الحاجة بثوان شبهة، أو هيجان مبتدع، أو لتشحيذ الخاطر، أو لادخار الحجة حتى لا يعجز عنها عند الحاجة على البديهة والارتجال، كمن يعد السلاح قبل القتال ليوم القتال. فهذا ما يمكن أن يذكر للفريقين.
They measure the need by a balance and not by a measure after beginning it. As for their not undertaking teaching and classification in it, such was their practice in fiqh, tafsir, and hadith as well. For if it is permissible to classify fiqh and to set down rare cases that occur only rarely, whether as a provision for the day of their occurrence even if rare, or as a sharpening of minds, then we too arrange the methods of debate in anticipation of the occurrence of a need for a second doubt, or the incitement of an innovator, or to sharpen the mind, or to store up an argument so that one is not incapable of it when needed on the spur of the moment and extemporaneously, like one who prepares weapons before battle for the day of battle. This is what can be mentioned for the two groups.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defends the practice of systematic arrangement of theological arguments. Ghazali begins by acknowledging that the early Muslims did not busy themselves with teaching and authoring works on kalam, but he points out that they also followed the same approach in fiqh, tafsir, and hadith. Yet it was permissible to compile rare legal cases (صور نادرة) for the sake of storing them for when they might occur, even if improbable, or for sharpening the intellect. By analogy, he argues that the kalam scholars similarly organize methods of debate in anticipation of encountering a doubt (شبهة) or the emergence of an innovator, or to sharpen the mind, or to have an argument ready so that one is not caught off guard and forced to improvise. He compares this to one who prepares weapons before battle for the day of battle. Thus, Ghazali responds to the objection that early Muslims avoided kalam by showing that the same principle of preparation applies in other religious sciences.

Correction: The passage justifies systematic preparation for defending creed against specific doubts, not free speculation. The analogy to weapons shows a defensive, not offensive, posture.

Two Types of Forbidden: Essence vs. External Cause
Ghazali classifies forbidden things into two types and subdivides the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between things forbidden by their essence and things forbidden due to external factors, and how predominant cases guide absolute rulings.

Core Idea
A thing is either forbidden by its essence or due to external harm.

Before diving into theological arguments, Ghazali establishes a crucial methodological distinction: some things are intrinsically forbidden, others become forbidden due to harm. This classification helps us understand why absolute rulings (like 'wine is haram') hold despite rare exceptions like necessity or small quantities. In this passage, Ghazali presents the two categories, their subcategories, and a guiding principle for when to differentiate rather than generalize.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the two main categories: haram by essence and haram due to others.
Pay attention to the examples: wine, carrion, selling during option period, eating clay, poison, honey.
2
Understand the first category
Focus on the definition of haram by essence: the cause of prohibition is an intrinsic quality (e.g., intoxication in wine). Note the rule: absolute prohibition, ignoring exceptions like necessity.
Ghazali says 'we pay no attention' to exceptional permissibility.
3
Understand the second category and its division
Examine haram due to others: prohibition arises from external harm. Then see the two subcategories: things harmful in small/large amounts (absolutely haram, like poison) and things harmful only when abundant (absolutely halal based on predominant cases, like honey).
The absolute ruling on clay and honey is based on predominant cases (أغلب الأحوال).
4
Grasp the concluding principle
Read the final sentence: if cases are balanced (both benefit and harm), it is better to differentiate rather than give an absolute ruling. This shows Ghazali's nuanced approach.
This principle will be applied in theological discussions.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding: exceptional permissibility does not negate the absolute rule. Ghazali's framework accounts for this by grounding rulings in predominant cases.
This is a key methodological point for kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلم أولاً أن الشيء قد يحرم لذاته كالخمر والميتة، وأعني بقولي لذاته أن علة تحريمه وصف في ذاته وهو الإسكار والموت. وهذا إذا سئلنا عنه أطلقنا القول بأنه حرام، ولا يلتفت إلى إباحة الميتة عند الإضطرار، وإباحة تجرع الخمر إذا غص الإنسان بلقمة، ولم يجد ما يسيغها سوى الخمر. وإلى ما يحرم لغيره كالبيع أخيك المسلم في وقت الخيار، والبيع وقت النداء، وكأكل الطين، فإنه يحرم لما فيه من الأضرار. وهذا ينقسم إلى ما يضر قليله وكثيره فيطلق القول عليه بأنه حرام كالسم الذي يقتل قليله وكثيره، وإلى ما يضر عند الكثرة فيطلق القول عليه بالإباحة كالعسل، فإن كثيرة يضر بالمحرور، وكأكل الطين. وكان إطلاق التحريم على الطين والخمر، والتحليل على العسل، التفات إلى أغلب الأحوال. فإن تصدى شيء تقابلت فيه الأحوال فالأولى والأبعد عن الإلتباس أن يفصل.
Know, first, that a thing may be forbidden due to its essence, like wine and carrion. By 'due to its essence' I mean that the cause of its prohibition is a description in its essence, which is intoxication and death. So when asked about it, we state absolutely that it is forbidden, and we pay no attention to the permissibility of carrion in necessity, nor to the permissibility of drinking wine when one is choking on a morsel and finds nothing to swallow it except wine. And [there is] that which is forbidden due to something else, like selling to your Muslim brother during the option period, and selling during the call to prayer, and like eating clay, for it is forbidden because of the harm it contains. And this is divided into what is harmful in small and large amounts, so we state absolutely that it is forbidden, like poison, both small and large amounts of which kill; and into what is harmful when abundant, so we state absolutely that it is permissible, like honey, for a large amount harms the feverish, and like eating clay. And the absolute prohibition of clay and wine, and the absolute permission of honey, is on account of the predominant cases. And if something arises whose cases are balanced, the better and further from confusion is to differentiate.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a classification and rule-setting passage. Ghazali begins by distinguishing two types of forbidden things. First, things forbidden due to their essence (حرام لذاته), where the cause of prohibition is an intrinsic quality, such as intoxication in wine or being dead in carrion. For such things, we issue an absolute ruling of prohibition, disregarding exceptional cases where they become permissible (e.g., carrion in necessity, or wine when choking). Second, things forbidden due to something else (حرام لغيره), where the prohibition stems from external factors that cause harm. Examples include selling to a Muslim brother during the option period (when he may change his mind), selling during the call to Friday prayer, and eating clay—all prohibited because of the harm they entail. Ghazali then divides this second category into two subcategories: (a) things that are harmful in both small and large quantities, like poison, so we rule them absolutely forbidden; (b) things that are harmful only in large quantities, like honey for a feverish person or eating clay in large amounts, so we rule them absolutely permissible based on the predominant cases. He concludes with a principle: if a thing presents balanced cases (both benefit and harm), it is better and less confusing to differentiate (by specifying when it is forbidden and when permissible) rather than giving an absolute ruling. This sets the stage for applying this framework to ilm al-kalam.

Key Terms
حرام لذاته
حرام لغيره
الإطلاق
أغلب الأحوال
التفصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the permissibility in exceptional cases (like carrion in necessity) negates the absolute ruling of prohibition.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that the ruling is based on the predominant cases, not rare exceptions. Absolute rulings stand despite occasional permissibility.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is wine considered forbidden by essence (حرام لذاته)?
A) Because it is harmful in large quantities
B) Because its cause of prohibition is an intrinsic quality, intoxication
C) Because it is forbidden due to external reasons
D) Because it is harmful in small and large amounts

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's classification of forbidden things into those forbidden by essence and those forbidden due to external harm, with the principle of basing absolute rulings on predominant cases, provides a methodological foundation for Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever justify a sin by pointing to rare exceptions, like 'I only did it once' or 'It was necessary'? How does Ghazali's principle of predominant cases challenge that excuse?
?
Are there areas in your life where you treat something as absolutely forbidden when it only harms in excess (like social media or food)? How can applying Ghazali's principle of differentiation change your approach?
?
When you judge others' actions, do you consider whether the prohibition is by essence or by external cause, or do you lump them together? What would change if you did?

The First Harm of Kalam: Raising Doubts and Shaking Beliefs
Ghazali enumerates the threefold harm of kalam and its uncertain recovery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali warns that engaging in kalam can initially weaken certainty and why the return to correct belief through evidence is not guaranteed.

Core Idea
Kalam stirs doubts and shakes beliefs before evidence can restore them.

Imam Ghazali begins his critique of kalam by identifying its first and most fundamental harm: it unsettles beliefs. Before any reasoning can rebuild them, the very act of questioning can remove the certainty that faith requires. This lesson examines that warning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let Ghazali's own words set the stage.
Notice the clarity of the three harms: stirring doubts, shaking beliefs, removing certainty.
2
Identify the three specific harms
The passage lists three harms: إثارة الشبهات (raising doubts), تحريك العقائد (shaking beliefs), and إزالتها عن الجزم والتصميم (removing them from certainty and determination).
These are progressive: doubts are stirred, then beliefs are shaken, then certainty is lost.
3
Understand the timing: occurs at the beginning
Ghazali says this harm happens in the beginning (في الإبتداء) of engaging in kalam. It is an immediate effect.
This is important: the harm is not hypothetical but a real initial consequence.
4
Analyze the uncertainty of recovery
The return to correct belief through evidence (الدليل) is uncertain and varies among people. Some may not recover their certainty.
This warns that the process of rational argument does not guarantee a successful return to faith.
5
Reflect on the implication for correct belief
The harm is specifically to correct belief (الإعتقاد الحق). Ghazali is cautioning those who already have sound belief.
This lesson sets the stage for understanding why kalam can be dangerous for the laity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما مضرته فإثارة الشبهات ، وتحريك العقائد ، وإزالتها عن الجزم والتصميم. فذلك مما يحصل في الإبتداء. ورجوعها بالدليل مشكوك فيه ويختلف فيه الأشخاص ، فهذا ضرره في الإعتقاد الحق.
As for its harm: it is the stirring up of doubts, the shaking of beliefs, and removing them from certainty and determination. That occurs in the beginning. And their return through evidence is uncertain and differs among people. So this is its harm with respect to correct belief.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage explains the first type of harm caused by ilm al-kalam. Ghazali states that the harm lies in three things: (1) raising doubts (إثارة الشبهات), (2) disturbing firmly held beliefs (تحريك العقائد), and (3) removing those beliefs from the state of certainty (جزم) and determination (تصميم). He adds that this happens at the beginning (في الإبتداء) when one first engages in theological debate or study. Furthermore, even if one attempts to return to correct belief through rational evidence (الدليل), that return is uncertain and varies from person to person—some may recover, others may not. Thus, the harm is specifically to correct belief (الإعتقاد الحق). This warning is important: the process of seeking evidence can shake a person's faith before it is rebuilt, and success is not guaranteed.

Key Terms

عقائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Engaging in kalam always strengthens faith and removes doubts.
Correction: Ghazali shows that initially kalam weakens certainty and recovery through evidence is not guaranteed.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about returning to correct belief through evidence after kalam?
A) It is certain
B) It is uncertain and differs among people
C) It is impossible
D) It is easy for everyone

Key Takeaway
The first harm of kalam is that it raises doubts and weakens certainty, and the recovery through evidence is not guaranteed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you engaged in a discussion that stirred doubts in your heart about something you firmly believed. What did that experience do to your certainty? Are you still certain now? What would Ghazali say about that experience?
?
This lesson warns that the recovery from doubts through evidence is uncertain. If you found yourself in a state of doubt after theological study, do you have a plan to return to certainty? What if the evidence did not convince you? Would you be able to live with that uncertainty?
?
Ghazali says the harm happens at the beginning, before any recovery. When you start learning a new theological argument, do you ever pause to consider that your faith might be shaken before it is rebuilt? How do you protect your heart from the initial harm of doubt?
