Teaching Aqidah to Children When Innovation Spreads
Ghazali gives the ruling on teaching children aqidah when innovation is prevalent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific condition under which Imam al-Ghazali permits teaching theological beliefs to children, and the purpose behind this teaching.

Core Idea
Protect children from innovation with concise 'aqidah.

Imam al-Ghazali, in his Qawaid al-Aqaid, addresses a practical concern: when heretical innovations become widespread, children are vulnerable. He provides a ruling that balances the need to protect their faith without burdening them with complex theology. In this passage, he explains when it is permissible to teach children 'aqidah and what content is appropriate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure starting with 'if'.
2
Identify the condition
Find the two conditions that must be met for the ruling to apply: innovation widespread and fear of deception.
The condition is key to understanding the permissibility.
3
Understand the purpose
Why does Ghazali mention al-Risala al-Qudsiyya? The teaching is meant to repel the influence of innovators' arguments.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice that Ghazali emphasizes brevity and conciseness. He is not advocating for extensive theological training for children.
This protects children from confusion.
5
Apply the principle
Consider how this ruling guides educators in times of religious confusion. When would you apply this in your own context?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كانت البدعة شائعة وكان يخاف على الصبيان أن يخدعوا ، فلا بأس أن يعلموا القدر الذي أودعناه كتاب الرسالة القدسية ليكون ذلك سببا لدفع تأثير مجادلات المبتدعة إن وقعت إليهم. وهذا مقدار مختصر ، وقد أودعناه هذا الكتاب لاختصاره.
If innovation is widespread and there is fear that the children may be deceived, there is no harm in teaching them the amount that we have deposited in the book al-Risala al-Qudsiyya, so that it may be a cause for repelling the influence of the disputations of the innovators should they encounter them. And this is a concise amount, and we have deposited it in this book for its brevity.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling and a pedagogical guideline. Ghazali begins by stating a conditional situation: when innovation (bid'a) is widespread and there is a real fear that children may be deceived by it, then it becomes permissible to teach them a specific amount of 'aqidah. The amount is precisely what he has included in his work al-Risala al-Qudsiyya. The purpose of this teaching is to protect the children; it is a defensive measure so that if they ever encounter disputations from the innovators, the knowledge they have will help them repel those arguments. Ghazali then notes that this book (meaning the present work, Qawaid al-Aqaid) contains that concise amount, because he designed it to be brief and accessible. The implication is that one should not burden children with lengthy theological debates, but only with what is necessary for protection.

Key Terms
بدعة
الرسالة القدسية
مجادلات المبتدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think children should never be taught kalam.
Correction: Ghazali permits it when necessary to protect them from innovation.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, when is it permissible to teach children the content of al-Risala al-Qudsiyya?
A) Always, as part of basic Islamic education
B) Only when innovation is widespread and children are at risk of deception
C) Only after they have reached puberty
D) Only if they ask questions about theology

Key Takeaway
Ghazali permits teaching concise aqidah to children only when innovation is widespread and they are at risk of deception, as a protective measure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you knew your own child was at risk of being deceived by innovation, what specific 'aqidah would you prioritize teaching them, and how would you ensure it was concise? Are you actively guarding those under your care right now?
?
This passage assumes innovation is widespread. Do you recognize the innovative ideas prevalent in your community? How will you equip yourself to distinguish truth from falsehood so you can protect your own heart first?
?
Ghazali says to teach only what is necessary for protection. Are you prone to burdening yourself or others with excessive theological details that may confuse rather than clarify? What one step can you take this week to simplify your understanding of core 'aqidah?

Advancing to al-Iqtisad for the Intelligent Student
Ghazali describes when to advance from the concise book to al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's measured pedagogical approach for moving a student from a concise creedal text to a more detailed rational work, and the ethical boundaries for such advancement.

Core Idea
Advance a student only when doubt arises, and stop when convinced.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali provides a practical guide for teachers of theology. He identifies the precise moment when a concise creedal summary is no longer sufficient and a more detailed rational work, al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad, becomes appropriate. Importantly, he also sets limits: once the student is convinced, further discussion should cease, and if the student remains unconvinced, the teacher must act with gentleness and ultimately rely on divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then'.
The passage uses medical metaphors: malady, disease, physician.
2
Identify the condition for advancement
Find what triggers the advancement: intelligence that leads to a question or doubt. Understand that doubt is not inherently negative here; it signals readiness for deeper study.
3
Notice the scope of al-Iqtisad
Ghazali emphasizes that al-Iqtisad remains within the fundamentals of beliefs (qawa'id al-'aqa'id) and does not delve into mutakallimun debates. This reassurance is crucial.
4
Explore the two outcomes
After presenting al-Iqtisad, two possibilities: the student is convinced (stop) or not convinced (chronic disease). Ghazali advises a gentle approach and reliance on Allah.
5
Reflect on the teacher's role
Ghazali portrays the teacher as a physician who must be gentle and patient, not force convictions. Ultimate guidance comes from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان فيه ذكاء ، وتنبه بذكائه الموضع سؤال ، أو ثارت في نفسه شبهة فقد بدت العلة المحذورة وظهر الداء فلا بأس أن يرقى منه إلى القدر الذي ذكرناه في كتاب الإقتصاد في الإعتقاد وهو قدر خمسين ورقة ، وليس فيه خروج عن النظر في قواعد العقائد إلى غير ذلك من مباحث المتكلمين. فإن أقنعه ذلك كفّ عنه ، وإن لم يقنعه ذلك فقد صارت العلة مزمنة والداء غالبا والمرض ساريا ، فليتلطف به الطبيب بقدر إمكانه ، وينتظر قضاء الله تعالى فيه ، إلى أن ينكشف له الحق بتنبيه من الله سبحانه ، أو يستمر على الشك والشبهة إلى ما قدر له.
If there is intelligence in him, and by his intelligence he perceives the place of a question, or a doubt arises in his soul, then the feared malady has appeared and the disease has become manifest. There is no harm in advancing him from that [concise level] to the amount that we mentioned in the book al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad, which is about fifty folios, and there is no departure in it from considering the fundamentals of beliefs to other topics of the mutakallimun. If that convinces him, then stop [discussing further] with him. And if that does not convince him, then the malady has become chronic, the disease predominant, and the illness spreading. Then let the physician be gentle with him as much as possible, and await the decree of Allah, until the truth is disclosed to him by an inspiration from Allah, or he continues in doubt and uncertainty until what is decreed for him.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a pedagogical progression. Ghazali first identifies a scenario: a student who already has intelligence and, through that intelligence, notices a point of questioning or a doubt arises in his mind. This indicates that the concise level (from lesson 1) is no longer sufficient; the 'malady' of doubt has appeared. At this point, Ghazali says it is permissible to move him to the next level: the book al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad, which is about fifty folios in length. Ghazali assures that this book, although more detailed, still remains within the domain of the fundamentals of beliefs (qawa'id al-'aqa'id) and does not venture into the extraneous topics of the mutakallimun. The purpose is to provide rational arguments to remove the doubt. After presenting this deeper material, Ghazali gives two outcomes: if the student becomes convinced, then stop further discussion (do not go beyond what is needed). If he is not convinced, then the situation becomes more serious: the malady is chronic and disease is spreading. Then the teacher should act like a physician, gently treating the student as much as possible, and ultimately relying on Allah's decree, hoping for divine inspiration to guide the student or accepting that the student may remain in doubt by divine decree. The key point is the measured approach: only advance when necessary, and do not push beyond recovery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that once doubts arise, one must engage in extensive kalam debates without limits.
Correction: Ghazali sets clear boundaries: stop if convinced, and if not, be gentle and rely on Allah, not forceful argumentation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ghazali teaches that a teacher should advance a student to deeper rational theology only when doubt arises, and must stop once the student is convinced, acting with gentleness and reliance on Allah otherwise.

?

The Two Divisions of Kalam: Fundamentals vs. Extraneous Topics
Ghazali divides kalam into proper aqidah fundamentals and extraneous debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between the fundamental topics of aqidah and the extraneous debates, and why he warns against delving into the latter.

Core Idea
Not all kalam discussion is beneficial; some topics stray from the core of faith.

In this passage from al-Iqtisad, Imam al-Ghazali sets out a crucial methodological point. He divides the subjects discussed in works of kalam (speculative theology) into two categories: those that belong to the core fundamentals of beliefs (qawa'id al-'aqa'id), and those that are extraneous to this core. This lesson will examine the extraneous topics he lists, which he considers unhelpful or even harmful for the average believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own classification come through.
Notice the phrase 'فذلك قسمان' – the division is explicitly stated.
2
Identify the two divisions
Ghazali divides knowledge into two: that which lies within the fundamentals of beliefs, and that which lies outside. Reflect on what 'beneficial' means here.
3
Examine the examples of extraneous topics
Look at the terms: al-i'timadat, al-akwan, al-idrakat, and the discussion on ru'ya and its opposite man'. These are the topics Ghazali considers outside the scope of necessary beliefs.
The exact interpretation of these terms is debated; here they serve as examples of speculative theology.
4
Understand the warning
Ghazali implies that these topics should not preoccupy the believer. They are not part of the core creed that this book (al-Iqtisad) aims to establish.
This reflects Ghazali's concern with practical faith over abstract speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقدر الذي يحويه ذلك الكتاب وجنسه من المصنفات هو الذي يرجى نفعه. فذلك قسمان: فأما الخارج عن قواعد العقائد فكالبحث عن الإعتمادات وعن الأكوان وعن الإدراكات وعن الخوض في الرؤية: هل لها ضد يسمى المنع؟
The amount that this book contains, and the genre of such works, is that from which benefit is hoped. That is of two divisions: As for that which lies outside the fundamentals of beliefs, such as research into al-i'timadat, al-akwan, al-idrakat, and delving into the vision [of Allah]: does it have an opposite called man'?
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a classification. Ghazali first asserts that the amount of knowledge contained in al-Iqtisad (and works of its type) is what is hoped to be beneficial. Then he divides knowledge into two divisions: (1) that which is within the fundamentals of beliefs (qawa'id al-'aqa'id) – this is what he recommends – and (2) that which is outside the fundamentals. He gives examples of the extraneous topics: discussions about al-i'timadat (perhaps 'reliance' or 'intentions' in the sense of theological causation), al-akwan (incorporeal entities or 'coming into being'?), al-idrakat (perceptions), and debating the vision of Allah (ru'ya) and whether it has an opposite called man' (prevention). These topics are considered beyond the necessary fundamentals and are thus dangerous or unhelpful for the average believer. By giving these examples, Ghazali warns against straying into speculative theology that does not directly serve the basic creed.

Key Terms
قواعد العقائد
الإعتمادات
الأكوان
الإدراكات
الرؤية
المنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All kalam discussions are equally valid and beneficial for faith.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes between beneficial fundamentals and harmful extraneous debates; not all kalam topics serve the core creed.

Checkpoint

A) Research into al-i'timadat
B) Research into al-akwan
C) Research into the proof of the existence of God
D) Delving into the vision of Allah and its opposite

Key Takeaway
Ghazali divides kalam into fundamental and extraneous topics, cautioning against delving into debates that do not directly support core creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islamic theology, do you ever find yourself drawn into debates about obscure topics that have no impact on your daily worship or character? How will you use this lesson to guard your time and focus?
?
Ghazali warns that even discussing the vision of Allah (ru'ya) can become extraneous if it strays into speculative opposites. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that are based on theological reasoning rather than clear revelation? Will you commit to prioritizing the fundamental texts over human speculation?
?
If you were to examine your recent study sessions, what percentage of your learning was spent on the fundamentals of 'aqidah versus debating secondary issues? What would it cost you to shift that balance? Make a concrete intention to focus on what strengthens your certainty and practice.

The Physician's Analogy: Kalam as Medicine for Doubt
Ghazali gives a practical guideline using a physician analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's analogy of the physician to explain when kalam (theological debate) is appropriate and when it is harmful.

Core Idea
Kalam is a medicine, not a daily diet.

Imam al-Ghazali uses a powerful medical analogy to guide when theological discussion is beneficial or harmful. Just as a physician prescribes medicine only to the sick, kalam is a cure for doubt, not a routine practice for the healthy. This passage helps us balance between engaging with doubt and protecting sound faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the original Arabic and its literal translation to grasp Ghazali's flow. Notice the two distinct scenarios he presents.
2
Identify the two types of people
The passage describes two cases: one who is obstinate and rejects faith, and one who shows signs of guidance. Note how Ghazali advises different responses.
The first type is to be avoided, the second to be treated gently.
3
Understand the physician analogy
Reflect on the core analogy: medicines (kalam) are for the sick (those with doubts). The healthy (sound fitra) should be protected from unnecessary debate.
This clarifies that kalam is a necessity, not a virtue in itself.
4
Apply the principle to today
Consider how this principle applies in contexts where theological debates are common. How can we distinguish between beneficial and harmful discussion?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وجد اللجاج والجدل غالبا عليه وعلى طبعه، لم تجادله، وطهرنا وجه الأرض منه، إن كان مجانباً من أصل الإيمان. وإن تفرسنا بالقرائن محايل الرشد والقبول لجوزنا به من الكلام الظاهر الى تدقيق الأدلة عالجنا بما قدرنا عليه ذلك، وداويناه بالجدال المسدد، والبراهين الجلية. وليس المقصود من كلامنا هذا فتح الباب في الكلام مع الكافة، فإن الأدوية تستعمل في حق المرض وهم الأقلون، وما يعالج به المريض بحكم الضرورة يجب عليه أن يوقى عنه الصحيح، والفطرة الصحيحة الاصلية تعد لقبول الإيمان دون المجادلة، وتحرير حقائق الأدلة، وليس الضرر في استعمال الأصحاء بأقل من الضرر في إهمال المداواة مع المرضى، فليوضع كل شيء في محله.
If obstinacy and argumentativeness are found to be predominant over him and his nature, do not argue with him, and we would purify the face of the earth from him, if he is turning away from the foundation of faith. And if we perceive through indications signs of guidance and acceptance, we might elevate him from apparent speech to the precision of proofs, we treat him as far as we can, and we cure him with right-guided debate and clear proofs. And the intent of our speech here is not to open the door to kalam with everyone, for medicines are used only in the case of the sick, and they are the minority. And that which is used to treat the patient out of necessity must be prevented from the healthy. And the sound, original disposition is prepared for acceptance of faith without disputation and without verifying the realities of proofs. And the harm in using [medicine] on the healthy is not less than the harm in neglecting treatment for the sick. So let everything be placed in its proper place.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a practical guideline and an analogy. Ghazali first describes how to handle a person who is obstinate and argumentative by nature: do not engage in debate with him, and if he is turning away from the very foundation of faith (i.e., rejecting Islam altogether), then it is better to avoid him altogether ('purify the face of the earth from him' – a strong expression meaning to distance oneself). However, if there are signs (qara'in) of guidance and acceptance, then one may gradually elevate him from simple apparent speech to precise proofs, treating him with right-guided debate and clear evidences. Then Ghazali emphasizes the general principle: this permission to use kalam is not for everyone. He uses the analogy of a physician: medicines are only for the sick, who are the minority. The medicine used for the patient should be kept away from the healthy. The healthy, sound disposition (fitra) is naturally inclined to accept faith without the need for disputation or detailed verification of proofs. Harming the healthy with such 'medicine' is as harmful as neglecting the sick. Therefore, everything must be placed in its proper context. The key takeaway is that kalam is a cure for doubt, not a daily nourishment for the faithful.

Key Terms

جدل
طباع
جدل مسدد
براهين جلية
فطرة صحيحة
محايل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali completely forbids kalam (theological debate) for everyone.
Correction: Ghazali actually permits kalam as a necessary medicine for those with doubts, but warns against using it on healthy believers who do not need it.

Checkpoint

B) Avoid arguing with him and distance oneself from him

Key Takeaway
Kalam is a medicine for doubt, prescribed only for the sick, not for the healthy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Types of Harmful Theological Speculation
Ghazali classifies two categories of extraneous theological inquiry and refutes the mind-sharpening justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of two types of theological inquiry that go beyond basic creed and his refutation of the claim that such speculation is beneficial for sharpening the mind.

Core Idea
Not all theological discussion is beneficial; some are misleading and even harmful.

In this passage, Ghazali defines the proper scope of his book and warns against two types of theological discussions that stray from the foundations of faith. He then addresses a potential objection that such discussions sharpen the mind, comparing it to the absurd claim that playing chess is religious.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a classification followed by a refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two divisions
Find where Ghazali mentions 'the first division' (أحدهما) and 'the second division' (القسم الثاني). Note the specific topics he lists for each.
The first division includes research into dependencies, atoms, perceptions, and vision of God.
3
Understand why each is harmful
For the first, Ghazali calls it 'misleading nonsense' (الترهات المضلات). For the second, he says excessive elaboration increases obscurity and does not help the unconvinced.
He is not forbidding all theological discussion, only that which goes beyond the foundations.
4
Analyze the refutation
Ghazali presents an objection: such research sharpens the mind, which is useful for religion. He refutes it by comparing it to playing chess, which also sharpens the mind but is not religious.
The analogy shows that the end does not justify the means if the means are harmful or irrelevant.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this principle applies to contemporary Islamic discourse. What topics are essential, and what might be considered 'misleading nonsense'?
Ghazali emphasizes focusing on core beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقدر الذي يحويه ذلك الكتاب وجنسه من المصنفات هو الذي يرجى منه فقسمان: فأما الخارج ( أحدهما ) بحث عن غير قواعد العقائد، كالبحث عن الإعتمادات وعن الأكوان، وعن الإدراكات، وعن الخوض في الرؤية: هل لها ضد يسمى المنع أو ما لا العمى، وإن كان فذلك واحد هو منع عن جميع يرى، أو ثبت لكل مرئي يمكن رؤيته منع بحسب عدده إلى غير ذلك من الترهات المضلات. والقسم الثاني: زيادة تقرير لتلك الأدلة في غير تلك القواعد، وزيادة أسئلة وأجوبة، وذلك أيضاً إستقصاء لا يزيد الاضلالاً وجهلا في حق من لم يقنعه ذلك القدر، فرب كلام يزيده الأطناب والتقرير غموضا. ولو قال قائل: البحث عن حكم الإدراكات والإعتمادات فيه فائدة تشحيذ الخواطر، والخاطر آلة الدين كالسيف آلة الجهاد، فلا بأس بتشحيذه، كان كقوله لعب الشطرنج يشحذ الخاطر، فهو من الدين أيضاً، وذلك هوس.
The scope that this book and its kind of works contain is that from which benefit is hoped. So [it is] two divisions: As for the external (one of them), it is research into other than the foundations of creed, such as research into dependencies and atoms, and into perceptions, and delving into the vision [of God]: whether it has an opposite called prevention or what is not blindness, and if that is one thing, it is prevention from all that is seen, or for every visible that can be seen, a prevention is established according to its number, and so on from misleading nonsense. And the second division: the increase of elaboration of those proofs beyond those foundations, and the increase of questions and answers, and that too is an exhaustive treatment that increases nothing but misguidance and ignorance for one whom that amount did not convince. For many a speech increases obscurity through prolixity and elaboration. And if someone were to say: 'Research into the ruling of perceptions and dependencies contains the benefit of sharpening the minds, and the mind is the instrument of religion just as the sword is the instrument of jihad, so there is no harm in sharpening it,' it would be like his saying 'Playing chess sharpens the mind, so it too is from religion' - and that is nonsense.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a classification-and-refutation unit. Ghazali first defines the scope of his book and then divides theological discussions that go beyond basic creed into two categories. The first category is research that strays from the foundations of creed, such as debates about dependencies (i‘timādāt), atoms (akwān), perceptions (idrākāt), and the nature of vision of God (ru’ya) - whether it has an opposite called 'prevention' (man‘) or 'blindness' (‛amā), and further absurdities like assigning a separate prevention for each visible object. He dismisses this as 'misleading nonsense' (turahāt mudillāt). The second category is expanding the proofs and adding questions and answers beyond what is necessary; this also does not help the one who is not satisfied with the basic amount, and excessive elaboration can actually increase obscurity. Ghazali then refutes an objection: someone might argue that such research sharpens the mind, which is the instrument of religion, just as a sword is sharpened for jihad. Ghazali responds by comparing this to the claim that playing chess sharpens the mind, so it too would be religious - which is absurd (hawas). Thus, he rejects the idea that intellectual exercise justifies delving into harmful theological speculation.

الأكوان
الإدراكات
الرؤية

الترهات المضلات
الأطناب
تشحيذ الخواطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any theological discussion that sharpens the mind is beneficial and permissible.
Correction: Ghazali argues that only discussions reinforcing the fundamentals of faith are permissible; discussions straying from creed are misleading nonsense, and the mind-sharpening justification is invalid.

Checkpoint

B) Playing chess
C) Studying medicine

Key Takeaway
Ghazali warns against two types of theological inquiry beyond the basics—straying into irrelevant details and excessive elaboration—and rejects the mind-sharpening justification as absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What motivates that pull—curiosity, pride, or a genuine need for understanding? Commit to redirecting that energy toward learning that increases certainty in the fundamentals.
?
Ghazali compares the mind to a sword. How are you currently using your mind? Is it primarily in service of religious practice (like the sword in jihad) or do you derive pleasure from 'sharpening' it on theological puzzles for their own sake? Be honest: which activity occupies more of your time and energy?
?
Consider a recent religious discussion you prolonged with extra questions or details. Did it bring clarity to the listener, or did it create confusion? What does that reveal about your intention—were you seeking truth or seeking to display knowledge? Repent and resolve to speak only what benefits the listener's faith.

Blameworthy Kalam and the Chess Analogy
Ghazali defines blameworthy kalam and refutes the chess analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of blameworthy kalam as excessive elaboration and his refutation of the claim that such kalam is beneficial because it sharpens the mind.

Core Idea
Not every mental sharpening is religiously praiseworthy; kalam must be rooted in sound principles.

This lesson focuses on the first part of Ghazali's discussion: he identifies a second type of blameworthy kalam—excessive elaboration of proofs beyond proper limits. He then anticipates an objection: that such research sharpens the intellect, like a sword is sharpened for jihad. Ghazali refutes this by comparing it to playing chess, which also sharpens the mind but is not religiously beneficial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to Ghazali's description of the second division and his use of the chess analogy.
The chess analogy is a key refutation tool.
2
Identify the two parts
Notice that the passage has two main parts: the definition of blameworthy kalam (excessive elaboration) and the refutation of the objection that it sharpens the mind.
3
Analyze the refutation
How does Ghazali refute the objection? He says the argument that sharpening the mind is beneficial is like saying playing chess is beneficial—both are nonsense unless rooted in religious benefit.
Consider why Ghazali chooses chess as an example.
4
Summarize the lesson
Write a one-sentence summary of what makes kalam blameworthy according to this passage, and why the chess analogy is used.
This will reinforce the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثاني : زيادة تقرير لتلك الأدلة في غير تلك القواعد ، وزيادة أسئلة وأجوبة ، وذلك أيضاً إستقصاء لا يزيد الاضلالاً وجهلا في حق من لم يقنعه ذلك القدر ، فرب كلام يزيده الأطناب والتقرير غموضا ولو قال قائل : البحث عن حكم الإدراكات والإعتمادات فيه فائدة تشحيذ الخواطر ، والخاطر آلة الدين كالسيف آلة الجهاد ، فلا بأس بتشحيذه ، كان كقوله لعب الشطرنج يشحذ الخاطر ، فهو من الدين أيضاً ، وذلك هوس ، فإن وهم غير الإدراكات إطلاقات أيضاً ، في ثبوتها ونفيها ومذهب أهل السنة : الإدراكات كلها من فعل الله سبحانه وأنه ليس شيء منها فعلا للإنسان ولا كسبا له فإن قالت المعتزلة : العمى والصمم مانعان له عن أن يكون مدركا ، قيل : ما معنى منعهما عن كونه مدركا هل هو منع عن نفسه ، أو عن معنى سواه ، ولا يجوز أن يكون منعا عن نفسه ، فوجب أن يكون المنع إنما وقع عن معنى سواه ، وهو إدراك إذ لا يجوز أن يكون المنع منعا لا عن شيء . الخاطر يتشحذ بسائر علوم الشرع ، ولا يخاف فيها مضرة ، فقد عرفت بهذا القدر : المذموم والقدر المحمود من الكلام ، والحال التي يذم فيها ، والحال التي يحمد فيها ، والشخص الذي ينتفع به، والشخص الذي لا ينتفع به
The second division: excessive elaboration of those proofs outside of those principles, and an increase of questions and answers. That also is an exhaustive treatment that only increases misguidance and ignorance for one whom that amount did not convince, for sometimes speech increases in obscurity by prolixity and elaboration. And if someone were to say: 'Research into the rule of perceptions and attestations contains the benefit of sharpening the thoughts, and the thought (khatir) is the instrument of religion just as the sword is the instrument of jihad, so there is no harm in sharpening it'—that is like his saying: 'Playing chess sharpens the thought, so it is also from religion,' and that is nonsense. For [the claim that] other than perceptions are also conventions regarding their affirmation and negation, and the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna: all perceptions are from the act of Allah the Exalted, and that none of them is an act of man nor an acquisition (kasb) for him. For if the Mu'tazila say: 'Blindness and deafness are two preventers preventing Him from being a perceiver,' it is said: 'What is the meaning of their preventing Him from being a perceiver? Is it a prevention from His essence, or from something other than it? It is not permissible that it be a prevention from His essence, so it must be that the prevention only occurred from something other than it, and that is perception, since it is not permissible that the prevention be a prevention from nothing.' The thought is sharpened by the rest of the sciences of the Shari'a, and no harm is feared in them. So you have known by this amount: the blameworthy and the praiseworthy amount of kalam, and the circumstance in which it is blamed, and the circumstance in which it is praised, and the person who benefits from it, and the person who does not benefit from it.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. Ghazali begins by describing the second division of blameworthy kalam: excessive elaboration of proofs and questions beyond proper principles, which only increases confusion and ignorance for those not satisfied with basic arguments. He then addresses an objection: someone might argue that researching the exact nature of perceptions (like whether blindness prevents perception) sharpens the mind, which is beneficial for religion. Ghazali refutes this by comparing it to playing chess—both may sharpen the intellect, but that does not make them religious acts; it is nonsense. He then presents the Sunni doctrine that all perceptions are directly from Allah and are not human acts or acquisitions. This leads into a specific debate with the Mu'tazila: they claim that blindness and deafness are preventers that prevent Allah from perceiving. Ghazali argues that if they are preventers, they must either prevent His essence (impossible) or prevent something else—namely, perception. Thus the prevention is not from the essence but from a separate attribute. He concludes that the mind is better sharpened by other Shari'a sciences without harm. Finally, he summarizes that the reader now knows the distinction between blameworthy and praiseworthy kalam, the contexts for each, and who benefits or not.

Key Terms
الكلام المذموم
التقرير
الأطناب
التشحيذ
الخاطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any intellectual exercise that sharpens the mind is religiously praiseworthy.
Correction: Ghazali shows that sharpening the mind is not inherently praiseworthy; the activity must be within beneficial Shari'a sciences.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ghazali use to refute the claim that researching perceptions is beneficial for sharpening the mind?
A) Playing chess
B) Training a horse
C) Eating food
D) Using a sword

Key Takeaway
Blameworthy kalam is characterized by excessive elaboration that confuses rather than clarifies; sharpening the mind is not a valid justification if the content is not rooted in sound doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself drawn to theological debates not to deepen your faith, but to prove your intellectual sharpness? How does Ghazali's chess analogy expose the emptiness of that pursuit?
?
When was the last time you justified an activity by saying 'it's beneficial' without asking whether it actually draws you closer to Allah? What will you change after reading this lesson?
?
You have a limited amount of time and energy. Are you spending it on studies that clarify your faith, or on intellectual pursuits that only 'sharpen your mind' while leading to more confusion and misguidance? Commit to a specific change today.

Sunni Doctrine on Perceptions and Debate with Mu'tazila
Ghazali presents Sunni doctrine on perceptions and refutes Mu'tazila claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that all perceptions are from Allah and not human acts, and Ghazali's refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that blindness and deafness prevent Allah from perceiving.

Core Idea
All perceptions are acts of Allah alone; nothing in creation prevents His perception.

This lesson picks up where the previous left off. After refuting the chess analogy, Ghazali turns to the Sunni position on perceptions: they are all from Allah and not acquired by humans. He then engages with a Mu'tazila objection about blindness and deafness as preventers of perception, arguing that such preveners cannot be from Allah's essence but must be separate attributes. Finally, he concludes that the mind is better sharpened by other Shari'a sciences, and summarizes the criteria for blameworthy and praiseworthy kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It contains the Sunni doctrine, the Mu'tazila objection, and Ghazali's refutation.
The passage ends with a summary of the entire section.
2
Understand the Sunni doctrine
Identify the Sunni position: all perceptions are from Allah, not human acts or acquisitions.
This is a key tenet of Sunni theology.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila objection and refutation
The Mu'tazila claim that blindness and deafness prevent Allah from perceiving. Ghazali asks: is the prevention from His essence or from something else? He argues it cannot be from His essence, so it must be from something else (perception itself), and thus the preventer is not a separate entity.
This is a subtle logical argument.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Notice how Ghazali wraps up: he reiterates that the mind is sharpened by other Shari'a sciences without harm, and he summarizes the distinctions.
This conclusion ties together the whole discussion.

Kalam as a Communal Duty and Its Selective Teaching
Ghazali rules that kalam is a communal duty but not to be taught generally, using a medicine analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers kalam a communal duty but restricts its teaching to those in need, using the analogy of medicine versus food.

Core Idea
Kalam is like medicine: essential for the afflicted, but not for everyone.

Ghazali addresses a pressing question: if theology (kalam) is needed to refute heretics, should it be taught widely? In this passage, he affirms its necessity as a communal obligation but warns against universal instruction, comparing kalam to medicine and fiqh to food. This analogy clarifies the delicate balance between protecting faith and causing confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali structures his argument as a response to an imagined objector. This is a common pattern in his works.
Pay attention to the shift from the hypothetical question to the ruling.
2
Identify the analogy
Find the food/medicine analogy. What is the key difference between the two? How does it apply to kalam and fiqh? Consider the audience: everyone needs food, but only the sick need medicine.
The analogy is the core teaching of this passage.
3
Reflect on the ruling
Consider why Ghazali distinguishes between the time of the Companions and his own time. What does this say about the context-dependent nature of theological study? Why is it important to limit teaching kalam to those capable?
Reflect on the danger of teaching kalam to all without discernment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت مهما اعترفت بالحاجة إليه في دفع المبتدعة ، والآن قد ثارت البدع وعمت البلوى وأرهقت الحاجة ، فلا بد أن يصير القيام بهذا العلم من فروض الكفايات ، كالقيام بحراسة الأموال ، وسائر الحقوق كالقضاء والولاية وغيرهما ، وما لم يشتغل العلماء بنشر ذلك ، والتدريس فيه والبحث عنه لا يدوم ، ولو ترك بالكلية لا ندرس ، وليس في مجرد الطباع كفاية لحل شبه المبتدعة ما لم يتعلم ، أن يكون التدريس فيه والبحث عنه أيضاً من فروض الكفايات ، بخلاف زمن الصحابة رضي عنهم ، فإن الحاجة ما كانت ماسة اليه فاعلم أن الحق أنه لا بد في كل بلد من قائم بهذا العلم ، مستقل بدفع شبه المبتدعة التي ثارت في تلك البلدة ، وذلك يدوم بالتعليم ، ولكن ليس من الصواب تدريسه على العموم كتدريس الفقه والتفسير ، فإن هذا مثل الدواء ، والفقه مثل الغذاء
For if you say: 'Whenever you acknowledge the need for it in repelling the heretics, and now heresies have arisen, tribulation has become general, and need has become urgent, then the performance of this science must become from the communal obligations (furuḍ al-kifāyāt), like the performance of guarding wealth, and the rest of rights such as judgeship, governance, and others. And as long as scholars do not occupy themselves with spreading that, teaching it, and researching it, it will not endure. If it were totally abandoned, it would disappear. And there is not in mere natural disposition (tabāʿ) enough capability to solve the doubts of the heretics unless one learns. So the teaching of it and research about it should also be from the communal obligations, unlike the time of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, for the need was not urgent for it.' Know that the truth is that there must be in every town someone who upholds this science, independently repelling the heresies that have arisen in that town, and that endures through teaching. But it is not correct to teach it generally like the teaching of fiqh and tafsir, for this (kalam) is like medicine, and fiqh is like food.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and an analogy. Ghazali addresses a hypothetical question: if kalam is needed to refute heretics, and heresies have become widespread, then shouldn't it become a communal obligation (fard kifaya), just like guarding wealth or holding judicial positions? If scholars do not engage in teaching and researching it, it will not endure. Mere human nature cannot solve heretical doubts without learning. Ghazali responds that, due to the urgency, teaching and researching kalam is indeed a communal obligation, unlike the time of the Companions when it was not needed. However, he adds an important qualification: it is not correct to teach kalam generally to everyone like fiqh and tafsir. He explains with a powerful analogy: kalam is like medicine—needed only by those afflicted with doubts—while fiqh is like food, which everyone needs generally. Thus kalam should be studied only by those capable and in need, not as a universal subject.

Key Terms
فروض الكفايات
المبتدعة
الدواء
الغذاء
الطباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because kalam is necessary, it should be taught to everyone like basic fiqh.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that kalam is like medicine, only needed by those afflicted with doubts, and teaching it broadly could cause more harm.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali compare kalam to in terms of its necessity and teaching scope?
A) Food
B) Medicine
C) A sword
D) Chess

Key Takeaway
Ghazali affirms that kalam is a communal duty in times of heresy, but it must be taught selectively like medicine, not universally like food.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali says kalam is like medicine—only for those afflicted with doubts. But have you ever felt a doubt about your faith and done nothing, either because you were too comfortable or because you feared the answer? What is one doubt you have ignored, and what concrete step will you take this week to seek a sound answer—from a trusted teacher or a reliable source based on the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
Imagine you are in a situation where someone close to you expresses a heretical idea. Would you be able to gently correct them with clear reasoning, or would your silence allow the doubt to grow? What can you learn from this lesson to prepare yourself—even if you are not a scholar—to defend the truth when needed?
?
Ghazali warns against teaching kalam to everyone indiscriminately. But what about your own heart? Are you studying these matters out of genuine need and humility, or out of intellectual pride? Take a moment to examine your intention: are you seeking to worship Allah or to appear knowledgeable? Ask Allah to purify your intention before you continue.

Kalam: Praiseworthy and Blameworthy Summarized
Ghazali summarizes the criteria for praiseworthy and blameworthy kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between praiseworthy and blameworthy kalam as summarized by Imam al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Not all kalam is forbidden; some is praiseworthy.

Imam al-Ghazali has been discussing the criteria for praiseworthy and blameworthy theological discourse (kalam). In this passage, he provides a concise summary of the key points: the mind is sharpened by religious sciences without harm, and the distinction between praiseworthy and blameworthy kalam depends on the situation and the person.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own summary come through.
Notice the concise concluding style.
2
Identify the key elements
The passage lists several things you have 'known by this amount'. Identify the blameworthy, praiseworthy, the situations, and the persons.
These are the criteria for evaluating kalam.
3
Reflect on the broader context
Consider how this summary encapsulates Ghazali's nuanced approach: kalam is not simply forbidden or allowed; it depends on conditions.
This connects to the learning objective.

Why Kalam is Taught Selectively: Medicine Not Food
Ghazali responds to an objection about the necessity of teaching kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's nuanced position that teaching kalam is a restricted communal obligation, necessary but not universally taught.

Core Idea
Kalam is medicine for specific ills, not daily food for everyone.

Ghazali anticipates an objection: if kalam is needed to refute innovators, shouldn't teaching it be a communal obligation like other essential duties? He responds with a careful distinction—kalam must be taught in every town to those who can handle it, but it cannot be taught indiscriminately like fiqh. This passage explains why kalam is like a potent medicine that requires careful administration.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector argues that since kalam is needed to repel innovators, teaching it should be a communal obligation, similar to guarding wealth or being a judge. Note the logic: need + persistence requires teaching.
This objection reflects a common desire to make kalam universally taught.
2
Identify Ghazali's concession
Ghazali agrees that there must be someone in every town capable of refuting local innovations, and that teaching sustains this capability. He does not reject the need for teaching entirely.
He affirms the necessity but with qualifications.
3
Understand the medicine vs. food analogy
Ghazali says kalam is like medicine (beneficial but potentially harmful), while fiqh is like food (always safe). Therefore, kalam cannot be taught generally to everyone.
This is the key insight: the potential for harm restricts its teaching.
4
Reflect on the implication
Kalam is necessary but restricted. The passage teaches that theological training must be managed carefully, not opened to all. This guards against both negligence and recklessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت مهما اعترفت بالحاجة إليه في دفع المبتدعة ، والآن قد ثارت البدع وعمت البلوى وأرهقت الحاجة ، فلا بد أن يصير القيام بهذا العلم من فروض الكفايات ، كالقيام بحراسة الأموال ، وسائر الحقوق كالقضاء والولاية وغيرهما ، وما لم يشتغل العلماء بنشر ذلك ، والتدريس فيه والبحث عنه لا يدوم ، ولو ترك بالكلية لا ندرس ، وليس في مجرد الطباع كفاية لحل شبه المبتدعة ما لم يتعلم ، أن يكون التدريس فيه والبحث عنه أيضاً من فروض الكفايات ، بخلاف زمن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم ، فإن الحاجة ما كانت ماسة إليه. فاعلم أن الحق أنه لا بد في كل بلد من قائم بهذا العلم ، مستقل بدفع شبه المبتدعة التي ثارت في تلك البلدة ، وذلك يدوم بالتعليم ، ولكن ليس من الصواب تدريسه على العموم كتدريس الفقه والتفسير ، فإن هذا مثل الدواء ، والفقه مثل الغذاء ، وضرر الغذاء لا يحذر ، وضرر الدواء محذور لما ذكرنا فيه من أنواع الضرر
If you say: Whenever you acknowledge the need for it in repelling the innovators, and now innovations have arisen and the affliction has become general and the need has become urgent, then it is necessary that undertaking this science becomes among the communal obligations, like undertaking the guarding of wealth and other rights such as judgeship and governance and others. And unless scholars engage in spreading that, and teaching it and researching it, it will not persist; and if it is entirely abandoned, it will not be studied. And mere natural disposition is not sufficient for solving the doubts of the innovators as long as it is not learned. Therefore, teaching it and researching it should also be among the communal obligations, contrary to the time of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, for the need was not pressing to it. Then know that the truth is that in every town there must be someone who undertakes this science, independent in repelling the innovations that have arisen in that town, and that is sustained by teaching. But it is not correct to teach it generally like teaching jurisprudence and exegesis, for this is like medication, and jurisprudence is like food. The harm of food is not feared, but the harm of medication is feared because of the types of harm we mentioned in it.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. First, an objector argues: since you acknowledge the need for kalam to refute innovators, and now innovation is widespread, teaching kalam should be a communal obligation (fard kifaya) similar to guarding wealth or appointments like judgeship. Moreover, if scholars do not teach it, it will perish; mere natural intellect is insufficient to handle doubts. Therefore, teaching it should also be obligatory, unlike the time of the Companions when need was not pressing. Ghazali responds: the truth is that there must be someone in every town who can handle the specific innovations of that town, and this is sustained by teaching. However, it is not correct to teach it universally like fiqh or tafsir, because kalam is like a medicine (da') that can be harmful if misused, whereas fiqh is like food that is always safe. Thus, while it is necessary, it should be taught selectively.

Key Terms
فروض الكفايات (communal obligations)
المبتدعة (innovators)
الطباع (natural disposition)
الدواء (medication)
الغذاء (food)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam should either be taught to everyone or not taught at all.
Correction: Ghazali shows that kalam is a restricted communal obligation: necessary in every town but only for those who can handle its potential harm, like a medicine.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why should kalam not be taught generally like fiqh and tafsir?
A) Because it is forbidden
B) Because it is like medication that can cause harm
C) Because only the intelligent can understand it
D) Because the need for it has passed

Key Takeaway
Kalam is a necessary science that must be taught selectively in every community, like a medicine prescribed only to those who need it and can handle it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever engaged in theological debates or studied kalam without proper guidance, assuming it was harmless like food? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of such an approach? How will you change your pursuit of knowledge from today?
?
In your town or community, is there someone qualified to refute doubts about the creed? If not, what responsibility falls on you? Are you willing to become that person, or to at least support someone who is?
?
This lesson compares kalam to medicine: it can heal or harm. When you learn or teach matters of deeper theology, do you check your own intention and qualification as carefully as you would before taking a medicine? What is one concrete step you can take to ensure you use this knowledge responsibly?

Three Prerequisites for Studying Kalam
Ghazali lists three qualities required for a person to be taught kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential qualities that a student of kalam must possess according to Imam al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Not everyone is fit for kalam; it requires devotion, intelligence, and piety.

Imam al-Ghazali, the great theologian and philosopher, sets clear criteria for who should be taught the science of kalam. He emphasizes that kalam is not for everyone; it requires a combination of moral and intellectual virtues. In this passage, he identifies three specific characteristics that a student must have to benefit from this science and avoid harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Try to grasp the overall meaning before diving into analysis.
Let Ghazali's own words set the foundation.
2
Identify the three characteristics
The passage lists three qualities. Find each one and note how Ghazali describes them. Look for the words 'إحداها', 'الثانية', 'الثالثة'.
These are the three pillars of a suitable student.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each
For each characteristic, Ghazali gives a reason. For example, the professional is too occupied; the dull-witted cannot benefit; the immoral person uses doubt as an excuse. Think about why each reason matters.
The reasons show the potential harms if these qualities are missing.
4
Reflect on the moral dimension
Notice that the third characteristic is about moral uprightness. Ghazali warns that kalam can be dangerous for an immoral person. Consider why faith must be strong before engaging in theological debate.
Kalam is not just intellectual; it requires spiritual maturity.
5
Test your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz and complete the activity to reinforce what you've learned.
Apply the knowledge to see if you can recall and explain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعالم ينبغي أن يخصص بتعليم هذا العلم من فيه ثلاث خصال: إحداها التجرد للعلم والحرص عليه، فإن المحترف يمنعه الشغل عن الإستتمام وإزالة الشكوك إذا عرضت. الثانية الذكاء والفطنة والفصاحة، فإن البليد لا ينتفع بفهمه، والقدم لا ينتفع بحلجاجه فيخاف عليه من ضرر الكلام ولا يرجى فيه نفعه. الثالثة أن يكون في طبعه الصلاح والديانة والتقوى، ولا تكون الشهوات غالبة عليه، فإن الفاسق بأدنى شبهة ينخلع عن الدين، فإن ذلك يحل عنه الحجر ويرفع السد الذي بينه وبين الملاذ، فلا يحرص على إزالة الشبه بل يغتنمها ليتخلص
So the scholar should specify for teaching this science only the one who possesses three characteristics: First: devotion to learning and eagerness for it, for the professional is prevented by occupation from completing it and removing doubts when they arise. Second: intelligence, acumen, and eloquence, for the dull-witted does not benefit from his understanding, and the slow-witted does not benefit from his argument, so harm from kalam is feared for him and no benefit is hoped from it. Third: that there be in his nature righteousness, religiosity, and piety, and that desires not be dominant over him, for the immoral person is uprooted from religion by the slightest doubt, for that removes the barrier and lifts the dam between him and pleasures, so he is not eager to remove the doubt but rather seizes it to escape.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage enumerates three prerequisites for a student of kalam. First, the student must be devoted solely to learning, not a professional engaged in worldly occupations that would distract from deep study and resolving doubts. Second, the student must possess intelligence (dhaka'), acumen (fitna), and eloquence (fasaha); otherwise, the dull (balid) and slow-witted (qadam) will neither understand nor argue effectively, and may even be harmed by kalam. Third, the student must have righteousness (salah), religiosity (diyana), and piety (taqwa), and not be dominated by desires; an immoral person (fasiq) will use the slightest doubt as an excuse to abandon religion, since kalam can remove the deterrent barrier of faith and allow him to indulge in pleasures. Thus, the teacher must carefully select students with all three qualities.

الفصاحة (eloquence)
الصلاح (righteousness)
الديانة (religiosity)
التقوى (piety)
الفاسق (immoral)
البليد (dull)
القدم (slow-witted)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think kalam can be studied by anyone; Ghazali restricts it to those with specific moral and intellectual qualities.
Correction: Ghazali makes it clear that kalam is not for everyone. It requires devotion, intelligence, and piety to be beneficial and avoid harm.

Checkpoint

D) Being a scholar of fiqh

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ghazali teaches that a student of kalam must possess devotion to learning, intelligence and eloquence, and moral righteousness, otherwise kalam may cause harm rather than benefit.

?

Three Qualities for Teaching Kalam
Ghazali enumerates three qualities a teacher of kalam must look for in a student.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential qualities a teacher must find in a student before teaching kalam, according to Imam al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Teaching kalam requires students with devotion, intelligence, and piety.

Imam al-Ghazali sets strict prerequisites for who should be taught kalam, because it is a powerful discipline that can both build and destroy faith. He outlines three qualities that a student must possess to benefit from kalam without being harmed by it. Let's explore these qualities as stated in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Ghazali uses a clear three-part enumeration.
2
Identify the three qualities
Scan the passage and list the three qualities. Each is introduced by a number in the text: (First), (Second), (Third). Write them down in your own words.
The terms for each quality are: التجرد والحرص, الذكاء والفطنة والفصاحة, الصلاح والديانة والتقوى.
3
Understand why each quality matters
For each quality, read the explanation Ghazali gives. Why must a student be devoted? Why must they be intelligent? Why must they be pious? Note the negative examples (the dullard, the slow, the immoral).
4
Reflect on the overall principle
Ask yourself: Why does Ghazali insist on these strict requirements? What happens if a student lacks any of these qualities? Connect this to the purpose of kalam as a tool for defending faith, not indulging desires.
The passage warns that a corrupt student will do more harm than good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعالم ينبغي أن يخصص بتعليم هذا العلم من فيه ثلاث خصال ) إحداها ( التجرد للعلم والحرص (۱) عليه ، فإن المحترف يمنعه الشغل عن الإستتمام وإزالة الشكوك إذا عرضت (٢) (۱) والاستعداد لطلب المعرفة ، والحرص عليه بالإكباب على درسه وتعلمه (٢) وذلك لعدم إستعداده لذلك (الثانية) الذكاء والفطنة والفصاحة (۳) ، فإن البليد لا ينتفع بفهمه (٤) ، والقدم لا ينتفع بحلجاجه فيخاف عليه من ضرر الكلام ولا يرجى فيه نفعه (۳) والذكاء : وهو سرعة الإدراك، وحدة الفهم ، وقيل : هو سرعة إقتراح النتائج وأما الفطنة : فهي سرعة هجوم على حقائق معان مما تورده الحواس عليها وأما الفصاحة : فهي ملكة يقتدر بها على التعبير عن المقصود (٤) البليد : هو المتحير في أمره الذي لا يوصف بذكاء ولا فطنه ، فهو لا ينتفع بفهمه بل هو دائما حيران في أمره . أما القدم : فهو البطيء الفهم (الثالثة) أن يكون في طبعه الصلاح والديانة والتقوى (٥)، ولا تكون الشهوات غالبة عليه (٦) ، فإن الفاسق بأدنى شبهة ينخلع عن الدين ، فإن ذلك يحل عنه الحجر ويرفع السد الذي بينه وبين الملاذ ، فلا يحرص على إزالة الشبه بل يغتنمها ليتخلص من أعباء التكليف ، فيكون ما يفسده مثل هذا المتعلم أكثر مما يصلحه (۱) (٥) الصلاح : وهو ضد الفساد ويختصان في أكثر الإستعمال بالأفعال ؛ وقوبل في القرآن الكريم تارة الفساد ، وأخرى بالسيئة أما الديانة : فهي التمسك بأمور الدين ؛ والتقوى : وهي تجنب القبيح خوفا من الله تعالى وقيل : التقوى هي الإمتثال لما أمر الله به ، والإجتناب لما نهى الله عنه (٦) وفي معنى الشهوات النفسية : التعصبات للمذاهب ، والمباهات بالمعارف
The scholar should only teach this knowledge to one who possesses three characteristics: (First) Devotion to knowledge and eagerness for it, for the professional is prevented by occupation from perfecting it and removing doubts when they arise. (1) Preparation for seeking knowledge, and eagerness for it by diligently studying and learning it. (2) That is due to his lack of preparedness for that. (Second) Intelligence, acumen, and eloquence, for the dullard does not benefit from his understanding, and the slow one does not benefit from his argument, so it is feared for him from the harm of kalam and no benefit is hoped for in him. (3) Intelligence: it is quickness of perception, sharpness of understanding, and it is said: it is quickness in producing results. As for acumen: it is quickness in arriving at the realities of meanings from what the senses bring. As for eloquence: it is a faculty by which one is able to express what is intended. (4) The dullard: is the one bewildered in his affair who is not described with intelligence or acumen, so he does not benefit from his understanding, but is always confused in his affair. As for the slow one: he is the one slow of understanding. (Third) That he should have in his nature righteousness, religiosity, and piety, and that desires should not dominate him, for the immoral person is stripped of religion by the slightest doubt, for that releases the barrier and removes the dam between him and pleasures, so he does not strive to remove doubts but rather seizes them to be freed from the burdens of obligation, so what such a learner corrupts is more than what he rectifies. (5) Righteousness: it is the opposite of corruption, and they are used mostly in actions; in the noble Quran it is contrasted sometimes with corruption, and other times with evil. As for religiosity: it is adherence to the matters of religion; and piety: it is avoiding the ugly out of fear of Allah, and it is said: piety is complying with what Allah has commanded and avoiding what He has forbidden. (6) And among the meanings of psychological desires: fanaticism for schools of thought and boasting about knowledge.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage enumerates three essential qualities that a scholar must find in a student before teaching him kalam. The first quality is complete devotion and eagerness for knowledge, as opposed to someone who treats it as a mere profession or distraction. The second quality is intellectual capacity: intelligence (rapid comprehension), acumen (quick grasp of underlying meanings), and eloquence (ability to articulate). Two negative examples are given: the dullard (who cannot benefit) and the slow-witted (who cannot argue effectively), both likely to cause harm or be harmed by kalam. The third quality is moral and religious integrity: righteousness, religiosity, and piety, along with control over base desires. Without these, a person may use kalam to justify abandoning religious obligations for pleasure. The passage explains each term and then concludes that such a misapplied student does more harm than good.

Key Terms
التجرد
الحرص
الذكاء
الفطنة
الفصاحة
الصلاح
الديانة
التقوى
الشهوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That kalam can be taught to anyone willing to learn.
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes strict prerequisites: a student must possess devotion, intelligence, and piety; otherwise, kalam may cause harm rather than benefit.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the first quality a teacher should look for in a student of kalam?
A) Intelligence and acumen
B) Devotion to knowledge and eagerness
C) Righteousness and piety
D) Eloquence and articulateness

Key Takeaway
Students of kalam must possess devotion, intelligence, and piety to benefit from it without being led astray.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Four Audiences and Ruling for Definitive Kalam
Ghazali enumerates four audiences for kalam and gives the ruling for definitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of audiences for kalam discussion and the ruling for definitive (qat‘i) knowledge.

Core Idea
Definitive kalam may be shared with suitable seekers but not with commoners.

Imam al-Ghazali provides a detailed classification of who may engage in theological discussion. He distinguishes four types of audiences and explains when definitive knowledge may be divulged. This lesson covers the categories and the ruling for certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of four audiences
Read the passage and identify the four types of people with whom a scholar may discuss kalam: himself, a peer in insight, one prepared for insight, and a commoner.
This classification is the foundation for the rulings that follow.
2
Understand the conditions for a peer
Notice the qualities required for a peer to be suitable: devotion to knowledge, freedom from worldly desires and fanaticism.
Only a peer free from these flaws may be spoken to about definitive matters.
3
Learn the ruling for definitive knowledge with self and peer
The passage states that a scholar may speak to himself and to a suitable peer about qat‘i matters.
4
Understand why commoners are forbidden
The commoner is defined as anyone lacking the above qualities, and the passage explicitly forbids discussing definitive knowledge with them.
This is because they may misunderstand or misuse the knowledge.
5
Reflect on the principle of deservingness
Notice the statement: 'preventing knowledge from its people is injustice, but giving it to those not worthy is even worse.' This captures the wisdom behind the rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر الإمام الغزالي رضي الله عنه في كتاب الجام العوام ما يشرح هذا ويوضحه فقال : « التحدث في هذا العلم للعالم إنما يكون على أربعة أوجه : إما أن يكون مع نفسه ، أو مع من هو مثله في الإستبصار ، أو مع من هو مستعد للإستبصار بذكائه وفطنته وتجرده لطلب معرفة الله أو مع العامي » فإن كان قاطعا ، أي لا ظانا ، أي غير حاكم مع نفسه بموجب ظنه حكما جازما فله أن يحدث نفسه به ، ويحدث من هو مثله في الإستبصار، وهو متجرد لطلب المعرفة مستعد لها ، خال عن الميل إلى الدنيا والشهوات والتعصبات للمذاهب ، وطلب المباهاة بالمعارف ، والتظاهر بذكرها مع العوام ، فمن اتصف بهذه الصفات فلا بأس بالتحدث معه ، لأن الفطن المتعطش إلى المعرفة للمعرفة لا لغرض يحبك في صدره ، أشكال الظواهر ، وربما يلقيه في التأويلات الفاسدة لشدة شرهه عن الفرار عن الظواهر ومقتضاها ، ومنع العلم أهله ظلم كبته الى غير أهله وأما العامي : فلا يحدث به، وفي معنى العامي كل من لا يوصف بالصفات المذكورة ؛ والمظنون : فيحدث به مع نفسه إضطرار فإن ما ينطوي عليه الذهن من ظن وشك وقطع لا تزال النفس تحدث به ولا قدرة على الخلاص منه ، ولا منع منه ، ولا شك في منع التحدث به مع العوام ، بل هو أولى بالمنع من المقطوع، أما تحدثه به مع من هو في مثل درجته في المعرفة أو مع المستعد له فيه نظر فيحتمل أن يقال هو جائز إذ لا يزيد على أن يقول : أظن كذا وهو صادق ؛ ويحتمل المنع ، لأنه قادر على تركه ، وهو بذكره متصرف بالظن في صفة الله تعالى ، أو في مراده من كلامه ، وفيه خطر ، وإباحته إنما تعرف بنص أو إجماع أو قياس على منصوص ، ولم يروشيء من ذلك ، بل ورد قوله تعالى : ﴿ ولا تقف ما ليس به علم ﴾ وفي الجام العوام للإمام الغزالي بسط هذا المعنى فإرجع إليه إن شئت
And Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah be pleased with him, mentioned in his book Iljam al-Awamm what explains and clarifies this, saying: ‘The scholar’s discussion of this knowledge is only of four kinds: either with himself, or with one like him in insight, or with one who is prepared for insight by his intelligence, acumen, and devotion to seeking knowledge of Allah, or with the commoner.’ If it is definitive (qati‘), not merely suppositional (zann), that is, he is not judging by his assumption with a decisive judgment, then he may speak to himself about it, and he may speak to one like him in insight, who is devoted to seeking knowledge, prepared for it, free from inclination to worldly desires, passions, fanaticism for schools of thought, and seeking to boast about knowledge or to display it among commoners. Whoever is described with these qualities, there is no harm in speaking with him, because the intelligent person thirsting for knowledge for its own sake, not for some purpose, finds the formal aspects of apparent meanings in his heart, and perhaps excessive craving to escape from apparent meanings and their implications may lead him to corrupt interpretations. And preventing knowledge from its rightful people is injustice, but giving it to those not worthy is even worse. As for the commoner: he is not to be spoken to about it, and the meaning of commoner is everyone who is not described with the mentioned qualities. As for suppositional knowledge (al-maznun): he speaks to himself about it out of necessity, for what the mind contains of assumption, doubt, and certainty—the soul continues to speak about it and there is no ability to be free from it, nor prevention from it. There is no doubt about forbidding speaking about it to commoners; indeed, it is more deserving of prohibition than the definitive. As for his speaking about it with one who is at his level in knowledge or with one who is prepared—there is consideration: it is possible to say it is permissible, since he does not go beyond saying ‘I suppose such-and-such’ and he is truthful; and it is possible to say it is forbidden, because he is able to leave it, and by mentioning it he is dealing with supposition regarding the attributes of Allah or what He intends from His speech, and there is danger. Its permissibility is only known by a text (nass), consensus (ijma‘), or analogy (qiyas) on a text, and nothing has been narrated concerning that; rather, Allah’s saying ‘And do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge’ (Qur’an 17:36) has come down. And in Iljam al-Awamm of Imam al-Ghazali there is a fuller exposition of this meaning, so refer to it if you wish.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Ghazali’s Iljam al-Awamm that provides the detailed rulings for discussing kalam. It begins by listing four possible audiences for such discussion: oneself, a peer in insight, one prepared for insight, or a commoner. Then it elaborates on each case. For definitive knowledge (qat‘i), discussion is allowed with oneself and with a peer who is devoted and free from worldly motives. The commoner is never to be told. For suppositional knowledge (maznun), one may only speak to oneself out of necessity, and it is forbidden to speak to commoners—in fact, it is even more strictly forbidden than definitive knowledge. As for speaking with a peer or one prepared, there is a valid debate: some consider it permissible because it is honest expression of opinion, others forbid it due to the danger of speaking about Allah without knowledge. Ghazali notes that permissibility would require a clear scriptural basis, which is lacking; rather, the Qur’anic verse prohibiting pursuing what one has no knowledge of supports caution. The passage ends by referring the reader to the fuller treatment in Iljam al-Awamm.

عامي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all kalam discussion is equally forbidden.
Correction: Ghazali shows that definitive knowledge can be shared with qualified individuals, while commoners are forbidden.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, with whom is a scholar allowed to discuss definitive (qat‘i) matters of kalam?
A) With a commoner
B) Only with himself
C) With himself and a peer who is devoted and free from worldly desires
D) With anyone who asks

Key Takeaway
Definitive kalam may be discussed with oneself and suitable peers, but never with commoners, because knowledge must be given to those who can handle it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge, do you find yourself attached to any worldly desires, biases, or a need to appear knowledgeable? How might that affect your ability to truly understand and handle definitive truths? Commit to checking your intentions before seeking deep knowledge.
?
Has there been a time when you shared a deep theological insight with someone who was not ready, causing confusion or harm? What does this lesson teach you about guarding knowledge and the consequences of misplacing it?
?
Are you in the category of the 'prepared seeker' as described by Ghazali – free from the shackles of fanaticism and worldly ambitions? If not, what specific step will you take today to purify your intent so that you are worthy of deeper knowledge?

Ruling for Suppositional Knowledge (Maznun) in Kalam
Ghazali details rulings for suppositional knowledge (maznun) in kalam discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling for suppositional (maznun) knowledge in kalam, including the necessity of speaking to oneself, the prohibition with commoners, and the scholarly debate regarding discussing it with peers.

Core Idea
Suppositional knowledge about Allah is dangerous and requires even greater caution than definitive knowledge.

After covering definitive knowledge, Ghazali turns to suppositional knowledge (maznun). He explains that one cannot help but entertain such thoughts privately, but speaking about them publicly or even with peers is subject to stricter rules. This lesson examines the rulings and the underlying reasons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on maznun
Read the passage starting from 'والمظنون' to the end. Notice the distinction between speaking to oneself and speaking to others.
This section follows the earlier discussion on qat‘i.
2
Understand the necessity of private reflection
The passage states that speaking to oneself about maznun is necessary because the mind cannot be prevented from having suppositions.
This is allowed out of necessity, not as a general permission.
3
Learn the stricter prohibition with commoners
Ghazali says that discussing maznun with commoners is even more forbidden than discussing qat‘i, because of the danger of speaking about Allah without knowledge.
4
Explore the debate about speaking with peers
The passage presents two opinions: some say it is permissible if one honestly states it as opinion, others forbid it due to the risk. Ghazali notes the lack of textual evidence for permissibility.
The Quranic verse 'Do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge' is cited as a caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر الإمام الغزالي رضي الله عنه في كتاب الجام العوام ما يشرح هذا ويوضحه فقال : « التحدث في هذا العلم للعالم إنما يكون على أربعة أوجه : إما أن يكون مع نفسه ، أو مع من هو مثله في الإستبصار ، أو مع من هو مستعد للإستبصار بذكائه وفطنته وتجرده لطلب معرفة الله أو مع العامي » فإن كان قاطعا ، أي لا ظانا ، أي غير حاكم مع نفسه بموجب ظنه حكما جازما فله أن يحدث نفسه به ، ويحدث من هو مثله في الإستبصار، وهو متجرد لطلب المعرفة مستعد لها ، خال عن الميل إلى الدنيا والشهوات والتعصبات للمذاهب ، وطلب المباهاة بالمعارف ، والتظاهر بذكرها مع العوام ، فمن اتصف بهذه الصفات فلا بأس بالتحدث معه ، لأن الفطن المتعطش إلى المعرفة للمعرفة لا لغرض يحبك في صدره ، أشكال الظواهر ، وربما يلقيه في التأويلات الفاسدة لشدة شرهه عن الفرار عن الظواهر ومقتضاها ، ومنع العلم أهله ظلم كبته الى غير أهله وأما العامي : فلا يحدث به، وفي معنى العامي كل من لا يوصف بالصفات المذكورة ؛ والمظنون : فيحدث به مع نفسه إضطرار فإن ما ينطوي عليه الذهن من ظن وشك وقطع لا تزال النفس تحدث به ولا قدرة على الخلاص منه ، ولا منع منه ، ولا شك في منع التحدث به مع العوام ، بل هو أولى بالمنع من المقطوع، أما تحدثه به مع من هو في مثل درجته في المعرفة أو مع المستعد له فيه نظر فيحتمل أن يقال هو جائز إذ لا يزيد على أن يقول : أظن كذا وهو صادق ؛ ويحتمل المنع ، لأنه قادر على تركه ، وهو بذكره متصرف بالظن في صفة الله تعالى ، أو في مراده من كلامه ، وفيه خطر ، وإباحته إنما تعرف بنص أو إجماع أو قياس على منصوص ، ولم يروشيء من ذلك ، بل ورد قوله تعالى : ﴿ ولا تقف ما ليس به علم ﴾ وفي الجام العوام للإمام الغزالي بسط هذا المعنى فإرجع إليه إن شئت
And Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah be pleased with him, mentioned in his book Iljam al-Awamm what explains and clarifies this, saying: ‘The scholar’s discussion of this knowledge is only of four kinds: either with himself, or with one like him in insight, or with one who is prepared for insight by his intelligence, acumen, and devotion to seeking knowledge of Allah, or with the commoner.’ If it is definitive (qati‘), not merely suppositional (zann), that is, he is not judging by his assumption with a decisive judgment, then he may speak to himself about it, and he may speak to one like him in insight, who is devoted to seeking knowledge, prepared for it, free from inclination to worldly desires, passions, fanaticism for schools of thought, and seeking to boast about knowledge or to display it among commoners. Whoever is described with these qualities, there is no harm in speaking with him, because the intelligent person thirsting for knowledge for its own sake, not for some purpose, finds the formal aspects of apparent meanings in his heart, and perhaps excessive craving to escape from apparent meanings and their implications may lead him to corrupt interpretations. And preventing knowledge from its rightful people is injustice, but giving it to those not worthy is even worse. As for the commoner: he is not to be spoken to about it, and the meaning of commoner is everyone who is not described with the mentioned qualities. As for suppositional knowledge (al-maznun): he speaks to himself about it out of necessity, for what the mind contains of assumption, doubt, and certainty—the soul continues to speak about it and there is no ability to be free from it, nor prevention from it. There is no doubt about forbidding speaking about it to commoners; indeed, it is more deserving of prohibition than the definitive. As for his speaking about it with one who is at his level in knowledge or with one who is prepared—there is consideration: it is possible to say it is permissible, since he does not go beyond saying ‘I suppose such-and-such’ and he is truthful; and it is possible to say it is forbidden, because he is able to leave it, and by mentioning it he is dealing with supposition regarding the attributes of Allah or what He intends from His speech, and there is danger. Its permissibility is only known by a text (nass), consensus (ijma‘), or analogy (qiyas) on a text, and nothing has been narrated concerning that; rather, Allah’s saying ‘And do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge’ (Qur’an 17:36) has come down. And in Iljam al-Awamm of Imam al-Ghazali there is a fuller exposition of this meaning, so refer to it if you wish.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Ghazali’s Iljam al-Awamm that provides the detailed rulings for discussing kalam. It begins by listing four possible audiences for such discussion: oneself, a peer in insight, one prepared for insight, or a commoner. Then it elaborates on each case. For definitive knowledge (qat‘i), discussion is allowed with oneself and with a peer who is devoted and free from worldly motives. The commoner is never to be told. For suppositional knowledge (maznun), one may only speak to oneself out of necessity, and it is forbidden to speak to commoners—in fact, it is even more strictly forbidden than definitive knowledge. As for speaking with a peer or one prepared, there is a valid debate: some consider it permissible because it is honest expression of opinion, others forbid it due to the danger of speaking about Allah without knowledge. Ghazali notes that permissibility would require a clear scriptural basis, which is lacking; rather, the Qur’anic verse prohibiting pursuing what one has no knowledge of supports caution. The passage ends by referring the reader to the fuller treatment in Iljam al-Awamm.
