Correction: Ghazali allows speaking to oneself out of necessity, but forbids it with commoners and leaves room for debate with peers.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the ruling for discussing suppositional knowledge (maznun) with a peer who is at the same level of knowledge?

B) Definitely forbidden

Key Takeaway
Suppositional knowledge may be privately considered, but discussing it with others, especially commoners, is strictly cautioned against due to the risk of speaking about Allah without knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If speaking about Allah without certain knowledge is so dangerous, how many times have you unknowingly spoken about Him based on hearsay or weak reasoning? Will you now commit to verify before you speak?

The Four Categories of Speaking About Kalam
Al-Ghazali classifies speaking about kalam into four categories and gives their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's classification of speaking about kalam into four categories and the corresponding rulings for certain and probable knowledge.

Core Idea
Speaking about kalam is not one-size-fits-all — it depends on certainty and audience.

Al-Ghazali presents a nuanced framework for when and to whom a scholar may discuss theological matters. He identifies four types of interlocutors and distinguishes between certain and probable knowledge. This passage establishes a key principle of Islamic scholarly ethics: knowledge must be shared with the right person at the right level of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four categories
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that al-Ghazali immediately lists four situations: speaking with oneself, with an equal in insight, with someone prepared for insight, or with a common person.
These categories are based on the audience's capacity and the speaker's certainty.
2
Understand the ruling for certain (qati') knowledge
Al-Ghazali explains that certain knowledge can be spoken with oneself and with a worthy equal who is devoted and free from biases. However, it must not be shared with the common person.
3
Understand the ruling for probable (maznun) knowledge
For probable knowledge, speaking with oneself is compelled by necessity. Speaking with the common person is prohibited. For equals or prepared persons, there are two scholarly opinions: one permits it as honest opinion, the other prohibits it due to lack of certainty.
Al-Ghazali cites the Quranic verse 'Do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge' to support caution.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why knowledge is withheld from the common person: to prevent confusion and corrupt interpretations. Withholding from the worthy is injustice, but giving to the unworthy is also injustice.
This balances the responsibility of the scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر الإمام الغزالي رضي الله عنه في كتاب الجام العوام ما يشرح هذا ويوضحه فقال : « التحدث في هذا العلم للعالم إنما يكون على أربعة أوجه : إما أن يكون مع نفسه ، أو مع من هو مثله في الإستبصار ، أو مع من هو مستعد للإستبصار بذكائه وفطنته وتجرده لطلب معرفة الله أو مع العامي » فإن كان قاطعا، أي لا ظانا، أي غير حاكم مع نفسه بموجب ظنه حكما جازما فله أن يحدث نفسه به، ويحدث من هو مثله في الإستبصار، وهو متجرد لطلب المعرفة مستعد لها، خال عن الميل إلى الدنيا والشهوات والتعصبات للمذاهب، وطلب المباهاة بالمعارف، والتظاهر بذكرها مع العوام، فمن اتصف بهذه الصفات فلا بأس بالتحدث معه، لأن الفطن المتعطش إلى المعرفة للمعرفة لا لغرض يحبك في صدره، أشكال الظواهر، وربما يلقيه في التأويلات الفاسدة لشدة شرهه عن الفرار عن الظواهر ومقتضاها، ومنع العلم أهله ظلم كبته الى غير أهله وأما العامي : فلا يحدث به، وفي معنى العامي كل من لا يوصف بالصفات المذكورة؛ والمظنون : فيحدث به مع نفسه إضطرار فإن ما ينطوي عليه الذهن من ظن وشك وقطع لا تزال النفس تحدث به ولا قدرة على الخلاص منه ، ولا منع منه ، ولا شك في منع التحدث به مع العوام ، بل هو أولى بالمنع أما التحدث به مع من هو في مثل درجته في المعرفة أو مع المستعد له فيه نظر فيحتمل أن يقال هو جائز إذ لا يزيد على أن يقول : أظن كذا وهو صادق ؛ ويحتمل المنع ، لأنه قادر على تركه ، وهو بذكره متصرف بالظن في صفة الله تعالى ، أو في مراده من كلامه ، وفيه خطر ، وإباحته إنما تعرف بنص أو إجماع أو قياس على منصوص ، ولم يرو شيء من ذلك ، بل ورد قوله تعالى : ﴿ ولا تقف ما ليس به علم ﴾
Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah be pleased with him, mentioned in the book 'Iljam al-Awam' what explains and clarifies this, saying: 'Speaking about this knowledge for the scholar is only in four ways: either it is with himself, or with one like him in insight, or with one who is prepared for insight due to his intelligence, acumen, and devotion to seeking knowledge of Allah, or with the common person.' If it is certain (qati'), meaning not probable, meaning he is not judging with himself according to his opinion a decisive judgment, then he may speak about it with himself, and he may speak with one like him in insight who is devoted to seeking knowledge, prepared for it, free from inclination to worldly matters, desires, partisan biases, seeking pride in knowledge, and showing off with it before the common people. Whoever is described with these attributes, there is no harm in speaking with him, because the intelligent person thirsty for knowledge for its own sake, not for any purpose, will have doubts about apparent meanings implanted in his chest, and perhaps it will lead him to corrupt interpretations due to his intense eagerness to flee from apparent meanings and their implications. Withholding knowledge from its people is an injustice, and giving it to other than its people is also injustice. As for the common person: he should not be spoken to about it. The meaning of 'common person' is everyone who is not described with the mentioned attributes. As for the probable (maznun): he speaks about it with himself out of necessity, because what the mind contains of probability, doubt, and certainty, the self continuously speaks about it and there is no ability to escape from it, nor prevention from it. There is no doubt about the prohibition of speaking about it with the common people; rather, it is more deserving of prohibition. As for speaking about it with one who is on his level in knowledge or with the prepared one, there is consideration. It is possible to say it is permissible, because it does not exceed saying 'I think such-and-such' and he is truthful. And it is possible to prohibit it, because he is able to leave it, and by mentioning it he is acting upon probability regarding an attribute of Allah or His intent from His speech, and in that is danger. Its permissibility is only known by a text, consensus, or analogy upon a text, and nothing of that has been reported, rather the saying of Allah the Exalted has come: 'And do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge.'
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a classification and ruling on speaking about theological knowledge (kalam). It is a direct quotation from al-Ghazali's Iljam al-Awam. The passage begins by stating that speech about this knowledge for a scholar occurs in four situations: with himself, with an equal in insight, with a person prepared for insight due to intelligence and devotion, or with the common person. The scholar then details each case. For certain knowledge (qati'), it is permissible for the scholar to speak with himself and with an equal who is devoted and free from worldly desires and biases. The reason given is that such a person, being sincerely seeking knowledge, might otherwise fall into corrupt interpretations due to eagerness. Withholding knowledge from such a person is unjust. However, speaking with the common person about even certain knowledge is prohibited. For probable knowledge (maznun), it is allowed for the scholar to speak with himself out of necessity, but prohibited for the common person. For speaking with an equal or a prepared person, there are two views: one permits it because it is just stating an opinion truthfully, and one prohibits it because it involves speaking about Allah without certainty and there is no clear textual basis for permission. The passage concludes by citing the Quranic verse prohibiting pursuing what one has no knowledge of.

قاطع
مظنون
عامي
إستبصار
جام العوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think speaking about kalam is either always forbidden or always permissible.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that the ruling depends on the audience and the certainty of the knowledge, not a blanket rule.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's classification, for whom is it permissible to speak about certain (qati') knowledge of kalam?
A) With the common person only
B) With oneself and a worthy equal who is devoted to knowledge
C) With anyone who asks, regardless of status
D) Only with oneself, never with others

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's framework shows that speaking about kalam requires careful consideration of both the certitude of the knowledge and the capacity of the audience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel uncertainty about a matter of faith, do you immediately share it with others or wrestle with it privately? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's guidance or your desire to appear knowledgeable?
?
Think of a time you spoke about Allah or the Quran without being absolutely certain. Were you seeking truth or seeking validation? How can you commit to only speaking from certainty, even if it means remaining silent?
?
If you knew that sharing a profound spiritual insight with someone unprepared would harm their faith, would you still share it? What is harder for you: holding back knowledge or guarding another's heart?

The Contextual Ruling on Defending the Creed
Ghazali explains the contextual permissibility of debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the ruling on debating creedal issues depends on the need of the time, and that the Companions' debates were praiseworthy because they were clear and necessary.

Core Idea
Debate is praiseworthy when needed; times change rulings.

Imam al-Ghazali addresses a key question: if some early Muslims prohibited theological debate, how do we explain the debates of Ibn Abbas and Ali? He explains that those debates were clear, necessary, and therefore praiseworthy. More importantly, the ruling on debate can change with time because the need for it fluctuates. This lesson establishes that defending the creed is not a fixed prohibition but a contextual obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
The author anticipates an objection: if debate is harmful, why did the Companions engage in it? Notice how he addresses it by distinguishing between harmful and beneficial debate.
The objection is based on earlier warnings about harm.
2
Understand the Companions' examples
He cites Ibn Abbas debating the Khawarij and Ali debating predestination. He says these were 'clear and apparent speech' needed at the time.
The footnotes clarify that these debates did not require complex philosophical argumentation.
3
Grasp the principle of changing rulings
The author states that times differ in need, so the ruling on debate can differ accordingly. This shows that Islamic law considers context.
This is a key methodological principle in fiqh and kalam.
4
Contrast this with the earlier warning
Recall that earlier the author warned about harm from debating. Now he clarifies that harm applies only to certain cases, not to necessary debates.
The footnotes specify prohibited cases: fanaticism, inability to attain certainty, corrupting beliefs, or unnecessary intricacies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتجرد له لما فيه من الضرر الذي نبهنا عليه (١) ، وأن ما نقل عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما من مناظرة الخوارج ، وما نقل عن علي رضي الله عنه من المناظرة في القدر وغيره ، كان من الكلام الجلي الظاهر (٢) وفي محل الحاجة ، وذلك محمود في كل حال (٣) نعم : قد تختلف الأعصار في كثرة الحاجة وقلتها ، فلا يبعد أن يختلف الحكم لذلك (٤) . فهذا حكم العقيدة التي تعبد الخلق بها ، وحكم طريق النضال عنها وحفظها هي. فأما إزالة الشبهة وكشف الحقائق ومعرفة الأشياء على ما عليه ، وإدراك الأسرار التي يترجمها ظاهر ألفاظ هذه العقيدة ، فلا مفتاح له إلا المجاهد (٥) ، وقمع الشهوات والإقبال بالكلية على الله تعالى (٦) وملازمة الفكر الصافي عن شوائب المجادلات ، وهي (٧) رحمة من الله عز وجل تفيض على من يتعرض لنفحاتها (٨) بقدر 
 حواشي: (١) فإن أوالهم محمولة على نهي المتعصب في الدين، أو القاصر عن تحصيل اليقين أو القاصد إفساد عقائد المسلمين، أو الخائض فيما لا يفتقر إليه من غوامض المتفلسفين، وإلا فلا يتصور من شريف تلك الحضرات وقوع المنع فيها هو أصل الواجبات واساس المشروعات (٢) الذي لا يحتاج إلى فتح باب الجدل (٣) غير مذموم عند الرجال (٤) ولأجل ذلك ما خاض فيه الأولون إلا قليلا لعدم حدوث البدع في زمانهم فلم يحتاجوا إلى إبطالها وإفحام منتحلها (٥) المشار إليه في قوله عز وجل : ( وَالَّذِينَ جَاهَدُوا فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا (٦) بحيث لا يخطر في خاطره خاطر لسواه . (٧) أي تلك الحالة الحاصلة من هذه الأمور (٨) لما ورد في الحديث النبوي الصحيح : تعرضوا لنفحات الله، فإن لله نفحات )
And devotion to it because of the harm we have warned about, and that what was narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, regarding debating the Khawarij, and what was narrated from Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, regarding debate on predestination and other matters, was from clear and apparent speech, and in a place of need, and that is praiseworthy in every case. Yes: times may differ in the abundance or scarcity of need, so it is not unlikely that the ruling differs accordingly. This is the ruling of the creed by which the creation are obligated, and the ruling of the method of defending it and preserving it. As for removing doubts, uncovering realities, knowing things as they are, and grasping the secrets which the apparent wording of this creed translates, there is no key to it except the mujahid (one who strives), suppressing desires, turning completely to Allah the Exalted, and adhering to pure thought free from the impurities of disputations. And it (i.e., that state resulting from these matters) is a mercy from Allah the Exalted that flows upon those who expose themselves to His breezes, according to their capacity. Footnotes: (1) For their sayings are interpreted as prohibiting the fanatic in religion, or the one incapable of attaining certainty, or the one intending to corrupt the beliefs of Muslims, or the one delving into unnecessary intricacies of the philosophers. Otherwise, it is inconceivable from the noble presences that prohibition would occur in that which is the foundation of obligations and the basis of legislations. (2) That which does not require opening the door of debate. (3) Not blameworthy among men. (4) For this reason, the earlier ones did not delve into it except a little, because no innovations occurred in their time, so they did not need to refute them and silence their proponents. (5) Referred to in His saying, the Exalted: 'And those who strive for Us, We will surely guide them to Our ways.' (6) Such that no thought for other than Him occurs in his mind. (7) That is, that state resulting from these matters. (8) Based on what has come in the authentic prophetic hadith: 'Expose yourselves to the breezes of Allah, for Allah has breezes.'
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument about the proper method of defending the creed and attaining true knowledge. The author begins by reaffirming that devoting oneself to this knowledge involves harm, as previously warned. He then addresses a potential objection: what about the debates of Ibn Abbas with the Khawarij and Ali's debates on predestination? These were clear, apparent speech in a place of need, and thus praiseworthy. The author then notes that rulings can change with time because the need for debate varies. This is the ruling regarding the creed and the method of defending it. However, the key point follows: removing doubts, uncovering realities, and grasping the secrets behind the creed's wording—for this, the only key is the mujahid, one who strives (referencing Quran 29:69). This involves suppressing desires, turning completely to Allah, and pure thought free from the impurities of disputations. This state is a mercy from Allah that flows upon those who expose themselves to His breezes, as per a hadith. The footnotes clarify: the prohibition of debate from the salaf applies only to certain groups, not to the foundational obligations; the debates of the Companions were clear and needed; the early Muslims had no need to debate due to lack of innovations; and the hadith encourages seeking Allah's breezes.

Key Terms
نضال
إزالة الشبهة
مجادلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that all theological debate is prohibited because of warnings from early Muslims.
Correction: The author clarifies that debate is permissible and even praiseworthy when there is a genuine need, as demonstrated by the Companions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why were the debates of Ibn Abbas and Ali praiseworthy?
A) Because they used philosophical arguments
B) Because the debates were clear, apparent, and needed
C) Because they were held in secret
D) Because they avoided all disputation

Key Takeaway
The ruling on debating creedal matters is not fixed; it depends on the need of the time, and what is harmful in one situation may be praiseworthy in another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever engaged in a religious argument more to prove yourself right than to seek truth? How can you distinguish between defending Allah's honor and defending your ego?
?
When was the last time you remained silent about a false belief because it was easier, even though the need to speak was clear? What held you back?
?
If the ruling on debate changes with the needs of the time, are you honest about assessing your community's current needs, or do you follow a comfortable past fatwa without reflection?

The True Path to Knowledge: Striving and Divine Mercy
Ghazali reveals the path to true knowledge beyond debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true knowledge of the creed's deeper meanings is not attained through debate alone, but through spiritual striving (mujahada), suppressing desires, and receiving divine mercy.

Core Idea
Knowledge's key is striving, not argument.

After discussing the ruling on defending the creed, Imam al-Ghazali now turns to the deeper goal: removing doubts and understanding the realities behind the creed's words. He declares that the only key to this deeper knowledge is spiritual striving—mujahada. This lesson explores that path, which involves purifying the soul and turning to Allah, culminating in a divine mercy that 'breezes' upon the sincere seeker.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the goal
The author lists several objectives: removing doubts, uncovering realities, knowing things as they are, and grasping the secrets behind the creed's wording.
This is the ultimate aim of 'ilm al-kalam and spiritual realization.
2
Find the key
He says there is no key to these except the mujahid (one who strives). This is a powerful metaphor: just as a key opens a locked door, striving opens the door to true knowledge.
The verse referenced is Quran 29:69: 'And those who strive for Us, We will surely guide them to Our ways.'
3
Understand the conditions of the key
The key has three components: suppressing desires, turning completely to Allah, and maintaining pure thought free from the impurities of disputations.
Pure thought means reflection untainted by polemics and personal bias.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The state resulting from these efforts is a mercy from Allah that flows upon those who expose themselves to His 'breezes' (nifahat). This is a reference to a hadith encouraging seekers to be receptive to divine grace.
The amount of mercy received is according to one's capacity and sincerity.

Yes: times may differ in the abundance or scarcity of need, so it is not unlikely that the ruling differs accordingly. Conditional Permissibility of Theological Debate
Ghazali explains that the ruling on theological debate depends on need and era.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the permissibility of engaging in theological debate (kalam) is conditional upon need and clarity, and that it varies with time and context.

Core Idea
The ruling on kalam depends on need and clarity, not on blanket permission.

Imam Ghazali addresses a critical question: When is it permissible to engage in theological debate? He acknowledges the potential harms of kalam but shows that the Companions themselves engaged in it when necessary. This passage establishes the principle that the ruling on kalam varies with the need of the time and the clarity of the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp Ghazali's flow.
Notice how Ghazali structures his argument: harm, examples, conditional ruling.
2
Identify the Companions' examples
Find the references to Ibn Abbas and Ali. What type of debate did they engage in? Ghazali describes it as 'clear and apparent speech' in a 'place of need'.
These examples are key to understanding the conditional permission.
3
Understand the conditional rule
Ghazali says that eras differ in need, so the ruling may differ. This means kalam is not absolutely prohibited nor absolutely permitted—it depends on circumstances.
Contrast this with a static ruling; Ghazali introduces contextual ethics.
4
Reflect on modern application
Consider today: Are there situations where theological debate is necessary? How does the principle of need and clarity apply?
This helps internalize the lesson rather than just memorize.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتجرد له لما فيه من الضرر الذي نبهنا عليه، وأن ما نقل عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما من مناظرة الخوارج، وما نقل عن علي رضي الله عنه من المناظرة في القدر وغيره، كان من الكلام الجلي الظاهر وفي محل الحاجة، وذلك محمود في كل حال. نعم: قد تختلف الأعصار في كثرة الحاجة وقلتها، فلا يبعد أن يختلف الحكم لذلك. فهذا حكم العقيدة التي تعبد الخلق بها، وحكم طريق النضال عنها وحفظها.
And refraining from it because of the harm we pointed out, and that what was reported from Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) regarding debating the Khawarij, and what was reported from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding disputation about qadr and other matters, was from clear and apparent speech and in a place of need, and that is praiseworthy in every case. Yes: eras may differ in the abundance and scarcity of need, so it is not unlikely that the ruling differs accordingly. This is the ruling of the creed by which the creation are obligated, and the ruling of the path of defense and preservation of it.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and justification for engaging in theological debate (kalam). Imam Ghazali explains that refraining from kalam is due to its potential harm, which he previously mentioned. He then cites examples from the Companions: Ibn Abbas's debate with the Khawarij and Ali's debate on qadr. These were clear and apparent speech ('kalam jali zahir') done in a place of need, so they are praiseworthy in every situation. He then qualifies that eras differ in their need for such debate; when need is greater, the ruling may differ accordingly. He concludes that this is the ruling regarding the creed itself and the ruling regarding the method of defending and preserving it. The passage thus sets a conditional permissibility for kalam based on necessity and context.

Key Terms
كلام (kalam)
مناظرة (munazara)
جدل (jadal)
حاجة (haja)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Companions' engagement in debate proves kalam is always permissible.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly limits it to clear, necessary contexts; the ruling varies with need.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what determines whether engaging in kalam is praiseworthy?
A) The personal opinion of the scholar
B) The need of the time and the clarity of the debate
C) The number of participants
D) The use of logical arguments

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of kalam depends on the presence of genuine need and the clarity of the discussion, not on a blanket rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious doubt, do you immediately seek clear answers or find yourself drawn to speculative debates? How does Ghazali's condition of 'clear and apparent speech' challenge your tendency in such moments?
?
Reflect on a recent conversation where you argued about faith. Was it rooted in a genuine need to protect truth or in personal pride? What would it mean for you to honestly assess your motives before speaking?
?
If the permissibility of theological debate depends on need, what is the specific need in your life right now? Are you pursuing knowledge to defend the truth for Allah's sake, or are you merely engaging in intellectual pleasure? Ask Allah to purify your intention.

The Key to Deeper Knowledge is Spiritual Struggle
Ghazali describes the means to uncover hidden truths of the creed beyond its outward form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that, according to Imam al-Ghazali, the deeper truths of the creed are not attained through debate but through spiritual struggle, purity of heart, and divine mercy.

Core Idea
Deeper knowledge of faith comes through struggle, not argument.

Imam al-Ghazali teaches that while the outward form of the creed can be learned, its inner secrets require a different approach. In this passage, he explains that the key lies in spiritual struggle and purification, not in intellectual disputation. As you read, notice how he describes the process and its divine origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between 'apparent wording' and 'secrets'.
2
Identify the four elements of the key
The passage lists four things that serve as the key to deeper understanding. Find each one and notice how Ghazali presents them as essential prerequisites.
The first element is 'mujahada' (spiritual struggle).
3
Reflect on the nature of this knowledge
Ghazali describes this knowledge as a mercy from Allah that flows like gusts, and as an unfathomable sea. Ask yourself: what does this imagery tell you about how this knowledge is received?
Pay attention to the conditions: proportion to provision, exposure, and purity of heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما إزالة الشبهة وكشف الحقائق ومعرفة الأشياء على ما عليه، وإدراك الأسرار التي يترجمها ظاهر ألفاظ هذه العقيدة، فلا مفتاح له إلا المجاهد، وقمع الشهوات والإقبال بالكلية على الله تعالى وملازمة الفكر الصافي عن شوائب المجادلات، وهي رحمة من الله عز وجل تفيض على من يتعرض لنفحاتها بقدر الرزق، وبحسب التعرض وبحسب قبول المحل وطهارة القلب، وذلك البحر الذي لا يدرك غوره ولا يبلغ ساحله.
As for removing doubts, uncovering realities, knowing things as they are, and perceiving the secrets that the apparent wording of this creed expresses, there is no key to it except through the one who struggles (mujahid), and suppressing desires, turning entirely to Allah Almighty, and constant pure thought devoid of the impurities of disputations. And it is a mercy from Allah Almighty that flows upon those who expose themselves to its gusts according to the measure of provision, and according to the exposure, and according to the acceptance of the place and the purity of the heart. And that is a sea whose depth is not reached nor its shore attained.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a description of the method to attain true understanding of the creed beyond its outward expressions. Ghazali states that removing doubts (shubuhat), uncovering realities (haqa'iq), knowing things as they are, and perceiving the secrets (asrar) expressed by the apparent words of the creed have no key except through four things: mujahada (spiritual struggle), suppressing desires, complete turning to Allah, and constant pure thought free from the impurities of disputations (mujadalat). He then explains that this knowledge is a mercy from Allah that flows upon those who expose themselves to its breezes, in proportion to their provision (rizq), their exposure, and the acceptance of the vessel (the heart) and its purity. He concludes by describing this realm of knowledge as an unfathomable sea. The emphasis is on inner purification and divine grace rather than intellectual debate.

Key Terms
مجاهدة (mujahada)
شبهة (shubha)
فكر صاف (safi thought)
رحمة (rahma)
نفحات (nafahat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That deeper religious knowledge can be obtained through argumentation alone.
Correction: Ghazali insists it requires spiritual struggle, purity, and is ultimately a divine gift.

Checkpoint
What is the key to removing doubts and uncovering the secrets of the creed?
A) Extensive reading and debate
B) Spiritual struggle, suppressing desires, and pure thought
C) Following the majority opinion
D) Memorizing all theological arguments

Key Takeaway
The deeper secrets of faith are unlocked not by argument but by spiritual struggle, pure thought, and divine mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your pursuit of understanding your faith, have you placed more trust in your own arguments and debates than in spiritual struggle and turning entirely to Allah? What would it take for you to shift that balance starting today?
?
When you read the words of the creed, do you feel the weight of their secrets, or are you satisfied with mere intellectual assent? What is one desire you can begin to suppress this week to open your heart to deeper realities?
?
Ghazali says this knowledge is like a boundless sea. Are you content to remain on the shore, or are you willing to dive in through struggle and pure thought? What is the first step you will take to enter that sea?

The Outward and Inward Distinction Is Not Against Sharia
Ghazali refutes the objection that distinguishing apparent and hidden aspects contradicts the Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali affirms the division of sciences into outward and inward dimensions and why he considers denial of this division a sign of intellectual stagnation.

Core Idea
The Sharia itself supports the existence of both outward and inward dimensions of knowledge.

In this passage, Ghazali anticipates a potential objection to his earlier remarks: that suggesting sciences have hidden and evident aspects seems to contradict the unity of Sharia. He responds by clarifying that this division is not only permissible but is recognized by insightful scholars and supported by scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the imagined objection carefully. It states that Sharia has no distinction between outward and inward, and that Ghazali's position nearly contradicts Sharia.
Notice that the objector equates 'Sharia' with a single, uniform reality.
2
Analyze Ghazali's response
Read Ghazali's answer. He asserts that the division into hidden and evident is not denied by anyone with true insight; only the 'qasirun' (deficient ones) deny it.
Identify the description of the deniers: they picked up something early and froze, never ascending.
3
Connect to Sharia proofs
Ghazali claims this division is clear from the proofs of Sharia. Reflect on what Sharia evidence might support an inner dimension of knowledge (e.g., hadith about ihsan, inner sincerity).
This step requires prior familiarity with Islamic sources.
4
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: Why does Ghazali consider this objection a sign of deficiency? What does this teach about the nature of Islamic knowledge?
The answer lies in the objector's inability to move beyond surface-level understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الحقيقة والشريعة

فإن قلت: هذا الكلام يشير إلى أن هذه العلوم لها ظواهر وأسرار، وبعضها جلي يبدو أولاً، وبعضها خفي يتضح بالمجاهدة والرياضة والطلب الحثيث والفكر الصافي والسر الخالي عن كل شيء من أشغال الدنيا سوى المطلوب، وهذا يكاد يكون مخالفا للشرع، إذ ليس للشرع ظاهر وباطن وسر وعلن، بل الظاهر والباطن والسر والعلن واحد فيه.

فاعلم أن إنقسام هذه العلوم إلى خفية وجلية لا ينكرها ذو بصيرة، وإنما ينكرها القاصرون، الذين تلقفوا في أوائل الصبا شيئا وجمدوا عليه، فلم يكن لهم ترق إلى شأو العلا، ومقامات العلماء والأولياء، وذلك ظاهر من أدلة الشرع.
Issue: Reality and Law.

If you say: This speech indicates that these sciences have outward aspects and secrets, some of which are evident and appear first, and some are hidden and become clear through mujahada, discipline, earnest seeking, pure thought, and a secret free from all worldly occupations except the sought. And this nearly contradicts the Sharia, for the Sharia does not have an outward and inward, secret and public; rather the outward, inward, secret, and public are one in it.

Know that the division of these sciences into hidden and evident is not denied by anyone possessing insight; rather only the deficient deny it, those who picked up something in early childhood and froze upon it, so they had no ascent to the high station, and the stations of the scholars and saints. And that is clear from the proofs of the Sharia.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa jawab) regarding the apparent/hidden distinction. The objector argues that Ghazali's earlier statements imply sciences have outward and inward dimensions, which seems contrary to Sharia because Sharia is all one. Ghazali responds by affirming the division into hidden and evident (khafiyya wa jaliyya). He states that this division is not denied by anyone of insight; only the deficient ('qasirun') deny it. These are people who learned something in early childhood and froze upon it, never ascending to higher stations of scholars and saints. He asserts that this division is clear from the proofs of the Sharia. The answer is that the objector's denial stems from intellectual limitation, not from the nature of Sharia.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqa)
شريعة (shari'a)
ظاهر (zahir)
باطن (batin)
سر (sirr)
علن (alan)
قاصرون (qasirun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that recognizing inner and outer dimensions of knowledge contradicts the Sharia's unity.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that the Sharia itself provides proof for such a distinction, and denying it is a sign of intellectual deficiency, not piety.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say that some people deny the division of sciences into outward and inward?
A) They have deeper knowledge of Sharia
B) They froze on what they learned in early childhood and cannot ascend
C) The division is truly against Sharia
D) They are scholars with insight

Key Takeaway
The Sharia embraces both outward and inward dimensions of knowledge, and denying this inner depth reflects a limitation in the seeker, not a flaw in the tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed an inner dimension of Islam—like spirituality or divine secrets—as irrelevant or even against the Sharia? Who taught you that, and have you examined the Sharia proofs for yourself, or are you relying on a frozen childhood understanding?
?
Are you one of those who 'froze upon what they picked up in early childhood,' never pushing beyond familiar doctrines? What specific step can you take today to break that pattern—perhaps reading a deeper text, seeking a teacher, or beginning a practice of mujahada?
?
This passage says the division between evident and hidden sciences is clear from Sharia proofs. Do you actively seek the hidden via mujahada and riyada, or have you settled for only the outward? What commitment will you make to purify your heart for deeper knowledge, starting this week?

The Outward and Inward in Religious Knowledge: Ghazali's Response
Ghazali responds to an objection about hidden meanings in religious sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that religious knowledge has both outward and inward dimensions, supported by primary texts, and how he refutes the objection that this contradicts the Shari'a.

Core Idea
Islamic knowledge has layers—outer and inner—and the Shari'a itself affirms this.

An objector claims that the idea of hidden meanings in religious sciences contradicts the Shari'a, which is one-dimensional. Ghazali responds by asserting that this division is obvious to the insightful and is actually supported by prophetic and companion statements. In this lesson, you will see how Ghazali uses key texts to defend the depth of religious knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's claim that religious knowledge has no inner/outer distinction and that such a distinction contradicts the Shari'a.
Notice how the objector assumes Shari'a is purely external.
2
Examine Ghazali's response
Notice how Ghazali dismisses the objection as the view of the intellectually limited and cites evidence from the Shari'a itself.
3
Study the proofs
Identify the four proofs Ghazali provides: the Qur'an's outer/inner, Ali's statement, and two hadith about speaking according to intellect.
Write down each proof and its source.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this lesson invites you to see religious knowledge as layered and not merely literal, and how it validates the pursuit of deeper understanding.
This connects to the broader theme of Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الحقيقة والشريعة

فإن قلت : هذا الكلام يشير إلى أن هذه العلوم لها ظواهر وأسرار، وبعضها جلي يبدو أولاً، وبعضها خفي يتضح بالمجاهدة والرياضة والطلب الحثيث والفكر الصافي والسر الخالي عن كل شيء من أشغال الدنيا سوى المطلوب، وهذا يكاد يكون مخالفا للشرع، إذ ليس للشرع ظاهر وباطن وسر وعلن، بل الظاهر والباطن والسر والعلن واحد فيه

فاعلم أن انقسام هذه العلوم إلى خفية وجلية لا ينكرها ذو بصيرة، وإنما ينكرها القاصرون، الذين تلقفوا في أوائل الصبا شيئا وجمدوا عليه، فلم يكن لهم ترق إلى شأو العلا، ومقامات العلماء والأولياء، وذلك ظاهر من أدلة الشرع.

قال : (إن للقرآن ظاهراً وباطناً وحداً ومطلعاً)

وقال علي رضي الله عنه وأشار إلى صدره : إن ها هنا علوماً جمة لو وجدت لها حملة

وقال صلى الله عليه وسلم نحن معاشر الأنبياء أمرنا أن نكلم الناس على قدر عقولهم

وقال صلى الله عليه وسلم «ما حدث أحد قوما بحديث لم تبلغه عقولهم إلا كان فتنة»
Issue: al-Haqiqa (Reality) and al-Shari'a (Law).

If you say: This speech indicates that these sciences have outward aspects and secrets, some of which are clear appearing first, and some of which are hidden becoming evident through striving, spiritual discipline, earnest seeking, pure thought, and a heart empty of all worldly preoccupations except the sought object. This nearly contradicts the Shari'a, for the Shari'a has no outward and inward, secret and public; rather the outward and inward, secret and public are one in it.

Know then that the division of these sciences into hidden and clear is denied only by those of limited sight, who in early youth picked up something and froze upon it, so they had no ascent to the high summit and the stations of the scholars and saints. That is evident from the proofs of the Shari'a.

He said: 'Indeed the Qur'an has an outward, an inward, a limit and an ascent.'

And 'Ali, may God be pleased with him, said, pointing to his chest: 'Indeed here there is abundant knowledge, if only I could find bearers for it.'

And the Prophet ﷺ said: 'We, the company of prophets, are commanded to speak to people according to the capacity of their intellects.'

And the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever tells people something their minds cannot grasp will only cause trial.'
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that the previous discussion about outer and inner dimensions of knowledge contradicts the Shari'a, because the Shari'a has no such division—everything is one. Ghazali responds by asserting that the division into hidden and clear is acknowledged by the insightful and denied only by the intellectually limited, who learned a little in youth and became rigid. He then cites three proofs: (1) a statement (attributed to the Prophet) that the Qur'an has an outward, an inward, a limit, and an ascent; (2) the saying of 'Ali, pointing to his chest, about vast knowledge needing bearers; (3) the hadith that prophets speak to people according to their intellects; and (4) the hadith that speaking beyond people's understanding causes trial. These show that religious knowledge indeed has levels and hidden dimensions, and that the Shari'a itself supports this distinction.

جلي
خفي
حد
مطلع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Islamic knowledge has no hidden or inner dimension—that everything is purely literal and external.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the Shari'a itself, through Quranic, prophetic, and companion statements, affirms that religious knowledge has both outward and inward dimensions.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, who denies the division of religious sciences into hidden and clear?
A) The insightful scholars
B) The limited ones who learned a little in youth and became rigid
C) The companions of the Prophet
D) The saints and spiritual masters

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the outward and inward dimensions of religious knowledge are affirmed by the Qur'an, the Prophet, and the Companions, and denying them reflects intellectual rigidity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever become comfortable with a single understanding of an Islamic teaching, assuming there is nothing deeper? What might you be missing, and what will you do today to seek the inward dimension?
?
The Prophet spoke to people according to their intellects. In your conversations about religion, do you adjust your words to the listener's capacity, or do you speak to impress? How will you change your approach this week?
?
Ali pointed to his chest and said there is abundant knowledge but few bearers. What kind of bearer are you—are you carrying only what you were given in childhood, or are you actively seeking deeper knowledge? Write one specific step you will take this week to become a bearer of hidden knowledge.

The Quran's Outward and Inward Dimensions
Hadith on four dimensions of the Quran and Ali's saying on hidden sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran possesses both outward and inward dimensions, as well as a limit and an ascent in meaning.

Core Idea
The Quran is not flat—it has layers from the outward to the innermost.

This lesson begins with a foundational hadith that describes the Quran as having four dimensions. It also includes the testimony of Ali that there is abundant, hidden knowledge. These traditions establish that sacred knowledge is not all on the surface.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and Ali's statement
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the four terms: ẓāhir, bāṭin, ḥadd, muṭṭalaʿ.
Think about what each term might refer to.
2
Reflect on the four terms
Consider each term as a layer of Quranic meaning. The ẓāhir is the literal text; the bāṭin is the hidden sense; the ḥadd is a limit of understanding; the muṭṭalaʿ is an ascent to higher comprehension.
3
Consider the implication for knowledge
Ali's testimony shows that such inner knowledge exists but requires worthy bearers. This sets up the need for cautious teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال صلى الله عليه وسلم : ( إن للقرآن ظاهراً وباطناً وحداً ومطلعاً ) وقال علي رضي الله عنه وأشار إلى صدره : إن ها هنا علوماً جمة لو وجدت لها حملة . وقال صلى الله عليه وسلم : « نحن معاشر الأنبياء أمرنا أن نكلم الناس على قدر عقولهم » . وقال صلى الله عليه وسلم : « ما حدث أحد قوما بحديث لم تبلغه عقولهم إلا كان فتنة عليهم » . وقال الله تعالى : ( وتلك الأمثال نضربها للناس وما يعقلها إلا العالمون ) . وقال : « إن من العلم كهيئة المكنون لا يعلمه إلا العالمون بالله تعالى » . وقال : « لو تعلمون ما أعلم لضحكتم قليلا ولبكيتم كثيراً » . والمراد من قوله « ما أعلم » من انتقام الله من أهل الجرائم وأهوال القيامة . وقوله : « لضحكتم قليلا » أي كان ضحككم على القلة ؛ وقيل معناه : لما ضحكتم أصلا وهذا هو المناسب للسياق ، لأن لو حرف امتناع لامتناع . و« لبكيتم كثيرا » قدم الضحك لكونه من المسرة، وفيه من أنواع البديع مقابلة الضحك بالبكاء ، والقلة بالكثرة .
He said, peace be upon him: 'Indeed for the Quran there is an outward, an inward, a limit, and an ascent.' And Ali, may God be pleased with him, said, pointing to his chest: 'Indeed here are abundant sciences, if only they found bearers.' And he said, peace be upon him: 'We, the company of prophets, have been commanded to speak to people according to the level of their intellects.' And he said, peace be upon him: 'No one speaks to a people with a hadith that their intellects do not reach except that it becomes a trial for them.' And God Almighty said: 'And such examples We strike for the people, but none understands them except the knowers.' And he said: 'Indeed there is knowledge like a hidden treasure, none knows it except the knowers of God Almighty.' And he said: 'If you knew what I know, you would laugh little and weep much.' And the meaning of his statement 'what I know' is of God's vengeance upon the criminals and the terrors of the Day of Resurrection. And his statement 'you would laugh little' means that your laughter would be scant; and it is said its meaning is: you would not laugh at all, and this is the appropriate meaning for the context, because 'law' (if) is a particle of prevention for prevention. And 'you would weep much' - he mentioned laughter first because it is from pleasure, and in it is a type of rhetorical device: contrasting laughter with weeping, and scantiness with abundance.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage assembles multiple proofs from revelation (Quran and Sunnah) to establish two intertwined principles: first, that the Quran and sacred knowledge have an inward dimension beyond the outward; second, that this inward knowledge must be imparted only to those capable of receiving it. The passage is a cumulative proof, beginning with the famous hadith describing the Quran as having an outward (zahir), inward (batin), limit (hadd), and ascent (muttala'). This is followed by Ali's testimony that his own chest contains abundant sciences, implying hidden knowledge. Then two hadiths establish the pedagogical principle: prophets speak to people according to their intellects, and speaking beyond a people's capacity causes fitna (trial). A Quranic verse (29:43) reinforces that only the 'alimun (knowers) understand divine parables. The next hadith describes knowledge as a hidden treasure (kahay'at al-maknun) known only to the knowers of God. Finally, the hadith 'if you knew what I know' warns of terrible knowledge that would eliminate laughter and increase weeping. Ghazali explains the meaning of 'what I know' as God's vengeance and resurrection terrors, and clarifies the rhetorical contrast in the hadith. The overall lesson is that the inward aspects of the faith require both recognition and cautious dissemination, a foundational principle for kalam methodology.

Key Terms
ظاهر
باطن
حد
مطلع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is only a literal text with no deeper meanings.
Correction: The hadith explicitly states that the Quran has an inward (bāṭin) and an ascent (muṭṭalaʿ), affirming deeper layers of meaning beyond the literal.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith, how many dimensions does the Quran have?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The Quran has layered meanings: an outward, an inward, a limit, and an ascent, and this inner knowledge requires worthy bearers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you stop at the surface, or do you long to ascend beyond the limit of your current understanding? What is one step you can take today to seek the inward of the Quran?
?
Ali (RA) lamented the lack of bearers for the abundant sciences. Are you a bearer? If not, what is holding you back from becoming one?
?
The hadith speaks of a 'limit' (ḥadd) and an 'ascent' (muṭṭalaʿ). Have you ever felt a limit in your understanding of the Quran? How did you respond—did you accept it, or did you seek to ascend?

Teaching According to Capacity
Hadiths on teaching according to capacity and verse on understanding examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prophetic principle that knowledge must be taught according to the listener's intellectual capacity, and that exceeding that capacity causes spiritual harm.

Core Idea
Teach to the level of the listener — or risk fitna.

This lesson presents two prophetic hadiths and a Quranic verse that together establish a core pedagogical rule: prophets and scholars must speak to people at their level of understanding, and those who cannot comprehend should not be burdened with knowledge beyond them.

Guided Path
1
Read the two hadiths and the verse
Read each text carefully. Notice the command in the first hadith and the warning in the second.
2
Identify the principle
The principle is: speak to people according to their intellects. The second hadith warns that violating this causes fitna.
The Quranic verse reinforces that only the 'ālimūn understand deeper parables.
3
Consider its importance for theology
This principle protects both the teacher and the student. It explains why not all knowledge is publicly taught.

Source Text

Knowledge as a Hidden Treasure
Hadiths on hidden knowledge and the weight of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that there is hidden knowledge known only to the true knowers of God, and that some divine knowledge would be overwhelming for ordinary people.

Core Idea
Some knowledge is a hidden treasure, not meant for everyone.

This lesson explores two hadiths that point to the existence of deep, esoteric knowledge. The first describes knowledge as a hidden treasure; the second warns that if people knew the full reality of divine wrath and the terrors of the Day of Resurrection, their lives would change entirely. Ghazali explains the rhetorical brilliance of the second hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the two hadiths and the explanation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the phrase 'kahay'at al-maknun' and the warning hadith.
2
Understand Ghazali's interpretation
Ghazali explains that 'what I know' refers to God's vengeance and resurrection terrors. He also clarifies the rhetorical contrast.
Note the use of 'law' implying impossibility.
3
Appreciate the wisdom of selective disclosure
These hadiths show that some knowledge is hidden and would be too heavy for most people to bear.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you laugh at things that displease Allah or feel secure from His vengeance, are you not living as if you do not know what the Prophet knew? Does your laughter reveal ignorance of the terrors of the Day of Resurrection?

The Secret of Knowledge: Withholding Due to Limited Understanding
Ghazali defends withholding some knowledge due to limited understanding, citing hadith and scholarly opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's defense of the principle that the Prophet conveyed all revelation, yet some deeper knowledge was kept from the general public due to their limited capacity.

Core Idea
The Prophet hid nothing, but some truths must be reserved for those who can bear them.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali addresses a delicate theological question: did the Prophet conceal any part of the revelation? He emphatically denies this, citing the principle that the Prophet was a mercy and conveyed everything. Yet, he argues that some deeper meanings were intentionally withheld from those unable to understand them, a practice supported by the companions and early scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two principles
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay special attention to the section beginning with 'وينكشف ذلك بتسليم أصلين' (And that is revealed by accepting two principles). Identify what these principles are.
The two principles are the core of Ghazali's argument: (1) the Prophet conveyed all revelation, (2) the companions are the most knowledgeable about its meanings.
2
Understand the role of the companions
Notice how Ghazali uses the examples of Abu Huraira and Abu Bakr to show that even among the companions, some knowledge was kept private. Reflect on why this does not contradict the first principle.
The secret knowledge is still part of the religion's foundations, but not suited for public dissemination.
3
Reflect on the classifications of knowledge
Consider Sahl al-Tustari's division of knowledge into outward, inward, and secret. How does this classification support Ghazali's overall argument? Also, ponder the warnings about disclosing the secret of lordship.
This step helps you see the practical implications of the theory.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فليت شعري إن لم يكن ذلك سرا منع من إفشائه لقصور الأفهام عن إدراكه، أو لمعنى آخر، فلم لم يذكره لهم، ولا شك أنهم كانوا يصدقونه لو ذكره لهم؟ وقال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما في قوله عز وجل: اللهُ الَّذِي خَلَقَ سَبْعَ سَمَوَاتٍ وَمِنَ الأَرْضِ مِثْلَهُنَّ يَتَنَزَّلُ الأمْرُ بَيْنَهُنَّ لو ذكرت تفسيره لرجمتموني؛ وفي لفظ آخر: لقلتم إنه كافر. ورواه أيضاً من طريقه ابن عساكر في التاريخ بتلك الزيادة وأخرج الحاكم أيضاً في كتاب الرقاق؛ والبيهقي في الشعب عن أبي الدرداء رفعه: «لو تعلمون ما أعلم لبكيتم كثيرا ولضحكتم قليلا، ولخرجتم الى الصعدات تجأرون لا تدرون تنجون أو لا تنجون»؛ وقال الحاكم صحيح، وأقره الذهبي. وقال الهيثمي رواه الطبراني من طريق ابنة ابي الدرداء عن أبيها ولم أعرفها وبقية رجاله رجال الصحيح. وأخرج الحاكم أيضاً في الأهوال عن أبي هريرة رفعه: لو تعلمون ما أعلم لبكيتم كثيرا ولضحكتم قليلا، يظهر النفاق وترفع الأمانة، وتقبض الرحمة، الأمين، ويؤتمن غير الأمين، أناخ بكم الشر الجور، الفتن كأمثال الليل المظلم، وقال ويتهم وأقره الذهبي صحيح. (۱) وينكشف ذلك بتسليم أصلين: الأول: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أفاض إلى الخلق ما أوحى الله إليه، وأنه ما كتم شيئا من الوحي، فلذلك كان رحمة للعالمين، فما ترك شيئا مما يقربهم الى رضا الله تعالى إلا ولهم عليه، وأمرهم به، ولا مما يسخط الله إلا حذرهم ونهاهم عنه في العلم والعمل جميعا. الثاني: أن أعرف الناس بمعاني كلامه وأحراهم بالوقوف على كنه درك أسراره الذين شاهدوا الوحي والتنزيل وصحبوه ولازموه متشمرين لتلقي ما يقوله بالقبول للعمل به أولا، والنقل إلى من بعدهم ثانياً، والتقرب إلى الله بسماعه وحفظه ونشره وهم الذين حضهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم على السماء والفهم والحفظ والأداء فقال: «نضر الله أمر أسمع مقالتي فوعاها وأداها كما سمعها الحديث . . .» (۲) الآية: (12) من سورة الطلاق (65). وقال ابو هريرة رضي الله عنه حفظت من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وعاءين أما أحدهما فبثثته وأما الآخر لو بثثته لقطع هذا الحلقوم. وقال صلى الله عليه وسلم: «ما فضلكم أبو بكر بكثرة صيام ولا صلاة ولكن بسر وقر في صدره» رضي الله عنه. ولا شك في أن ذلك السر كان متعلقا بقواعد الدين غير خارج منها، وما كان من قواعد الدين لم يكن خافيا بظواهره على غيره. وقال سهل التستري رضي الله عنه: للعالم ثلاثة علوم: علم ظاهر يبذله لأهل الظاهر، وعلم باطن لا يسعه أظهاره إلا لأهله وعلم هو بينه وبين الله تعالى لا يظهره لأحد. وقال بعض العارفين: إفشاء سر الربوبية كفر. وقال بعضهم: للربوبية سر لو أظهر لبطلت النبوة، وللنبوة سر لو كشف لبطل العلم. (۱) حديث ما فضلكم أبو بكر أخرجه الترمذي الحكيم في النوادر من قول ابي بكر بن عبد الله المزني وذكر العراقي معقبا عليه فقال: لم أجده مرفوعا. (۲) كلمة السر غير موجودة في الأصل. (۳) هكذا أورده صاحب القوت، عن سهل إلا أنه قال: وعلم هو سر بين الله وبين العالم هو حقيقة إيمانه لا يظهره لأهل الظاهر ولا لأهل الباطن. (٤) ذكر هذا صاحب قوت القلوب في الباب الثالث والثلاثين في آخر أخبار الصفات ونصبه: وحقيقة علم التوحيد باطن المعرفة وهو سبق المعروف إلى من به تعرف بصنعة مخصوصة بحبيب مغرب مخصوص ولا يسع معرفة ذلك الكافة وإنشاء سر الربوبيه كفر. وقال بعض العارفين: من صرح بالتوحيد، وأفشى الوحدانية فقتله أفضل من إحياء غيره. اهـ
Would that I knew, if that was not a secret withheld from disclosure due to the failure of understandings to grasp it, or for another reason, why did he not mention it to them? And there is no doubt that they would have believed him if he had mentioned it to them. And Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, said concerning the saying of Allah Almighty: 'Allah is He who created seven heavens and from the earth the like thereof; the command descends between them' (65:12): 'If I mentioned its interpretation you would stone me'; and in another wording: 'You would say he is a disbeliever.' And it was also narrated by Ibn Asakir in history with that addition, and al-Hakim also narrated in Kitab al-Riqaq, and al-Bayhaqi in al-Shu'ab, from Abu al-Darda, raised to the Prophet: 'If you knew what I know, you would weep much and laugh little, and you would go out to the highlands crying out, not knowing whether you will be saved or not.' Al-Hakim said it is authentic, and al-Dhahabi agreed. And al-Haythami said: al-Tabarani narrated it from the daughter of Abu al-Darda from her father, and I do not know her, and the rest of its narrators are narrators of the Sahih. And al-Hakim also narrated in al-Ahwal from Abu Huraira, raised: 'If you knew what I know, you would weep much and laugh little; hypocrisy would appear, trust would be lifted, mercy would be withdrawn, the trustworthy would be accused and the untrustworthy would be trusted; evil and injustice would alight upon you; tribulations like pieces of dark night.' And he said, 'and he accused', and al-Dhahabi affirmed it as authentic. (1) And that is revealed by accepting two principles: the first: that the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, conveyed to creation what Allah revealed to him, and that he concealed nothing of revelation; therefore he was a mercy to the worlds; he left nothing that brings them near to the pleasure of Allah Almighty except that he guided them to it and commanded them with it, and nothing that angers Allah except that he warned them and forbade them from it, in knowledge and action together. The second: that the people most knowledgeable of the meanings of his speech and most worthy of attaining the depth of understanding of his secrets are those who witnessed revelation and descent, accompanied him, and adhered to him, striving to receive what he said with acceptance for action first, and transmission to those after them second, and drawing near to Allah by hearing, memorizing, and spreading it; and they are those whom the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, encouraged to hear, understand, memorize, and convey, saying: 'May Allah brighten a man who hears my speech, comprehends it, and conveys it as he heard it...' (2) The verse: (12) of Surah al-Talaq (65). And Abu Huraira, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'I memorized from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, two vessels: as for one, I have spread it; as for the other, if I spread it, this throat would be cut.' And he, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Abu Bakr has not excelled you by much fasting or prayer, but by a secret that settled in his chest,' may Allah be pleased with him. And there is no doubt that that secret was related to the foundations of the religion, not outside them, and what was from the foundations of the religion was not hidden in its outward aspects from others. And Sahl al-Tustari, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'For the scholar there are three sciences: an outward science which he gives to the people of outwardness, an inward science which it is not possible for him to reveal except to its people, and a science which is between him and Allah Almighty, which he does not reveal to anyone.' And some of the gnostics said: 'Disclosing the secret of lordship is disbelief.' And some of them said: 'For lordship there is a secret; if it were manifested, prophethood would be invalidated; and for prophethood there is a secret; if it were uncovered, knowledge would be invalidated.' (1) The hadith 'What has made Abu Bakr excel you...' was narrated by al-Tirmidhi al-Hakim in al-Nawadir from the saying of Abu Bakr ibn Abdullah al-Muzani, and al-Iraqi commented on it saying: I did not find it raised (to the Prophet). (2) The word 'secret' is not present in the original. (3) Thus the author of al-Qut narrated it from Sahl, except that he said: 'And a science which is a secret between Allah and the world is the reality of his faith; he does not reveal it to the people of outwardness nor to the people of inwardness.' (4) The author of Qut al-Qulub mentioned this in chapter thirty-three at the end of the reports on attributes, and its text: 'And the reality of the science of tawhid is the inner knowledge, and it is the precedence of the known to the one by whom you know, with a specific craft by a specific beloved stranger; and it is not possible for the generality to know that, and disclosing the secret of lordship is disbelief.' And some of the gnostics said: 'Whoever openly declares tawhid and discloses the unity, killing him is better than reviving others.' End.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a combination of argumentation and citation, defending the idea that some religious knowledge was intentionally kept secret due to the limited understanding of the general public. The author begins with a rhetorical question: if the matter was not a secret, why did the Prophet not mention it? Then he cites Ibn Abbas's warning about interpreting a certain verse, followed by hadiths about the Prophet's knowledge causing weeping and fear. The core of the passage lies in the two principles given in footnote (1): first, that the Prophet conveyed all revelation and hid nothing, so any secrecy is not due to omission but due to the audience's incapacity; second, that the companions (who witnessed revelation) are the most qualified to understand the deeper meanings. Then Ghazali quotes Abu Huraira's statement about two vessels of knowledge—one public, one dangerous to disclose—and the hadith about Abu Bakr's secret, affirming that the secret pertains to the very foundations of religion. Sahl al-Tustari's classification of three sciences (outward, inward, and secret between the scholar and God) further reinforces the idea that some knowledge is beyond public dissemination. Finally, strong warnings are given: disclosing the secret of lordship is kufr (disbelief), and revealing certain secrets would invalidate prophethood or knowledge itself. The footnotes provide textual criticism and alternate wordings. The overall message is that while the Prophet fully delivered the message, certain truths are reserved for those who can bear them.

Key Terms

قصور الأفهام
قواعد الدين
علم ظاهر
علم باطن
إفشاء سر الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet hid part of the revelation from the believers.
Correction: The Prophet conveyed all revelation without concealment, as he was a mercy to the worlds. However, some deeper truths were not disclosed to those who could not bear them, which is not a form of hiding revelation but a matter of appropriate dissemination.

Checkpoint
According to the two principles presented in the passage, why is it not considered that the Prophet hid part of the revelation?
A) Because he was forced to reveal everything by the angels.
B) Because he was a mercy to the worlds and left nothing that brings near to Allah or keeps away from His displeasure.
C) Because the companions demanded that he reveal everything.
D) Because he was afraid of being accused of concealment.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet fully delivered the message, but deeper truths are reserved for those who can bear them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you dismissed a challenging truth about yourself or your faith because it was too uncomfortable, telling yourself it was 'secret knowledge' not meant for you? Will you now seek it out with humility?
?
Whom have you withheld a difficult truth from, claiming they were not ready, when in reality you feared their reaction or your own responsibility? What would change if you trusted in Allah's guidance and spoke with compassion?
?
The secret of lordship is said to be so great that disclosing it is disbelief. What aspect of Allah's majesty are you still reluctant to fully submit to? What would it cost you to surrender that area completely?

The Secrets of Lordship and Knowledge
Ghazali presents reports and commentary on hidden knowledge and its limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali reconciles the existence of hidden spiritual secrets with the outward foundations of religion.

Core Idea
Inner knowledge complements outward religion; revealing secrets to the unfit harms both.

This passage from Ghazali's Qawaid al-Aqa'id brings together reports from early Muslims about deeper layers of knowledge that were not publicly disclosed. Some warnings even suggest that revealing these secrets could nullify prophethood or rulings. But Ghazali immediately clarifies that these statements are conditional and do not imply any contradiction with the outward religion. In this lesson, you will see how he carefully distinguishes between different audiences and circumstances, preserving the integrity of both the inner and outer dimensions of Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the reports about hidden knowledge
Start with the hadith of Abu Huraira and the report about Abu Bakr's secret. Notice that the secret is related to the foundations of religion.
The secret is not outside qawa'id al-din.
2
Identify the three kinds of knowledge
Read Sahl al-Tustari's classification: outward, inner, and private. Understand that each level has its proper audience.
3
Analyze the warnings about disclosing secrets
The passage mentions strong statements: disclosing the secret of lordship is kufr, and a chain of nullifications. Ask yourself what these statements really mean.
Ghazali warns against taking them literally without context.
4
Understand the resolution
Read how Ghazali explains the conditional nullification: for the weak-minded, or due to disobedience. This preserves harmony between inner and outer.
The perfect person balances knowledge and piety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابو هريرة رضي الله عنه حفظت من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وعاءين أما أحدهما فبثثته وأما الآخر لو بثثته لقطع هذا الحلقوم
وقال صلى الله عليه وسلم : « ما فضلكم أبو بكر بكثرة صيام ولا صلاة ولكن بسر وقر في صدره » رضي الله عنه
ولا شك في أن ذلك السر كان متعلقا بقواعد الدين غير خارج منها ، وما كان من قواعد الدين لم يكن خافيا بظواهره على غيره .
وقال سهل التستري رضي الله عنه : للعالم ثلاثة علوم : علم ظاهر يبذله لأهل الظاهر ، وعلم باطن لا يسعه أظهاره إلا لأهله وعلم هو بينه وبين الله تعالى لا يظهره لأحد
وقال بعض العارفين : إفشاء سر الربوبية كفر
وقال بعضهم : للربوبية سر لو أظهر لبطلت النبوة ، وللنبوة سر لو كشف بطل العلم ، وللعلماء بالله سر لو أظهروه لبطلت الأحكام
وهذا القائل إن لم يرد بذلك بطلان النبوة في حق الضعفاء لقصور فهمهم فما ذكره ليس بحق ، بل الصحيح أنه لا تناقض فيه ، وأن الكامل من لا يطفىء نور معرفته نور ورعه وملاك الورع النبوة
وسر الالوهية الذي بمعرفته يستحق النبوة من وصل إلى الله باليقين الذي لولاه لم يكن نبيا لا يخلو إما أن يكون إنكشافه من الله تعالى مما يطلع على القلوب من الأنوار التي كانت غائبة عنها ، بأن كانت القلوب ضعيفة طرأ عليها من الدهش والإصطلام والحيرة والتيه ما يبهر العقول ويفقد الإحساس ، ويقطع عن الدنيا وما فيها وذلك لضعفه ، ومن انتهى إلى هذه الحالة فتبطل النبوة في حقه أن يعرفها ، أو يعقل ما جاء من قبلها إذ قد شغله عنها ما هو أعظم لديه منها ، وربما كان سبب موته لعجزه عن حمل ما يطرأ عليه
وإما أن يكون إنكشافه من عالم به على جهة الخبر عنه ، فتبطل النبوة في حق المخبر حيث نهى عن الافشاء فأفشي وأمر أن لا يتحدث فلم يفعل ، فخرج بهذه المعصية عن طاعة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيها ، فلهذا قيل في ذلك بطلت النبوة في حقه بإخباره
Abu Huraira, may Allah be pleased with him, said: I memorized from the Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him, two vessels: as for one of them, I disseminated it; and as for the other, if I were to disseminate it, this throat would be cut. And he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Abu Bakr has not surpassed you by much fasting or prayer, but by a secret (sirr) that settled in his chest.' There is no doubt that that secret was related to the foundations of the religion (qawa'id al-din), not outside them, and whatever was from the foundations of the religion was not hidden in its outward aspects from others. And Sahl al-Tustari, may Allah be pleased with him, said: For the scholar there are three kinds of knowledge: outward knowledge (zahir) which he gives to the people of the outward, inner knowledge (batin) which he cannot reveal except to its people, and knowledge which is between him and Allah Almighty, which he does not reveal to anyone. And some of the knowers (al-'arifin) said: Disclosing the secret of lordship (sirr al-rububiyya) is disbelief (kufr). And some of them said: For lordship there is a secret which, if manifested, would nullify prophethood; and for prophethood there is a secret which, if uncovered, would nullify knowledge; and for the scholars of Allah there is a secret which, if they manifested it, would nullify the rulings. This speaker, if he did not intend by that the nullification of prophethood with respect to the weak due to the deficiency of their understanding, then what he mentioned is not correct; rather, the correct view is that there is no contradiction in it, and that the perfect one is he who does not extinguish the light of his knowledge with the light of his piety (wara'), and the mainstay of piety is prophethood. The secret of divinity (sirr al-uluhiyya) by the knowledge of which one deserves prophethood—for whoever reaches Allah with certainty, without which he would not have been a prophet—is not free from either: it is either its disclosure from Allah Almighty by way of what He causes to appear in the hearts of lights that were absent from them, such that the hearts are weak and are overtaken by bewilderment, stupefaction, perplexity, and confusion that dazzle intellects, deprive sensation, and cut off from the world and what is in it—this due to weakness. Whoever reaches this state, prophethood becomes void for him in terms of knowing it or understanding what came before it, for he is preoccupied from it by what is greater for him than it, and perhaps it becomes a cause of his death due to his inability to bear what overtakes him. Or it is its disclosure from one who knows it by way of reporting it; then prophethood becomes void for the reporter, since he was forbidden from disclosure but disclosed, and commanded not to speak but did so. Thus he exits, through this disobedience, from obedience to the Prophet, peace be upon him, in that matter. For this reason it was said: prophethood became void for him due to his reporting.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a collection of reports and commentary establishing that there are deeper aspects of religious knowledge (batin) that should not be disclosed to those incapable of understanding, and that these do not contradict the outward law (zahir). The author begins with a hadith from Abu Huraira about two vessels of knowledge—one he disseminated, the other would have caused harm if revealed—and a hadith about Abu Bakr's special secret. He then comments that this secret is part of the foundations of religion (qawa'id al-din) and not hidden in its outward aspects. Next, he quotes Sahl al-Tustari's classification of three sciences: outward, inner, and secret between the scholar and Allah. Then come stronger warnings: 'Disclosing the secret of lordship is kufr' and a chain of secrets: the secret of lordship if revealed would nullify prophethood, the secret of prophethood would nullify knowledge, and the secret of knowledge would nullify rulings. The author then clarifies: if the intent is that these secrets would nullify prophethood for the weak-minded due to deficiency, then the statement is wrong. The correct view is that there is no contradiction. The perfect person does not let the light of his knowledge extinguish the light of his piety, and the mainstay of piety is prophethood. He then explains two ways the secret of divinity might cause nullification of prophethood: (1) if the revelation comes from Allah directly and overwhelms a weak heart, causing loss of normal function, then that person cannot exercise prophethood; (2) if the secret is disclosed by a knower who disobeys the command to conceal it, then he incurs disobedience and loses his prophetic quality. Thus the apparent warnings are not literal contradictions but conditional explanations.

Key Terms
سر الربوبية
سر النبوة
سر العلم
إفشاء السر
ظاهر
باطن
قواعد الدين
الورع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that inner spiritual knowledge (batin) contradicts the outward law (shari'a) and that revealing secrets always leads to heresy.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that such secrets are within the foundations of religion and do not contradict outward aspects; warnings apply only to those unfit to hear them or to those who disobey commands of concealment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct understanding of the statement 'disclosing the secret of lordship is kufr'?
A) It means any discussion of divine attributes is forbidden for everyone.
B) It refers only to revealing such secrets to those incapable of understanding them, not an absolute prohibition.
C) It means the inner meaning of religion contradicts the outward law.
D) It applies only to scholars, not to laypeople.

Key Takeaway
Hidden spiritual secrets are part of the religion's foundations; they must be disclosed only to those who can understand, and they do not contradict the outward law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe there is knowledge that you are not ready to receive, or does your pride tell you that you are capable of anything? Take a moment to ask Allah to protect you from seeking what He has not permitted for you.
?
Have you ever been entrusted with a secret about someone that you knew would harm them if revealed? How does that compare to the spiritual secrets Ghazali warns about? Will you commit to guarding the boundaries of sacred knowledge in your own life?
?
When you learn something profound about Allah, do you rush to share it with others to appear knowledgeable, or do you humbly weigh the capacity of your listener? Ask yourself: am I seeking knowledge to elevate myself or to draw closer to my Lord?
